Co-reference of Categories to Divine Attributes: Real vs. Conceptual
Defends conceptual co-reference of categories to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real (haqiqi) and conceptual (i'tibari) categories and explain how the Hanafi position on co-reference avoids attributing real multiplicity to God.

Core Idea
Categories apply to God only conceptually (i'tibari), avoiding real multiplicity or change in His essence.

In this passage, the commentator defends the Hanafi usul view that Aristotelian categories (maqulat) can be predicated of God's attributes without implying composition or change. The key is the distinction between real categories—which describe external essences—and conceptual categories that the intellect constructs. By restricting the application to conceptual categories, the Hanafi school safeguards tawhid while allowing meaningful discourse about divine attributes. The passage also delves into two nuanced investigations: whether an act can be described with obligation before its existence, and the conceptual relationship between wujub (obligation) and ijab (imposition).

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing claim
Note Ibn Sina's position: categories are essentially distinct (mutabayina bi al-dhat) and cannot co-refer to any single entity. This is the philosophical objection.
Ibn Sina's view applies to real categories only.
2
State the Hanafi response
The Hanafi author argues that Ibn Sina's objection does not apply to conceptual categories (i'tibariyya). Conceptual categories are mental constructs that can co-refer without implying real multiplicity.
The author even questions the force of Ibn Sina's argument, noting it lacks a proof.
3
Analyze the first investigation
The first investigation concerns whether an act can be described with obligation (wujub) before it exists. The author argues that obligation is a conceptual quality that attaches to the act's estimative existence, and to the obligee via liability arising from the eternal address.
This defends the possibility of describing a future act with obligation without attributing change to God.
4
Analyze the second investigation
The second investigation examines the difference between wujub and ijab. Although the concepts differ (one describes the act, the other the command), their referent (musadaq) is the same—the single divine command 'do it'. Thus unity of essence with conceptual difference.
This reinforces the conceptual approach, avoiding real plurality in God.
5
Derive the usul implication
The conceptual approach allows us to speak of divine attributes (e.g., will, command) in categorical terms without anthropomorphism. In usul al-fiqh, this underpins how obligation (wujub) is understood as a conceptual attribute of an act, not a real quality.
This ties directly to the Hanafi position that wujub is i'tibari.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلام الشريف (أقول) إنه قدس سره لم يرد المقولة الحقيقية كيف والفعل المقولة عبارة عن هيئة غير قارة حاصلة من التأثير ولا يصدق على صفات الباري بل أراد الاعتبارية والحاصل أن تصادق المقولات الحقيقية التي هي أجناس عالية لم يلزم وتصادق) المقولات (الاعتبارية) التي يعتبرها العقل وإن لم تكن أجناساً باعتبارات مختلفة ليس بممتنع) فلا بأس بأن يصدق عليه باعتبار انتسابه إلى الحاكم الفاعل فعل أي هيئة تأثيرية وباعتبار نسبته إلى الفعل المفعول انفعال أي هيئة تأثيرية فلا يرد ما قيل في حواشي ميرزاجان أن الشيخ) شيخ الفلاسفة أبا علي ابن سينا (في الشفاء صرح بأن المقولات متباينة بالذات تبايناً ذاتياً (فلا يتصادقان) على شيء (ولو) باعتبار) وجه عدم الورود أن قول ابن سينا في المقولات الحقيقية لا الاعتبارية، ونحن ندعي صدق الاعتبارية، فأين هذا من ذاك؟ واعلم أن ما ذكره المصنف تنزل بعد تسليم قول ابن سينا ولك أن تقول : أي حجة في حسبان ابن سينا فإنه ما أقام عليه دليلاً، فلنا أن لا نساعده، فمعنى كلامه قدس سره أن دعوى امتناع صدق المقولات، وإن اشتهر بين وصدر عن شيخهم محل مناقشة عندي فلا إيراد، ثم ها هنا بحثان : الأول : أنه لا يلزم من الدليل غير أن الفعل لا يتصف بصفة خارجية، ولا يلزم أن لا يتصف بصفة اعتبارية والوجوب يجوز أن يكون صفة اعتبارية وما قال في «الحاشية» أن الوجوب ليس صفة لفعل خارجي حال وجوده، بل هو صفة له حادثة قبل وجوده، والمعدوم ما دام معدوماً لا يتصف بصفة حادثة أصلاً، فحينئذ لا حظ للفعل من الوجوب إلا وجود افعل به ففيه أنه سلم تعلق افعل فكونه متعلقاً صفة حادثة فيلزم أن لا والتحقيق أن اتصاف الفعل به باعتبار وجوده التقديري وحينئذ يصح اتصافه بصفة اعتبارية أخرى، ثم إن تنزلنا نقول : سلمنا أن الفعل غير متصف بصفة ثبوتية، لكن المكلف التكليف قطعاً، ويحدث من تعلق الخطاب الأزلي به صفة فيه هي صيرورة ذمته مشغولة بأداء الفعل وهو الوجوب فافهم ؛ وأما الكلام بأن الطلب لا يتعلق بالمعدوم، وسيجيء أنه مكلف فلا بد له من ،وجود فالمكلف وإن كان معدوماً زمانياً لكنه حاضر عنده تعالى، فيتعلق به الطلب كذلك الفعل حاضر عنده تعالى موجود في زمانه، فيمكن أن يتصف بصفة ثبوتية الوجوب، فلا يفقهه هذا العبد فإن ما يجيء من تكليف المعدوم الطلب التعليقي، وهو لا يستدعي الوجود بل التعلق التعليقي يصح بالمعدوم، وأما وجود المكلف والفعل متحققين بزماني وجوديهما في الأزل عنده تعالى، فقول بالقدم الدهري والأصوليون يرونه شيئاً فرياً هذا الثاني أن تغاير الوجوب والإيجاب ضروري فإنهما متضايفان مقتضيان للموصوفين المتغايرين وإنكاره مكابرة؟ الجواب : أنا لا ننكر تغاير المفهومين، وإنما المقصود اتحاد المصداق بالذات مع المغايرة بالاعتبار، وهو معنى افعل قائماً بالفاعل متعلقاً بالمفعول، فالعقل ينتزع منه مفهومين أحدهما باعتبار قيام هذا المعنى بالفاعل فيصف به الفاعل، والآخر باعتبار تعلقه بالمفعول فيصف به المفعول المفروض عنده فمعنى افعل هو وجوب وإيجاب أي مصداقهما، وبهذا ظهر اندفاع الأول، كما لا يخفى على ذي كياسة والحق عند علام
The statement of al-Sharif: He (may his secret be sanctified) did not intend the real category, for how? The category of action is an expression of a non-stationary form resulting from effect, and it is not true of the attributes of the Creator. Rather, he intended the conceptual category. The result is that the co-reference of the real categories that are highest genera is not necessary, and the co-reference of the conceptual categories that the intellect considers, even if they are not genera, by different considerations is not impossible. So there is no harm in it being true of Him according to the aspect of His attribution to the agent—action, meaning an effective form—and according to the aspect of its attribution to the patient—passion, meaning a receptive form. Thus the objection raised in the glosses of Mirzajan that the Shaykh, the chief of philosophers, Abu Ali Ibn Sina, stated in al-Shifa that the categories are essentially distinct, so they cannot co-refer to anything, even by consideration, is not valid. The reason for the non-validity is that Ibn Sina's statement concerns the real categories, not the conceptual ones, and we claim the truth of the conceptual. So where is this from that? And know that what the author mentioned is a concession after conceding Ibn Sina's statement, but you may say: What proof is there in Ibn Sina's opinion? For he did not present a proof for it, so we need not agree with him. So the meaning of his words (may his secret be sanctified) is that the claim of impossibility of co-reference of categories, even if it is famous and issued from their shaykh, is a point of discussion in my view, so no objection. Then here are two investigations: First, it does not follow from the proof that the act is not qualified by an external quality, nor does it follow that it is not qualified by a conceptual quality, and obligation can be a conceptual quality. What was said in the gloss that obligation is not a quality of an external act at the time of its existence, but rather it is a quality arising before its existence, and the non-existent as long as it is non-existent is not qualified by any arising quality at all, so then the act has no share of obligation except the existence of 'do it' with it. The response in it is that he conceded the attachment of 'do it', so its being attached is an arising quality, so it would follow that not... And the verification is that the act's being qualified by it is by virtue of its estimative existence, and then it is correct for it to be qualified by another conceptual quality. Then if we concede, we say: We concede that the act is not qualified by a positive quality, but the obligated person is obligated definitely, and from the attachment of the eternal address to him, a quality arises in him which is the becoming of his liability occupied with performing the act, and that is obligation, so understand. As for the statement that the demand does not attach to the non-existent, and it will come that he is obligated, so he must have existence; the obligated person, even if he is temporally non-existent, is present with Him Almighty, so the demand attaches to him. Similarly, the act is present with Him Almighty, existing in its time, so it can be qualified by a positive quality of obligation. So this slave does not understand it, for what comes of the obligation of the non-existent is the conditional demand, and it does not require existence; rather, conditional attachment is valid for the non-existent. As for the existence of the obligated person and the act being realized in their temporal existences in eternity with Him Almighty, that is the statement of the temporal priority, and the usulists consider it a monstrous thing. This second investigation: The difference between obligation (wujub) and imposition (ijab) is necessary, for they are correlatives requiring two different described entities, and its denial is obstinacy. The answer: We do not deny the difference of the concepts; rather, the intent is the unity of the referent in essence with the difference by consideration, and that is the meaning of 'do it' subsisting in the agent and attached to the patient. The intellect extracts from it two concepts: one by virtue of the subsistence of this meaning in the agent, so it describes the agent with it, and the other by virtue of its attachment to the patient, so it describes the patient with it. So the meaning of 'do it' is obligation and imposition, i.e., their referent. And by this, the refutation of the first investigation becomes evident, as is not hidden from one of intelligence. And the truth is with the All-Knowing.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical and usul discussion about whether the Aristotelian categories (maqulat) can be applied to God's attributes. The author (presumably the commentator) argues that the categories are conceptual (i'tibariyya) rather than real (haqiqiyya), so applying them to God does not imply real multiplicity or composition. He first addresses an objection from Ibn Sina, who said categories are essentially distinct and cannot co-refer. The author responds that Ibn Sina meant real categories, while we only claim conceptual co-reference. Then he presents two investigations: (1) whether an act can be described with the attribute of obligation (wujub) before its existence. The author argues that obligation is a conceptual quality that attaches to the act by virtue of its estimative existence, and to the obligee by a liability arising from the eternal address. He rejects the claim that the non-existent cannot be described, noting that conditional attachments are valid. (2) The distinction between wujub (obligation) and ijab (imposition) is conceptual, not real; they are two aspects of the same command 'do it', referring to the same entity but different considerations. This is a classic Hanafi position that avoids attributing real change or multiplicity to God's attributes.

Key Terms
مقولة (maqula): A category; one of the ten Aristotelian categories that classify beings.
اعتبارية (i'tibariyya): Conceptual; something that exists only in mental consideration, not externally.
وجوب (wujub): Obligation; a conceptual attribute of an act that makes its performance necessary.
إيجاب (ijab): Imposition of obligation; the act of making something obligatory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it valid to apply categories (maqulat) to God's attributes?
A) Because God's attributes are real and distinct, compatible with categories.
B) Because the categories are conceptual, not real, so co-reference is possible.
C) Because Ibn Sina's argument was based on a false premise.
D) Because categories are only applied to created beings, not to God.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses conceptual (i'tibari) categories to speak of divine attributes, preserving tawhid while allowing rational theological and usul discourse.

Lifetime vs Expanded Obligations: Sin for Delay
Commentary refining a distinction on sin and delay in obligations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-wajib al-'umari and al-wajib al-muwassa' regarding sin for delay, and explain the Hanafi principle that separates the obligation itself from the obligation to perform.

Core Idea
For lifetime obligations, delaying until death is sin; for expanded obligations, no sin until the time window ends.

The great usuli Ibn al-Hajib proposed a distinction: delaying a lifetime obligation like Hajj incurs sin even if one assumes safety and dies suddenly, whereas delaying an expanded obligation like prayer does not. The commentator first challenges this as unsound but then refines it, revealing a subtle Hanafi principle about the relationship between time, obligation, and sin. This lesson unpacks that refinement and its implications for usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of obligations
From the passage, locate the definitions of 'lifetime obligation' (wajib 'umari) and 'expanded obligation' (wajib muwassa'). Note their examples: Hajj for the former, daily prayers for the latter.
The whole life is the time for the first; a specific window for the second.
2
Understand Ibn al-Hajib's original claim
Ibn al-Hajib claimed that delaying a lifetime obligation is sinful even with assumption of safety and sudden death, but delaying an expanded obligation is not sinful under the same conditions.
This is the view the commentator initially critiques.
3
Analyze the initial critique
The commentator argues that if obligation is common to both types, then sin should apply equally. The excuse of sudden death is also common, so the distinction seems unsound.
This critique is stated as 'ليس بسديد' (not sound).
4
Recognize the refined distinction
The commentator then defends the distinction: for lifetime obligations, the entire life is the time, so missing it by death is a sin; for expanded obligations, death before the time's end means the time was not fully missed. Additionally, the obligation to perform (wujub al-ada') is separate from the underlying obligation, so no sin before the end.
The key phrase: 'الوجوب ووجوب الأداء مفترقان في الموسع'.
5
Apply the usul principle
The Hanafi principle of separating wujub al-asl from wujub al-ada' explains why sin is not incurred early in expanded obligations. This principle governs how delayed performance relates to sin.
This is a rational (aqli) derivation, not based on a specific text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفرق الشيخ ابن الحاجب بين ما وقته العمر كالحج فيعصي بالتأخير، وإن كان مع ظن السلامة والموت فجأة وبين غيره أي غير ما وقته العمر، وهو الواجب الموسع فلا يعصي بالتأخير مع ظن السلامة وإن مات فجأة ليس بسديد لأن الوجوب مشترك بين الواجب العمري والموسع، فإن كان سبب العصيان في الأول الوجوب فينبغي أن يعصي في الثاني أيضاً وعذر الفجأة عام فيهما، فلو قبل عذر الفجأة في الموسع قبل في العمري فلا فرق وفيه ما فيه) فإن الفرق ظاهر، لأن العمري وقته العمر كله فالوجوب فيه يقتضي أن يعصي بالتأخير عن العمر، فإذا أخر الحج مثلاً ومات فجأة فقد تركه في تمام وقته المقدر وهو إثم، وإلا ارتفع الوجوب بخلاف الواجب الموسع، فإنه إذا مات قبل الآخر فما تركه في تمام وقته بتقصير منه، لكن حينئذ ينبغي أن لا فيما إذا ظن الموت، وأيضاً الوجوب ووجوب الأداء مفترقان في الموسع، فلا يأثم قبل الآخر لعدم وجوب الأداء، وفي العمرى لا ينفصل أحدهما عن الآخر فيأثم بالترك، هذا والله الغفور للمأثم يغفر لمن يشاء ويعذب من يشاء.
And the Shaykh Ibn al-Hajib distinguished between what its time is the whole life, like Hajj, so one sins by delaying, even if with assumption of safety and sudden death, and other than it, i.e., other than what its time is the whole life, which is the expanded obligation, so one does not sin by delaying with assumption of safety even if one dies suddenly. This is not sound, because obligation is common between the lifetime obligation and the expanded obligation. If the cause of sin in the first is obligation, then one should also sin in the second, and the excuse of suddenness is common in both. So if the excuse of suddenness is accepted in the expanded, it would be accepted in the lifetime, so there is no difference. And there is something in it. Indeed the distinction is apparent, because the lifetime obligation's entire time is the whole life, so the obligation therein requires that one sins by delaying beyond life. So if he delays, for example, Hajj and dies suddenly, he has left it at the end of its appointed time, and it is sin. Otherwise, the obligation would be lifted, unlike the expanded obligation. For if he dies before the end, he has not left it at the end of its time by his negligence. But then it should not be so when he assumes death. Also, obligation and the obligation of performance are separate in the expanded, so he does not sin before the end due to lack of obligation of performance. In the lifetime obligation, one is not separate from the other, so he sins by leaving. This, and Allah is the Forgiving of sins, forgives whom He wills and punishes whom He wills.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a distinction made by Ibn al-Hajib between two types of obligations: the 'lifetime obligation' (wajib 'umari) such as Hajj, whose time is the entire life, and the 'expanded obligation' (wajib musa') such as prayer, which has a broad time window. Ibn al-Hajib claimed that delaying a lifetime obligation incurs sin even if one assumes safety and dies suddenly, whereas delaying an expanded obligation does not incur sin under the same assumption. The author critiques this distinction as unsound, arguing that the obligation is common to both, so if sin is due to obligation in the first, it should also occur in the second. Moreover, the excuse of sudden death applies equally. However, the author then defends a nuanced distinction: for lifetime obligations, the entire time is the lifespan, so delaying until death means missing the entire time, which is sinful. For expanded obligations, the time extends to the end of the time window; if one dies before the end, the time has not fully passed, so no sin. The author also notes that obligation and the obligation to perform are separate in expanded obligations: one is not obligated to perform until near the end, so no sin for early delay. In lifetime obligations, this separation does not exist. The passage concludes with a phrase seeking forgiveness.

Key Terms
الوجوب العمري (al-wujūb al-ʿumrī): An obligation whose entire life is its time, such as Hajj; delaying it until death constitutes sin.
الواجب الموسع (al-wājib al-muwassaʿ): An obligation that has a broad time window, like the five daily prayers; sin for delay is not incurred until the end of its time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, does delaying a lifetime obligation like Hajj with assumption of safety incur sin if death comes suddenly?
A) Yes, because the entire life is the time and death means missing the entire time.
B) No, because sudden death is an excuse that nullifies sin.
C) It depends on whether one intended to perform it later.
D) Only if one explicitly refuses to perform it.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi refined distinction: lifetime obligations incur sin upon delay until death; expanded obligations do not, because of the separation between the obligation itself and the obligation to perform.

Third and Fourth Types of Rukhsa: Definition and Application
Defines two types of rukhsa and discusses a derivative issue on khuffs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between the third and fourth types of rukhsa (license) in Hanafi usul and apply the classification to the subsidiary issue of wiping over khuffs.

Core Idea
The third rukhsa abrogates past burdens; the fourth drops a ruling due to an excuse, while the original ruling remains legislated.

The Hanafi usul scholars classified rukhsa into four types. This passage defines the third and fourth types, clarifying their metaphorical use, and then applies the fourth type to the case of wiping over khuffs—a classic derivative issue.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the four types of rukhsa
Recall the general definition of rukhsa (a license) and its categorization into four types: (1) actual change from hardship to ease (e.g., shortening prayer), (2) allowing something that remains prohibited in principle but permitted due to necessity (e.g., carrion for the starving, but the prohibition remains), (3) abrogation of prior burdens, and (4) dropping of a ruling due to an excuse while the ruling is still legislated in general.
The third type involves rulings that were obligations on previous nations but are completely removed for us.
2
Grasp the third type: abrogated burdens
Examples include trimming the place of impurity (from the Torah?), payment of one-fourth in zakat, repentance requiring killing, prohibition of plunder, non-permissibility of tayammum, prayer only in mosques, and non-permissibility of spoils. These rulings were abrogated for this ummah as a lightening.
The abrogating ruling itself is considered a rukhsa, even though the original ruling is no longer applicable.
3
Grasp the fourth type: license of dropping
This is where a ruling that normally applies is dropped due to an excuse, while the ruling remains legislated in principle. Example: carrion becomes permissible for the starving person; the prohibition is dropped due to the excuse of necessity.
If one does not avail the license and suffers harm, he sins. Some, like Abu Yusuf, consider this as part of the first type.
4
Analyze the khuff subsidiary issue
The claim: Dropping the washing of the foot when wearing khuffs is from the fourth type (license of dropping), since the khuff prevents the transmission of hadath to the foot. Objection: If washing were not legislated, it would match the fourth type, but washing is still legislated because one can wash the foot inside the khuff and water entering the khuff invalidates wiping (according to some narrations). Response: The narration is not authentic; wiping is not invalidated. The non-obligation of washing after expiry of period shows washing has already occurred, so it is a license of dropping.
The Hanafi position is that wiping over khuffs suffices and the fourth type applies.

The third: what was abrogated from us as a lightening, being what was upon those before us of burdens on previous nations, and the abrogating ruling is a license, like the trimming of the place of impurity, and the payment of one-fourth in zakat, and other things such as repentance being killing, prohibition of plunder, non-permissibility of tayammum, non-permissibility of prayer except in the mosque, and non-permissiblity of spoils. The fourth: that to which the ruling falls (i.e., is dropped) due to an excuse while its legislation remains in general terms, i.e., that excuse, and it is called a license of dropping, like the dropping of the prohibition of carrion for the one compelled (by hunger), for Allah Most High, by the evidence of prohibition and the exception, spoke of the remainder after the exception, so the prohibition does not attach to it due to this excuse, and similarly for the one coerced. And in this type, if he does not take it and suffers harm, he certainly sins. And according to some jurists, the dropping of the prohibition of carrion is from the first (type), and this is narrated from Imam Abu Yusuf. Then, for sin, knowledge of permissibility is absolutely necessary, as you have learned. They said: Naming the last two types as 'license' is metaphorical, for there is no change from hardship to ease; rather, ease is original, so there is no real license. And the third type is more complete in metaphoricity, since the original ruling is not at all legislated, so there is no trace of it being 'azima, unlike the fourth type, for in it there is a trace of license because it is legislated in other than the excuse situation, like the first (type) in reality, i.e., just as the first type is more complete in being a real license. And know that our scholars divided 'azima into fard, wajib, sunna, and nafl, and the license into what you have heard, and their intent is not that license is not divided into these divisions; rather, they divided 'azima because it is the original, and the status of license is known by comparison. And license had another division specific to it which they addressed, and their aim is not to divide 'azima absolutely, but the legislated 'azima which contains reward, therefore they did not divide it into mubah, haram, and makruh, since both are included in the divisions, because refraining is an action. And indeed a kind of verbosity occurred, but it is not devoid of benefit. A subsidiary issue: They said: The dropping of the washing of the foot with the khuff is from the fourth division of license, i.e., the license of dropping, because the khuff is considered legislatively as a preventer of the transmission of the hadath to it (the foot), and if it does not transmit, then what the legislator has placed for removing hadath is not legislated, and it becomes like the belly and the thigh. But it is responded that this would only be complete if the washing there of the foot were not legislated, because the characteristic of the fourth type is that, but it is legislated even if he does not remove his khuffs, for if he were to wash his feet while they are in the khuff, his wudu' would be complete. For this reason, i.e., the legislation of washing, his wiping would be invalidated if he wades into the river after having performed wudu' and wiped over the khuff and water entered the khuff. So it is known that washing is legislated. And then, washing is not obligatory a second time upon the expiry of the period, and this too is a sign of legislation; otherwise, washing would become obligatory upon expiry of the period. Similarly, the situation in removal (of the khuff). And it was answered by denying the authenticity of the narration of the invalidation of wiping, rather we say: wiping is not invalidated. And Shaykh Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir approved this. As for the non-obligation of washing after removal, it is because: Indeed washing is not obligatory after removal and after expiry of the period because it has already occurred, so washing after it is obtaining the obtained. This answer was refuted by the first aspect: that the narration is mentioned in reliable books like al-Zahiriya and others, so there is no basis for denying its authenticity. Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the third and fourth types of rukhsa (license) as classified in Hanafi usul. The third type consists of rulings that were originally burdensome upon previous nations but have been abrogated for us as a lightening (e.g., mandatory trimmed area for impurity removal, payment of one-fourth in zakat, repentance requiring killing, prohibition of plunder, tayammum not allowed, prayer only in mosque, spoils not permissible). The abrogating ruling itself is considered a rukhsa. The fourth type is the license of dropping: a ruling that normally applies drops due to an excuse (e.g., carrion becomes permissible for the starving person; similarly for coercion). This is called 'rukhsat al-isqat'. In this type, if a person does not avail the license and suffers harm, he sins. Some jurists, including Imam Abu Yusuf, consider the dropping of carrion's prohibition as part of the first type (actual change from hardship to ease). The passage then discusses a terminology point: calling the last two types 'rukhsa' is metaphorical because there is no change from hardship to ease; ease is original. The third type is more metaphorical since the original ruling is completely gone. The fourth type has a trace of license because it is still legislated in non-excuse situations. The text then notes that Hanafi scholars divided 'azima into fard, wajib, sunna, and nafl, but they did not divide rukhsa in the same way because they were focusing on the original category. However, rukhsa has its own specific divisions they addressed. A subsidiary issue (far') discusses whether the dropping of washing the foot when wearing khuffs belongs to the fourth type. The khuff prevents the transmission of hadath to the foot, so washing is not required; it is a license of dropping. But an objection arises: if washing the feet were not legislated while wearing khuffs, this would match the fourth type. However, washing is still legislated because if one washes the feet inside the khuff, wudu' is valid. Evidence: if water enters the khuff, the wiping is invalidated, proving washing is legislated. Also, washing is not required again after the period expires, showing the original washing sufficed. The response to the objection is that the narration about invalidation is not authentic; Ibn al-Humam agreed. As for non-obligation after removal, it is because the washing already occurred. The counterargument: the narration is in reliable books like al-Zahiriya, so it cannot be denied.

Key Terms
رخصة (rukhsa): A license: a ruling that changes from hardship to ease, or a dropping of a ruling due to an excuse. Here divided into four types.
عزيمة (ʿazīma): The original ruling of obligation or prohibition without concession; subdivided by Hanafi scholars into fard, wajib, sunna, and nafl.
رخصة إسقاط (rukhsat al-isqāṭ): A license of dropping: where the ruling falls due to an excuse while the ruling remains legislated in general; e.g., permissibility of carrion for the desperate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of the third type of rukhsa?
A) Permissibility of carrion for the starving
B) Shortening the prayer while traveling
C) Abrogation of the prohibition of plunder for this ummah
D) Wiping over khuffs instead of washing feet

Key Takeaway
The third and fourth types of rukhsa are metaphorical licenses: the third abrogates past burdens completely, while the fourth drops a ruling for an excuse while keeping it legislated in principle.

The Distinction Between Conceptualizing the Impossible as an Act and Absolutely
Response to the fifth objection on conceiving the impossible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conceptualizing the impossible as an act (iqa') and absolutely, and apply this distinction to respond to objections about taklif al-muhal.

Core Idea
Conceptualizing the impossible as an act is impossible, but absolutely is possible.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi argument in the debate on the obligation of the impossible. The author responds to the fifth objection that even stating impossibility involves conceptualizing the impossible. The response hinges on a precise distinction between two modes of conceptualization: as an act to be performed, and absolutely. Understanding this distinction is crucial for defending the Hanafi position on taklif al-muhal.

Guided Path
1
Understand the fifth objection
The opponent says that saying 'the conjunction of two opposites is impossible' requires conceptualizing an impossible subject, contradicting the claim that the impossible cannot be conceptualized at all.
2
Identify the Hanafi response
The author argues that the judgment is on the nature (title) by considering its instances; the impossible can be conceptualized as a title, not as an actualized act.
3
Grasp the distinction
There is a difference between conceptualizing the impossible as an act (iqa') and conceptualizing it absolutely (mutlaqan). The first is impossible because it demands actualization; the second is not impossible because it merely refers to a concept.
4
Apply to the objection
In the proposition about impossibility, only absolute conceptualization is needed, not conceptualization as an act. Therefore, the objection fails.
This distinction is a key tool in rational argumentation within Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثانية في الأحكام الحال، إذ ليس حقيقة تتمثل وتوصف بالإيقاع، فإنه لا يصلح الاتصاف به، وأشار إلى هذا الدفع في الاستدلال بمفهوم قوله : وتصور وقوع المحال من حيث هو محال باطل، أي وتصور وقوع الممكن بما هو ممكن صحيح (و) قال خامساً : إن قولنا اجتماع النقيضين محال) قضية موجبة يستلزم تصور المحال الموضوع (مثبتاً ) فأمكن تصور المحال فانتقض قوله : وتصور وقوع المحال الخ (أقول) في الجواب الحكم فيه على الطبيعة باعتبار الفرد كما حققنا في السلم وتقريره أن المحال لا يتصور فلا يحكم عليه لا إيجاباً ولا سلباً، وأما أمثال هذه القضية فالعنوان فيها ممكن عام ليس محالاً فلا يحكم عليه بالاستحالة، لكن يصح الحكم عليه باعتبار موارد تحققه فإن الانتفاء ثابت للعنوان، بمعنى أن موارد تحققه منتفية، وقد استوفينا الكلام المتعلق بهذا في شرحه فاطلبه هناك، ولولا كون الفن غريباً لأشبعنا الكلام فيه، وإن شئت أن يظهر لك حقيقة الحال في أمثال هذه القضية فاطلب من حواشينا المتعلقة بالحواشي الزاهدية على شرح المواقف، لكن اعلم ها هنا أن هذا غير واف فيما هو بصدده، فإن له أن يقول : لما كفى تصور العنوان للحكم باعتبار موارد تحققه فليكف في طلب موارد تحققه تصور العنوان، وإن شئت قل تصور المكلف العنوان وكلف بإيقاعه في ضمن موارد التحقق فالصواب في الجواب ما أشار إليه بقوله : على أنه فرق بين (تصوره أي المحال (إيقاعاً وبين تصوره مطلقاً فالأول محال لازم على تقدير التكليف به، لأنه طلب الإيقاع ولا بد من تصور المطلوب كما طلب بخلاف الثاني فإنه ليس مستحيلاً، وهو اللازم في القضية المنقوض بها، إذ لا بد للحكم من تصور العنوان لا تصور إيقاعه ( فتدبر)
The second article on rulings regarding the state, since it is not a reality that is represented and described as actualization, for it is not suitable to be described by it. He pointed to this refutation in inference by the implication of his statement: 'Conceptualizing the occurrence of the impossible, insofar as it is impossible, is false,' meaning conceptualizing the occurrence of the possible insofar as it is possible is correct. (And) he said fifthly: 'Our statement that the conjunction of two opposites is impossible is a positive proposition that necessitates the conceptualization of the impossible subject (affirmatively). Thus conceptualizing the impossible became possible, so his statement that conceptualizing the occurrence of the impossible is false is contradicted.' (I say) in response: the judgment in it is on the nature by consideration of the individual, as we verified in logic, and its meaning is that the impossible cannot be conceptualized, so no judgment—positive or negative—can be made about it. As for such propositions, the title in them is a general possible that is not impossible, so it is not judged as impossible. However, it is valid to judge it by considering its instances of actualization, for negation is established for the title, meaning that its instances of actualization are negated. We have covered the discussion related to this in its commentary, so seek it there. Were it not that the discipline is obscure, we would have elaborated on it. If you wish the truth of the matter in such propositions to become clear to you, seek from our glosses on the Zāhidiyya glosses on the commentary on Mawāqif. But know here that this is not sufficient for what he is dealing with, for he can say: when the conceptualization of the title suffices for judgment by consideration of its instances of actualization, then the conceptualization of the title should suffice in seeking its instances of actualization. Or if you wish, say: the conceptualization of the title by the obligated person, and he is commanded to actualize it within instances of actualization. So the correct response is what he indicated by his statement: 'For there is a distinction between conceptualizing it—i.e., the impossible—as an actualization and conceptualizing it absolutely. The first is necessarily impossible given the assumption of being commanded to do it, because it is a demand for actualization, and conceptualization of the demanded is necessary as demanded. The second, on the other hand, is not impossible, and it is what is necessary in the proposition used to refute, for judgment requires conceptualization of the title, not conceptualization of its actualization.' So contemplate.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the fifth objection in a debate about the obligation of the impossible (taklif al-muhal). The opponent argues that even saying 'the conjunction of two opposites is impossible' involves a conceptualization of an impossible subject, which would contradict the claim that the impossible cannot be conceptualized. The Hanafi response distinguishes between conceptualizing the impossible as an act (bi iqa') and conceptualizing it absolutely (mutlaqan). The former is impossible because it requires the actualization of the impossible, but the latter is possible because it merely involves thinking about the title. The author clarifies that judgments about impossibility relate to the title of the proposition, not its instances. This distinction resolves the objection and is a key Hanafi argument against taklif al-muhal.

Key Terms
المحال (al-muhal): That which is impossible by essence (muhal bi al-dhat), cannot be conceptualized or actualized.
التصور (al-tasawwur): Conceptualization; forming a mental image of something.
الإيقاع (al-iqa'): Actualization or making something occur; here it refers to conceiving something as an act to be performed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in this passage, what is the difference between conceiving the impossible as an act and conceiving it absolutely?
A) Both are impossible.
B) Conceiving it absolutely is impossible, but as an act is possible.
C) Conceiving it as an act is impossible, but absolutely is not.
D) Both are possible.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi distinction between conceptualizing the impossible as an act (impossible) and absolutely (possible) resolves the fifth objection against taklif al-muhal.

Refutation of the Impossibility of Taklif When the Act Already Exists
Refuting Ash'ari claim that taklif remains after the act

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Ash'ari claim that taklif persists after the act and apply the Hanafi decomposition argument to reject it.

Core Idea
Demanding what already exists is inherently impossible — taklif must precede each part of the act.

This passage addresses a key usul dispute between the Hanafis and the Ash'aris: does taklif (obligation) continue to exist at the moment the obligated act is being performed? The Ash'ari school holds that the obligation persists because taklif means incurring hardship, which continues. The Hanafi school rejects this by analyzing the act into parts and showing that the demand for each part exists before that part. Let's work through the Hanafi argument as presented by 'Abd al-'Aziz al-Bukhari in his commentary.

Guided Path
1
State the Ash'ari position
Recognize that the Ash'ari claim is that taklif remains when the act exists, either because taklif is hardship or because the demand is attached to the whole act gradually.
This is the view being refuted.
2
Identify the Hanafi objection
The Hanafi position is that demanding what already exists (talab al-mawjud) is impossible by necessity. If taklif persisted at the moment of the act's existence, it would be a demand for something that already exists.
This is the core logical objection.
3
Follow the Hanafi decomposition
For an extended act, the demand is resolved into parts corresponding to parts of the act. Each part of the demand precedes its corresponding part of the act. Thus, no part of the demand coincides with the existence of the act.
The key is that the demand is not attached to the whole as a single unit but to each part separately.
4
Rebut the Ash'ari counter-argument
The Ash'ari might argue that the demand is for the whole, which occurs gradually. The Hanafi response is that the demand for the whole exists before any part of the act, so it is still prior, not simultaneous.
The text states that the demand for the whole is before the act's occurrence, and each part's demand is before that part.
5
Conclude the ruling
Since no part of the demand coincides with the existence of the act, the Ash'ari view is false. Taklif must be prior to the act, not simultaneous.
This is the mufta bihi position in Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأشعري وأبطل بأنه تكليف بإيجاد الموجود، ورد بأن إيجاد الموجود بهذا الإيجاد غير ممتنع والمصنف قرر بنمط آخر وقال : (وهو باطل، لأنه كما تقول الطلب باق حين وجود المطلوب، وهو) أي طلب الموجود باطل بالضرورة كما ترى وقد يؤوّل بأن المراد بالتكليف إيقاع المكلف في الكلفة، ولا شك في بقائه، وحينئذ لا يرد شيء، والقول بأنه لم يقل ببقاء الطلب بل ببقاء اشتغال الذمة غير تام فإن اشتغال الذمة بالأمر المتحقق مما لا يعقل فافهم (وما يقال) لتصحيحه (إن التكليف متعلق بالمجموع من الفعل من حيث المجموع (وهو يحدث شيئاً فشيئاً) على التدريج (فيلزم مقارنته بالحدوث ولا يلزم طلب الموجود، لأنه إنما يوجد إذا وجد الجزء الأخير فمع أنه لا يتم في (الآنيات إذ ليس حدوثها شيئاً فشيئاً (فاسد، لأن الفعل إذا كان ممتداً كان الطلب المتعلق به محللاً إلى الأجزاء حسب أجزاء الفعل، وكل جزء من الفعل تعلق به جزء من الطلب فكل جزء منه مسبوق بجزء من الطلب المتعلق به وهذا ظاهر، فإن قلت : المطلوب بالذات ليس إلا المجموع بما هو المجموع، وإن كان الطلب المتعلق به ذا أجزاء بالعرض والطلب المتعلق بالمجموع متحقق حال ابتداء حدوثه فتم مطلوب الخصم قلت : إن طلب المجموع بما هو مجموع موجود قبل حدوثه، فإن حدوثه ليس في أول الأجزاء، إذ لم يوجد بعض أجزائه بعد، وكذا طلب كل جزء قبله، فلا معية أصلاً فتأمل
Al-Ash'ari invalidated (the argument) by saying it is a charge to bring into existence what exists. It was responded that bringing into existence what exists by this bringing-into-existence is not impossible. And the author (al-Musannif) established (the matter) in another way and said: (It is false because, as you say, the demand remains at the time when the demanded (act) exists. But) meaning: the demand for what exists is false by necessity, as you see. And it may be interpreted that what is meant by taklif is placing the obligated in hardship, and there is no doubt of its continuance, and then nothing is rebutted. And the statement that he did not say the demand remains but rather the engagement of the responsibility (dhimmah) remains is incomplete because the engagement of the responsibility with an already realized matter is unintelligible, so understand. (And what is said) to correct it: (that taklif is attached to the whole of the act in terms of the whole (and it occurs bit by bit) gradually, so it necessitates its coincidence with occurrence and does not necessitate demanding what exists, because it only exists when the last part exists, while it is not completed in instants since their occurrence is not bit by bit) is false, because if the act is extended, the demand attached to it is resolved into parts according to the parts of the act, and each part of the act has a part of the demand attached to it, so each part of it is preceded by a part of the demand attached to it, and this is obvious. If you say: the demanded in essence is only the whole as whole, even if the demand attached to it has parts accidentally, and the demand attached to the whole is realized at the beginning of its occurrence, so the opponent's claim is fulfilled, I say: the demand for the whole as whole exists before its occurrence, for its occurrence is not at the first part, since some of its parts have not yet come into existence, and likewise the demand for each part is before it, so there is no simultaneity at all, so reflect.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi refutation of the Ash'ari claim that taklif (obligation) persists even after the obligated act has come into existence. The author first states the Ash'ari objection: that charging someone with bringing into existence what already exists is impossible. The Ash'ari then responds that the obligation is not to bring into existence but to incur hardship, which continues. The Hanafi author (al-Musannif) refutes this by breaking down the act into parts and showing that demand must precede each part, not coincide with the whole. The key point is that demanding what already exists is inherently contradictory. The argument uses logical decomposition of continuous acts to demonstrate that the demand cannot be simultaneous with the act's existence.

Key Terms
طلب الموجود (talab al-mawjud): The demand for something that already exists, which is considered impossible in Hanafi usul.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Hanafis reject the Ash'ari claim that taklif remains when the act exists?
A) Because taklif only applies to past acts
B) Because demanding what already exists is impossible
C) Because the act is divine
D) Because the obligated is no longer responsible

Key Takeaway
Taklif cannot coincide with the existence of the act; it must precede each part, otherwise it would entail the impossible demand for what already exists.

Hanafi Refutation of Ash'ari: Act Not Maqdur at Existence
Responding to Ash'ari argument that the act is possible when occurring

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether an act is maqdur (possible) at the time of its existence, and explain the three refutations against the Ash'ari claim.

Core Idea
The act at the moment of existence is not maqdur: it is necessary by choice and demanding what exists is impossible.

The Ash'ari school argues that at the very moment an act is being performed, it is maqdur (possible) for the agent because it is the effect of their power. This would validate taklif (obligation) during the act. The Hanafi response is multilayered: first, power has no creative effect; second, even if it did, the act becomes necessary by choice and thus not possible; third, demanding what already exists is impossible. This lesson unpacks these arguments.

Guided Path
1
State the Ash'ari position
Identify the Ash'ari claim: the act is maqdur at the time of existence because it is the effect of power, so taklif is valid then.
They equate 'effect of power' with 'possible'.
2
First Hanafi response – denial of effect
The Hanafi denies that for the Ash'ari the act can be an effect of power, since Ash'aris hold that power has no role in creation (only kasb, which is not causal). This is a dialectical move.
This response is considered weak so the author moves on.
3
Second Hanafi response – necessity by choice
Even conceding that the act is an effect of power, the Hanafi argues that the act becomes necessary (wajib) by the agent's choice, and what is necessary cannot be maqdur (possible).
Key premise: 'a thing does not exist unless it becomes necessary' (something cannot exist without being necessitated).
4
Third Hanafi response – obstacle of demanding the existing
Even conceding that the effect of power is possible, there remains the obstacle that taklif demands what already exists, which is impossible.
This is considered the strongest rebuttal.
5
Summarize the tarjih
The Hanafi position is that the act is not maqdur at the time of existence, so taklif is valid only before the act. The three refutations build on each other.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتشكر الأشعرية (قالوا : الفعل مقدور حينئذ أي حين وجوده (لأنه أثر القدرة) وأثرها مقدور، وإذا كان مقدوراً (فيصح التكليف (به في هذا الحين إذ لا مانع من التكليف إلا عدم القدرة، وقد انتفى أيضاً قلنا: لا نسلم أنه أثرها فإنه لا تأثير للقدرة عندكم) أصلاً، لا في الكسب ولا في الإيجاد، ولما كان هذا الجواب جدلياً وفاسداً أيضاً لأنه أراد بأثر القدرة ما تعلقت به القدرة المتوهمة التي هي مدار صحة التكليف عنده لم يكتف به، وأجاب بعد تسليمه وقال (ولو سلم أنه أثر القدرة كما هو مذهبنا إذ لها دخل وتأثير في الكسب (فلا نسلم أنه يستلزم المقدورية، فإنه يجب الفعل بالاختيار، لأن الشيء ما لم يجب لم يوجد) والواجب لا يكون مقدوراً، ولما كان هذا أيضاً فاسداً لا لأنهم يجوزون الوجود من غير وجوب، وترجيح المختار أحد المتساويين، ولهذا صححوا حدوث العالم مع كونه مستنداً إلى الباري عز وجل، فإنك قد عرفت أن الوجود من غير وجوب باطل، وكذا الترجيح من غير رجحان وتصحيح الحدوث لا يتوقف على هذا بل يصح مع القول بالوجوب كما أشرنا سابقاً، بل لأن الوجوب بالاختيار لا يوجب الاضطرار، وعدم المقدورية كما بينا سابقاً لم يكتف بهذا الجواب أيضاً، وأجاب بوجه آخر وقال : (ولو سلم أن أثر القدرة مقدور (فلا نسلم أن لا مانع إلا ذلك، بل لزوم طلب الموجود أيضاً مانع .
And the Ash'aris doubted (or: and the Ash'aris said): they said: The act is possible at that time, i.e., at the time of its existence, (because it is the effect of power) and its effect is possible, and if it is possible, then the obligation (is valid) at that time, since there is no obstacle to obligation except lack of power, and that has also been removed. We said: We do not accept that it is its effect, for there is no effect of power according to you (the Ash'aris) at all, neither in acquisition (kasb) nor in creation (ijad). And since this reply was dialectical and invalid also because he meant by 'effect of power' that which the imagined power is attached to, which is the basis of validity of obligation according to him, he did not suffice with it, and he responded after conceding (the point) and said: (Even if we concede that it is the effect of power as is our doctrine, for it (power) has a role and effect in acquisition, we do not concede that it necessitates possibility, because the act becomes necessary by choice, for a thing does not exist unless it becomes necessary, and the necessary is not possible. And since this (reply) was also invalid, not because they (the Ash'aris) allow existence without necessity, and the preference of the free agent among two equals, and for this they established the origination of the world even though it is dependent on the Creator, for you have known that existence without necessity is false, and likewise preference without preference (rajhan), and the validation of origination does not depend on this but is valid even with the doctrine of necessity as we indicated earlier, but rather because necessity by choice does not cause compulsion and lack of possibility as we explained earlier. He did not suffice with this reply either, and answered with another face and said: (Even if we concede that the effect of power is possible, we do not concede that there is no obstacle except that, but rather the necessity of demanding what exists is also an obstacle.)
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the Hanafi-Ash'ari debate. The Ash'aris argue that at the moment the act is being performed, the act is possible (maqdur) because it is the effect of power, and therefore taklif is valid at that moment. The Hanafi responds first by denying that the act is an effect of power for the Ash'aris, since in their view power has no effect in creation. Then, even conceding that power has an effect, the Hanafi argues that the act becomes necessary by choice, and the necessary cannot be possible (i.e., it cannot be the object of voluntary power). Finally, even conceding that the effect of power is possible, the Hanafi points out that there is still the obstacle of demanding what already exists, which has already been proven impossible. The text shows a multi-layered Hanafi refutation, demonstrating that even if one grants the Ash'ari premises, the conclusion does not follow.

Key Terms
أثر القدرة (athar al-qudra): The effect of power; the act that results from the exercise of capacity.
الوجوب بالاختيار (al-wujub bi al-ikhtiyar): Necessity arising from choice; the act becomes necessary to exist once the free agent chooses it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a Hanafi response to the Ash'ari claim that the act is maqdur at the time of existence?
A) The act is not an effect of power because power has no effect in acquisition
B) The act, once necessary by choice, is not possible
C) There is still the obstacle of demanding what exists
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The act at the time of its existence is not maqdur, and taklif is valid only before the act, as demonstrated by the Hanafi refutations.

Istita'a vs Qudra: Hanafi Refutation of Ash'ari Simultaneity
Distinguishing istita'a from qudra and rebutting Ash'ari arguments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish istita'a from qudra and apply the Hanafi rebuttal to the three Ash'ari proofs regarding the simultaneity of power with the act.

Core Idea
Istita'a accompanies the act; qudra is a broader attribute that can precede—Hanafis defend this with the universal nature of power.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut defines istita'a as power with all conditions, simultaneous with the act, and distinguishes it from qudra. The author then presents three Ash'ari proofs arguing that power must be simultaneous, and refutes each. Understanding this distinction is key to appreciating the Hanafi position on obligation before performance.

Guided Path
1
Identify definitions
Locate the definitions of istita'a and qudra. Note that istita'a is the power that includes all conditions and is simultaneous with the act, while qudra is a broader attribute that can exist before the act.
The text says: 'اعلم أن القدرة المتعلقة بالفعل المستجمعة لجميع الشرائط ... تسمى استطاعة وهي مع الفعل'.
2
Examine first Ash'ari proof and response
Read the first proof: power is attached like a strike to the struck, requiring simultaneity. Identify the Hanafi response: (a) refuted by divine power, (b) power is an attribute with potential, not requiring existence of the object.
The response includes: 'منقوض بقدرة الباري' and 'لا نسلم أنها متعلقة بل صفة لها صلاحية التعلق'.
3
Examine second proof and response
Read the second proof: power is an accident that does not persist, so if it precedes the act it would perish. Identify the Hanafi response: (a) denial that accidents do not persist, (b) even if conceded, the universal nature (ṭabīʿa kulliyya) persists through successive instances.
Key phrase: 'الشرط في التكليف الطبيعة الكلية لها التي تبقى بتوارد الأمثال'.
4
Examine third proof and response
Read the third proof: the act cannot be before itself, so power cannot be before the act. Identify the Hanafi response: the argument is self-refuting (cf. divine power).
The text says: 'وهو فاسد كما ترى'.
5
Extract Hanafi principle
From the responses, extract the principle that power (qudra) is an attribute with potential attachment, and the universal nature (ṭabīʿa kulliyya) of power is what matters for obligation, not the specific individual instance.
This principle allows Hanafis to hold that power can exist before the act, enabling obligation before performance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن القدرة المتعلقة بالفعل المستجمعة لجميع الشرائط التي يوجد الفعل بها أو يخلق الله تعالى عندها تسمى استطاعة وهي مع الفعل ،البتة كما روي عن الإمام الهمام في «الوصايا : ولعل مراد الأشعري هذا وأما إنكار القدرة رأساً فالأشعري أجل من أن يتفوه أن يتخذه مذهباً، لكن لما جاء التابعون ولم يتعمقوا في مراده، فهموا أن القدرة لا فيما مر به فضلاً عن تكون قبل الفعل، ونقلوا هكذا واشتهر فيما بينهم، وقد صرح الإمام فخر الدين الرازي الذي من متبعيه بهذا أيضاً والله أعلم بحال عباده الأشعرية (قالوا : أولاً أنها متعلقة بالمقدور تعلق الضرب بالمضروب، ووجود المتعلق بهذا النحو من التعلق بدون المتعلق محال) وهذا الدليل أيضاً يرشدك إلى أنه أراد بها الاستطاعة المذكورة (قلنا) أولاً (منقوض بقدرة الباري عز وجل، فإن الدليل جار فيها مع أنها ليست مع المقدور وإلا لزم قدم العالم) وثانياً : لا نسلم أنها متعلقة (بل) القدرة (صفة لها صلاحية التعلق فلا تستدعي وجود المقدور (و) قالوا (ثانياً : إنها عرض، وهو لا يبقى زمانين فلو تقدمت على الفعل (لعدمت) عنده (فلم تتعلق بالفعل فانتفت فائدة خلق القدرة (قلنا لا نسلم أن العرض لا يبقى زمانين ولم يقم عليه دليل ولو سلم عدم البقاء فالشرط) في التكليف الطبيعة الكلية لها التي تبقى بتوارد الأمثال) وهي المتقدمة على الفعل لا جزئي معين منها (و) قالوا :(ثالثاً : لا يمكن الفعل قبله) أي قبل نفسه فلا يكون مقدوراً (قبله فإذن ليس القدرة قبل الفعل (وهو) فاسد كما ترى) لأنه منقوض بقدرة
Know that the power (qudra) attached to the act, comprising all the conditions by which the act exists or Allah creates it upon them, is called istita'a (ability), and it is definitely with the act, as narrated from the leading Imam in 'al-Wasaya'. And perhaps the Ash'ari meant this. As for denying power altogether, the Ash'ari is too great to utter that and adopt it as a doctrine. But when the followers came and did not delve into his intention, they understood that power does not (exist) in what has passed (before the act), let alone being before the act, and they transmitted it thus, and it became famous among them. And Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, who is among his followers, explicitly stated this as well. And Allah knows best about the condition of His Ash'ari servants. (They said: First, that it (power) is attached to the possible (maqdur) like the attachment of a strike to the struck (object), and the existence of the attached (power) in this manner of attachment without the attached (maqdur) is impossible.) And this proof also indicates to you that he meant by it the aforementioned istita'a. (We said: First, it is refuted by the power of Allah the Exalted, for the proof applies to it while it is not with the maqdur, otherwise it would necessitate the eternity of the world.) And second: We do not accept that it is attached; rather, power is an attribute with the potential for attachment, so it does not require the existence of the maqdur. (And they said: Second, that it is an accident, and it does not persist for two moments; so if it preceded the act, it would cease at the act, so it would not be attached to the act, and thus the benefit of creating power would be lost. (We said: We do not accept that the accident does not persist for two moments, and no proof has been established for it. And even if lack of persistence is conceded, the condition for obligation is the universal nature of it (power) that persists through the succession of similar instances, and that (nature) is prior to the act, not a specific particular of it. (And they said: Third, it is not possible for the act to be before it, i.e., before itself, so it would not be possible before it; thus, power is not before the act. (And it) is false as you see, because it is refuted by power...
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage first defines istita'a (ability) as the power accompanied by all conditions, which is simultaneous with the act. The author notes that the Ash'ari position might actually be about istita'a, not qudra in general. However, later Ash'aris misinterpreted this as a denial of any power before the act. The text then presents three Ash'ari proofs that power must be simultaneous with the act: (1) power is attached to its object like a strike to the struck; (2) power is an accident that does not persist; (3) the act cannot be before itself, so power cannot be before it. The Hanafi responses: (1) The analogy fails for divine power; (2) The ‘universal nature’ of power can persist through replacement of instances; (3) The argument is self-refuting. The lesson illustrates the sophisticated Hanafi critique of atomistic Ash'ari metaphysics.

Key Terms
استطاعة (istitā'a): Ability defined as power with all its conditions, simultaneous with the act.
قدرة (qudra): Power or capability that can precede the act; an attribute with potential for attachment.
الطبيعة الكلية (al-ṭabī'a al-kulliyya): The universal nature of power that persists through successive instances (tawārūd al-amthāl).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How do Hanafis refute the Ash'ari claim that power is an accident that does not persist?
A) They accept that power perishes but argue a new one is created
B) They say the universal nature of power persists through successive instances
C) They deny that power is an accident
D) They agree that power must be simultaneous

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi distinction between istita'a and qudra upholds the validity of obligation before performance, using the concept of a universal nature that persists through successive accidents.
