For what is observed at the time of coinage is the general universal matter like the concept of the one pointed to or the one absent male or the one described by the relative clause, but (it is not) observed (because it is coined for it, but rather to be made a mirror for the observation of (the individuals) the unlimited, so it is coined for them in their specificity, so no metaphor) occurs in them when used for specific instances, because they are used for what they were coined for (nor homonymy) because although they are coined for many, it is by one coinage not by many coinages, and in the homonym there are many coinages. And in it is a refutation of those who claim that they are coined for universal concepts to be used for individuals, not for individuals, otherwise homonymy between unlimited meanings would result. And it is attributed to the virtuous al-Taftazani (And as for the specific coinage for the general according to his point of view, which is that the general object is observed by a specific, particular face and the word is coined for it, it has not occurred; rather it is impossible due to the impossibility of this kind of observation. And as for what we have explained in the rule of the second and third divisions, the fourth division emerges: what is observed in it is words with a universal concept and the meaning with its specificity, and each one of the words is coined for this meaning once, and there is no doubt in its possibility and its non-occurrence.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the types of linguistic coinage (الوضع) as elaborated in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author identifies three main types and discusses a fourth. First, specific for specific (خاص لخاص): e.g., proper nouns like 'Zaid' and common nouns like 'man', where both the word and its meaning are observed in their particularity. Second, general for general (عام لعام): here both the word and meaning are observed under a universal aspect. The example given is the coinage of agent nouns (فاعل) on a general pattern. The author criticizes the view that this disregards the specificity of actual instances like past tense verbs, arguing that actual coinage always relates to specific events. The true summary is that multiple words are observed via a general concept, each referring to a specific meaning under that concept. Third, general for specific (عام لخاص): exemplified by demonstratives, pronouns, and particles. Here the word is observed under a general concept (e.g., 'that one'), but it is coined to refer to specific individuals. This avoids both metaphor and homonymy, since one coinage covers all individuals. The author refutes the notion that such words are coined for universals. Fourth, the possibility of specific for general (خاص لعام) is discussed via al-Taftazani's view: it would require observing the general object with a specific face, which is impossible. On the alternative classification presented, a fourth division emerges: words observed universally with meanings in their specificity, which is possible but not actually realized.

Key Terms
الوضع الخاص للخاص (al-wad' al-khas li al-khas): Coinage where both word and meaning are observed in their particularity; the object is not generalized.
الموضوع له (al-mawdu' lahu): The meaning for which a word is coined.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in the specific-specific type of coinage, what can be said about the object (موضوع له)?
A) It must be particular
B) It may be universal or particular
C) It must be universal
D) It is always a proper noun

Key Takeaway
Specific-specific coinage allows for both universal and particular objects, as long as the word and meaning are observed in their particularity.

Types of Wad': General for General and Compounds
Defines general-general coinage and discusses compounds

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe general-general coinage (عام لعام) and explain the standalone coinage of compound expressions.

Core Idea
In general-general coinage, multiple words and meanings are observed under a universal pattern; compounds have their own distinct coinage beyond their parts.

After examining specific-specific coinage, the author moves to a more complex type: general for general. Here, both the words and meanings are considered under a universal aspect. The text also discusses the coinage of compound expressions, arguing that they are not merely derived from their components but have a separate, rule-based coinage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general-general type
The author describes that both the words and meanings are multiple and observed by a universal concept. Example: the pattern of agent nouns (فاعل) is coined for any essence from which an action proceeds.
There is a critique that this view may be artificial because actual usage deals with specific instances.
2
Analyze the coining perspective in this type
The coiner observes a general pattern (e.g., every word on the pattern 'فاعل' from a certain root) and assigns it to a general meaning (any agent). The words are multiple but unified by a rule.
3
Examine the compound coinage issue
The text asserts that compounds (like 'Zaid stands') are coined for compositional meanings within a rule, not simply by combining the coinage of their parts. Those who say compounds have no separate coinage are mistaken.
The example: every compound one of whose parts is a verb and the other an agent is coined to inform that the event is happening by it.
4
Evaluate the counter-argument
The opposing view (that the coinage of single words suffices) is deemed corrupt. The text states that reflection shows the error.

Types of Wad': General for Specific and the Possibility of Specific for General
Defines general-specific coinage and discusses the fourth type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain general-specific coinage (عام لخاص) and evaluate the possibility of specific-general coinage according to Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
General-specific coinage uses a universal concept to coin words for specific individuals; specific-general is impossible due to the nature of observation.

The third type of coinage reverses the pattern: the word is observed under a general concept (e.g., 'that' as a demonstrative), but it is coined for specific individuals. This avoids both metaphor and homonymy. The lesson also discusses a hypothetical fourth type (specific for general) and explains why it cannot occur.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the general-specific type
The word is observed by a general concept (e.g., the concept of 'the one pointed to'), but it is coined for specific individuals each time it is used. Examples: demonstratives, pronouns, relative pronouns, particles.
The text says this type has been explained previously.
2
Understand the justification
By coining for the individuals directly, the word avoids metaphor (استعمال مجازي) because it is used in its literal sense, and it avoids homonymy (اشتراك لفظي) because it is a single coinage applied to many.
3
Refute the universal concept theory
Some scholars (like al-Taftazani) argued that such words are coined for universal concepts and then used for individuals. The text refutes this: if that were the case, using the word for many individuals would require either metaphor (if used for a specific) or homonymy (if many coinages).
The text attributes this view to al-Taftazani.
4
Examine the specific-general possibility
The fourth type (specific for general) would require observing a general object with a specific, particular face, which is impossible (ممتنع). However, if we reverse the perspective from the earlier classification, a fourth division emerges: words observed universally with meanings in their specificity. This is possible but not actually realized.
The text discusses both the impossibility and an alternative formulation.

Here the word is observed under a general concept (e.g., 'that one'), but it is coined to refer to specific individuals. This avoids both metaphor and homonymy, since one coinage covers all individuals. The author refutes the notion that such words are coined for universals. Fourth, the possibility of specific for general (خاص لعام) is discussed via al-Taftazani's view: it would require observing the general object with a specific face, which is impossible. On the alternative classification presented, a fourth division emerges: words observed universally with meanings in their specificity, which is possible but not actually realized.

Key Terms
الوضع العام للخاص (al-wad' al-'amm li al-khas): Coinage where the word is observed by a general concept but coined for specific individuals.
مرآة (mir'at): Mirror; the general concept serves as a means to observe the specific individuals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the specific-for-general coinage (الوضع الخاص للعام) considered impossible?
A) It would require too many coinages
B) It is impossible to observe a general object with a specific face
C) It was never done by Arabs
D) It leads to contradiction

Key Takeaway
General-specific coinage uses a universal concept as a mirror to coin words directly for specific individuals; specific-general is impossible.

Definition of al-Mushtaq (The Derived Word)
Definition of 'mushtaq' (derived word) as a category of singular word, with a note on definitional circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for a singular word to be considered derived (mushtaq) according to Hanafi usul al-fiqh and identify the potential circularity in the definition.

Core Idea
A derived word must agree with its root in both meaning and root letters — but the definition itself risks circularity.

This passage opens the first chapter of the text on singular words (al-mufrad). The author introduces the category of derived words (al-mushtaq) and provides a concise definition. The definition is critical for later discussions on how derivation affects rulings in usul al-fiqh, such as in qiyas (analogical reasoning). As you read, note the two conditions set forth and the comment about circularity, which is a methodological concern in definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The passage defines 'al-mushtaq' (the derived word). Locate the term and note that it is a category of 'al-mufrad' (singular word).
The example given (جرحى وجريح) illustrates derivation between a plural and singular form.
2
Extract the two conditions
The definition states two conditions: (1) agreement with a root (أصل) in meaning, and (2) agreement in the root letters that persist through inflections. Write these down.
These conditions are cumulative; both must be satisfied.
3
Understand the circularity concern
The commentator notes that using 'root' (أصل) in the definition may lead to circularity, because 'root' itself is often defined in terms of derivation. Consider why this matters for precision in definitions.
The text suggests clarifying what 'root' means here — likely the verbal root (مصدر) as used in morphology.
4
Apply to an example
Test the definition on a common derived word like 'qātil' (killer). It agrees with the root 'qatala' in meaning (killing) and root letters (q-t-l). Confirm it is mushtaq.
This exercise reinforces the conditions and prepares you for later istinbat tasks.

Definition and Divisions of Mushtaqq (Derived Nouns)
Definition and classification of mushtaqq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi definition of mushtaqq and enumerate the 15 forms of derivation from its original word.

Core Idea
Mushtaqq requires a change in the original word; changes give 15 forms.

In Hanafi usul, precise understanding of derived words is vital for interpreting legal texts. This passage defines mushtaqq and systematically classifies the types of changes that produce it, forming a complete typology of 15 derivation forms.

Guided Path
1
State the definition of mushtaqq
Read the first part: mushtaqq is taken from something (its original word, mabda'), and it necessarily involves a change (taghyir) in that original word.
2
Identify simple changes
The change can be either in vowel movement (haraka) or in a letter (harf), and each can be by increase (ziyada) or decrease (nuqsan). This gives 4 simple forms.
Movement-only changes: e.g., changing the vowel pattern. Letter-only changes: adding or removing a letter.
3
Understand compound changes
When change involves more than one of the above, we have compositions (tarkib). Twofold (thina'): combining any two simple changes (there are 6 such combos). Threefold (thulath): combining any three (4 combos). Fourfold (ruba'): all four together (1 combo).
The totals: 4 simple + 6 twofold + 4 threefold + 1 fourfold = 15.
4
Verify the total
Confirm that all 15 forms cover every possible systematic change. The passage states 'it amounts to 15 as we have shown'.
This classification is exhaustive for the Hanafi definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يؤخذ منه شيء لا ما يؤخذ منه مشتق أو يجعل التعريف لفظياً وهو أولى ولا بد) للمشتق (من تغيير ما في المبدأ الأصل (إما بحركة) فقط (أو بحرف فقط، وكل منهما إما (بزيادة أو نقصان) فهذه أربعة (والتركيب) أي التغيير المركب مما فوق الواحد حال كون التركيب (ثناء) أخذ التغيير بزيادة الحركة مع الثلاثة الباقية، وبنقصان الحركة مع وهذه ستة حاصلة من الأخيرين وبزيادة الحرف مع الأخير (و) حال كون التركيب (ثلاث) وهذه أربعة حاصلة الأول من أخذ مع اثنين اثنين من البواقي (و) حال كون التركيب (رباع) وهذا واحد وهو حاصل من اجتماع المفردات كلها ( يرتقي إلى خمسة عشر كما بينا والأمثلة واضحة
It is taken from something, not what is taken from it as derived, or the definition is verbal and it is better and necessary. For the derived noun, there must be a change in the original word (either by a movement only or by a letter only, and each either by increase or decrease) so these are four. And the composition (i.e., combined change of more than one) when the composition is twofold: taking change by increase of movement with the remaining three, and by decrease of movement with the last two, and by increase of the letter with the last two makes six. And when the composition is threefold: these are four, obtained by taking two with two of the rest. And when the composition is fourfold: this is one, obtained from combining all the singles. It amounts to fifteen as we have shown, and the examples are clear.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of mushtaqq (derived noun) in usul al-fiqh. The author explains that a mushtaqq is derived from its original word (mabda') by means of a change (taghyir), which can be either a change in vowel movement (haraka) or in a letter (harf), and each can be by addition or subtraction. These four simple changes combine into compound changes: two-fold (thina'), three-fold (thulath), and four-fold (ruba'), totaling 15 possible forms. This is the Hanafi definition which emphasizes the necessity of change in the original word for derivation. The examples are considered obvious.

Key Terms
مشتق (mushtaqq): A derived noun; a word taken from another (its original) with a change in form (movement or letter, increase or decrease).
تغيير (taghyir): Change in the original word's form, either in vowel movement or in a consonant letter, by increase or decrease.
مبدأ (mabda'): The original word from which the mushtaqq is derived, e.g., the root verb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 4
B) 15
C) 6
D) 10

Key Takeaway
Mushtaqq is defined by a change in its original word, and the exhaustive classification yields 15 derivation forms.

Regular vs Irregular Derived Nouns (Mutarrid & Ghayr Mutarrid)
Distinguishing regular vs irregular derived nouns

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mutarrid and ghayr mutarrid mushtaqq and explain how the presence of the original meaning determines regularity.

Core Idea
A mushtaqq is regular if the original meaning is always present; irregular if it is only a condition for the name.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, derived nouns (mushtaqqat) are classified by how they relate to their original meanings. Some terms always carry their root meaning when used, while others carry it as a condition but not always actually present. This distinction affects how we interpret legal texts and derive rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Read the passage and find the two categories of mushtaqq: mutarrid (regular) and ghayr mutarrid (irregular). Note the examples given: fa'il (active participle) for mutarrid, and qarurah (bottle) for ghayr mutarrid.
2
Analyze the condition for mutarrid
Understand that a mutarrid mushtaqq requires the original meaning (al-ma'na al-asl) to be included in the name itself. Whenever the original meaning exists, the derived term applies. For example, 'fa'il' applies to anyone performing the action.
3
Analyze ghayr mutarrid
For ghayr mutarrid, the original meaning is not included in the name but is a condition for its validity. That is, the term originated from the meaning, but the meaning does not need to be present at the time of utterance. Example: a broken bottle is still called qarurah even if it no longer holds liquid.
The text states that ghayr mutarrid is considered mushtaqq only by way of origin (i'tibar al-asl).
4
Apply to usul al-fiqh
Reflect on how this classification impacts legal interpretation. For instance, when a Quranic verse uses an active participle (mutarrid), it indicates that the action is ongoing. But for terms like 'qarurah', the original meaning is not necessarily present, so the ruling may depend on the condition of origin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو أي المشتق مطرد كاسم الفاعل) وغيره مما اندرج تحت ضابطة كلية (وغيره) أي غير مطرد (كالقارورة) وغيرها مما لا يندرج تحت ضابطة ( والفرق بينهما أن (المعنى الأصل (إما داخل في التسمية) فكل ما يوجد فيه المعنى يوجد فيه المشتق فيطرد (أو) ليس داخلاً فيه بل (شرط صحة التسمية) بهذا الاسم فقط فلا يطرد (وهو) أي غير المطرد (لا) يكون مشتقاً إلا باعتبار الأصل) وهو ظاهر فإن وجود المشتق منه غير شرط هناك في صحة إطلاقه حتى يقال للقارورة المنكسرة قارورة.
And the derived noun is either mutarrid, like the active participle and others that fall under a universal rule, or ghayr mutarrid, like 'qarurah' and others that do not fall under a rule. The difference between them is that the original meaning is either included in the name, so whenever the meaning is found, the derived noun is found, and it is mutarrid; or it is not included but rather a condition for the validity of the name, so it is not mutarrid. And ghayr mutarrid is not a derived noun except by way of origin, and that is apparent, for the existence of what it is derived from is not a condition for the validity of its application, such that a broken bottle is called a bottle.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage explains the difference between mutarrid (regular) and ghayr mutarrid (irregular) derived nouns. A mutarrid mushtaqq is one where the original meaning (al-ma'na al-asl) is included in the name, so whenever the meaning is present, the derived term applies. For example, 'fa'il' (doer) applies to anyone who performs the action. A ghayr mutarrid mushtaqq is where the original meaning is not included but is a condition for the name. For example, 'qarurah' (bottle) is called a bottle even if it is broken, as long as it was originally a bottle. Thus, ghayr mutarrid is considered mushtaqq only by way of origin (i'tibar al-asl), not by the actual presence of the original meaning.

Key Terms
مطرد (mutarrid): A regular mushtaqq where the original meaning is always present when the term is used, e.g., fa'il for doer.
غير مطرد (ghayr mutarrid): An irregular mushtaqq where the original meaning is not necessarily present, but is a condition for the name, e.g., qarurah for bottle.
المعنى الأصل (al-ma'na al-asl): The original meaning from which the derived noun (mushtaqq) is taken.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Mutarrid requires the original meaning to be included in the name; ghayr mutarrid requires it only as a condition.
C) Mutarrid terms are verbs, ghayr mutarrid are nouns.
D) Both categories require the original meaning to be present at all times.

Key Takeaway
A derived noun is regular (mutarrid) if its original meaning is always present; irregular (ghayr mutarrid) if the original meaning is only a condition for the name, not a requirement for its application.

The Reality of Derived Nouns for Past Actions
Dispute on linguistic reality of derived nouns for past actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on the use of derived nouns for past actions and evaluate the main argument from customary contradiction.

Core Idea
Using a derived noun for a past action is metaphorical, not literal, because it would otherwise dissolve customary contradictions.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahmut addresses a foundational linguistic dispute in usul al-fiqh: when we say 'Zaid is a striker' based on a past strike, are we speaking literally or metaphorically? The Hanafi school, represented here, argues that it must be metaphorical. The key proof is a rational argument from common linguistic practice: if it were literal, then two statements that are normally contradictory could both be true, which is unacceptable. This lesson unpacks the dispute, the arguments, and the resolution that serves as a precedent for how derived nouns function in the law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the points of agreement
Note that all scholars agree: using a derived noun for a present action is literal; for a future action is metaphorical. This sets the boundaries of the dispute.
The disagreement is only about past actions.
2
State the three positions on past actions
The text presents: (1) metaphorical absolutely (al-mukhtar), (2) literal absolutely (Mu'tazila), (3) detailed view (metaphorical for persistent actions, literal for non-persistent).
The author endorses the first.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof: customary contradiction
If 'Zaid is standing' could be literal for a past standing, then 'Zaid is standing' and 'Zaid is not standing' could both be true (one about past, one about present). But custom treats these as contradictory, so the premise fails.
This is a dalil aqli based on linguistic practice.
4
Evaluate the opposing arguments and Hanafi responses
The Mu'tazila cite consensus on 'striker yesterday' and the presumption of literalness. The Hanafi response: consensus on the phrase's validity does not prove literalness; it may be metaphorical. The presumption is overridden by the stronger evidence of contradiction.
Also consider the arguments from negation and from the sleeping believer example.
5
Extract the usul principle
This dispute illustrates that derived nouns (mushtaqqat) are analyzed for their temporal reference in legal language. The rule: a derived noun is literal only when the attribute is currently present; past attribution is metaphorical.
This principle has implications for interpreting legal texts and testimonies.

Source Text

نسلم أن ذلك الانعدام وعدم الحال يستلزم الإطلاق على غير الموضوع (له إذا أطلق على الذي كان أبيض وفي الحال أسود وإنما يلزم ذلك لو كان الاتحاد المعتبر في الوضع الاتحاد في الحال وليس الأمر كذلك عند الخصم (بل) الإطلاق الحقيقي يقتضي الاتحاد مطلقاً في الماضي كان أم في الحال فتدبر، القائلون بكونه حقيقة في الماضي مطلقاً قالوا) أولاً إطباق أهل اللغة) واقع على صحة ضارب أمس والأصل في الإطلاق (الحقيقة) وعورض بإطباقهم على صحة ضارب غداً) والأصل الحقيقة فيلزم أن يكون حقيقة في المستقبل، ولا يخفى أنه نقض لا معارضة، كيف ولم يثبت منه نقيض مدعي الخصم وكيفما كان فقد أجيب عنه بأن الأصالة إنما تكون حجة لو لم يردها الأقوى، وههنا الإجماع قد وقع على مجازيته للمباشر في الاستقبال بخلاف المستعمل في الماضي فإنه مختلف فيه فافهم قال المصنف : (وهذا النقض (لا يتم على ابن سينا) قد عرفت أنه لم يدع أنه حقيقة ولا كلام لنا معه (والحل أن الكلام في المشتق المطلق عن القيد وضارب أمس مقيد وأن صدق المقيد لغة لا يستلزم صدق المطلق كذلك) حتى يلزم صدق ضارب أمس ضارب مطلقاً ألا ترى أن قولك : زيد معدوم النظير، يلزم من صدقه صدق المطلق عقلاً وهو المعدوم أعم من أن يكون نفسه أو نظيره أو ضده (وأما في العرف فلا يقال : زيد معدوم) فإنه يفهم منه عرفاً أنه معدوم بنفسه فافهم (و) قالوا (ثانياً : إطلاق المؤمن) ثابت لغة وعرفاً لنائم، فإنه مؤمن إجماعاً) أن الإيمان غير حاصل له في الحال، فيصح مع الإطلاق باعتبار الماضي ( ويعارض) بامتناع) إطلاق (كافر) على رجل مؤمن (لكفر تقدم وإلا) يمتنع ذلك لزم أن يكون أكابر (الصحابة رضوان الله تعالى عليهم الذين هم أكابر المؤمنين بعد
The third article on linguistic principles. For His essence by itself discloses things to Him, and He does not need another thing to stand in for the disclosure of things, as we need something additional to our essence in the disclosure of things. The derived-from (mushaqq minhu) for these derived nouns is the first relative meaning subsisting in His essence, and it is not necessary for the truth of the derived noun that the principle subsist in a conjoined subsistence. Do you not see how derived nouns of relative matters are true of the entities from which the relatives are abstracted? And then they do not say that the derived noun is true without the principle subsisting. And this is more appropriate than what was said that what is considered in the truth of derived nouns is the general subsistence, meaning negation of subsistence by another, and this is realized. For it may be correct, but the expressions of the likes of al-Zamakhshari and al-Sakkaki, with their rigidity in their abominable positions of Mu'tazilism, do not support it. And the used [terms]? Issue: That the use of the derived noun like 'striker' for the one directly performing striking in the present is literal, by agreement. If you say: How is this consensus with their consensus that derived nouns do not indicate time by linguistic assignment? I say: It is valid that perhaps that consensus is about its assignment individually, and this is in the case of composition to convey meaning, so reflect. And the use of the derived noun with consideration of the future, i.e., applying it to what is not currently doing but will do in the future, is metaphorical by agreement, as they said. I say: There is a consideration regarding the transmission of agreement, for Ibn Sina and his followers held in deriving the meaning of the proposition that the meaning of 'every white' is everything of which 'white' is true in actuality in one of the three times, so there is no agreement. The answer: That his intention is not to explain language, but rather to explain that the proposition considered in logic and philosophy is that which considers the truth of the title actually, and even if it is conceded that he considered this in language, what evidence is in his consideration? For he is not among the people of this discipline, so the consensus of the people of this field does not harm. If you say: What do you do with the statement of his followers in preferring his consideration over that of al-Farabi that it is in agreement with language and custom, and among them are those who are from the people of this discipline? I say: They meant that it is close to custom and language; some of them stated it explicitly, so reflect. As for the use of the derived noun with consideration of the past on the one who performed in the past but is not currently performing, it is said—and it is the most correct, the chosen view—metaphorical absolutely, whether its remaining is possible like the lasting accidents of blackness and whiteness or its remaining is not possible like the fluid accidents like motion and the like. And it is said literal absolutely, whether its remaining is possible or not, and that is the school of Abu Ali al-Juba'i and his son. And it is said with detail: between what can remain—they said its use with consideration of the past is metaphorical—and other—they said literal. For us: the customary contradiction between our saying 'Zaid is standing' and 'Zaid is not standing'—for the people of custom consider these two statements contradictory and mutually false, and if it were valid for the past literally as they claim, it would be valid for the present by agreement, so they would combine in literalness since there is no contradiction between negating standing in one time like the present and affirming it in another like the past, so the contradiction would be invalid. This is a fallacy, so understand. And they argued for the chosen view that negation is valid, i.e., it is valid to negate the derived noun from the original doer in the past, and the validity of negation is among the signs of metaphor. As for the validity of negation, it is because it is said: 'He is not a writer in the present' so the use is truthful, and he is not a writer absolutely. In Fawatih al-Rahmut sharh Musallam al-Thubut, part one. Union: And it is prevented that the negation is valid linguistically, even if it is valid rationally by virtue of the implication of the restricted for the absolute. But linguistically, the restricted does not imply the absolute. Do you not see that it is said 'the peerless' and it is not said 'the nonexistent'? And what is among the signs of metaphor is linguistic validity. Then the argument is refuted by the fact that it is true of the current doer that he is not a writer in the past, so he is not a writer absolutely, and the validity of negation is among the signs of metaphor, so the use of the current doer would be metaphorical. If the arguer had folded the talk of the absolute and restricted and laid out the discussion as: We have surveyed linguistic uses and found the negation of derived nouns from the past doer and the validity of negation customarily to be among the signs of metaphor, the discussion would be complete and the disturbance repelled. And denying the validity of this survey may be obstinacy, so reflect. And he also argued that if it were valid for the past literally, it would be valid for the future because of the realization of existence in general that is considered on this estimation. And it is answered that existence in general is not sufficient, rather the common matter between past and present is required, which is the coming of the meaning of the origin in the world of action, and this is absent in the future, so we do not accept the entailment. And he argued that if a white object becomes black, it is called black literally due to its real existential union with black, and the concept of white has been negated from it actually. So applying 'white' to what became black is applying to what it was not assigned to, because it is in reality opposed to it. I say: The negation of whiteness in the present when it becomes black does not negate the union, i.e., the union of the object that became black with whiteness in the past. So we do not accept that that negation and present absence entails applying to what it is not assigned for if it is applied to what was white and is now black. It would only entail that if the union considered in assignment is union in the present, and that is not the case according to the opponent. Rather, real application requires absolute union, whether in the past or present, so reflect. Those who say it is literal in the past absolutely said: First, the agreement of the people of language is upon the validity of 'striker yesterday', and the origin in usage is literalness. And it is objected by their agreement upon the validity of 'striker tomorrow', and the origin is literalness, so it would follow that it is literal in the future, and it is not hidden that this is a refutation, not an objection. How, and the opposite of the claimant's claim is not established from it? However it may be, it was answered that the principle is only a proof if the stronger does not override it, and here consensus has occurred on its metaphoricality for the future doer, unlike the use in the past, for it is disputed. So understand. The author said: This refutation is not valid against Ibn Sina; you have known that he did not claim it is literal, and we have no argument with him. And the solution is that the discussion is about the derived noun unrestricted from the time restriction, and 'striker yesterday' is restricted, and the truth of the restricted linguistically does not entail the truth of the unrestricted likewise, such that the truth of 'striker yesterday' would entail being a striker absolutely. Do you not see that your saying 'Zaid is peerless' entails from its truth the truth of the absolute rationally, i.e., the nonexistent, which is more general whether it be himself, his peer, or his opposite? But customarily it is not said 'Zaid is nonexistent', because it would be understood customarily that he is nonexistent in himself. So understand. And they said secondly: The use of 'believer' is established linguistically and customarily for a sleeping person, for he is a believer by consensus, since faith is not present in him at the time. So the use with consideration of the past is valid. And it is objected by the impossibility of applying 'disbeliever' to a believing man due to past disbelief, otherwise if it were not impossible, then the great Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, who are the great believers after [the Prophet] would be...
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a detailed dispute in usul al-fiqh regarding the linguistic status of derived nouns (mushtaqqat) when predicated of a subject who performed the action in the past. The author first establishes the points of agreement: all scholars agree that using a derived noun like 'striker' for someone currently striking is literal (haqiqah), and that using it for someone who will strike in the future is metaphorical (majaz). The central dispute is about the past tense: is calling someone 'striker' based on a past action literal or metaphorical? The preferred view (al-mukhtar) is that it is metaphorical absolutely, whether the action's effect can persist (like whiteness) or not (like motion). A minority holds it literal absolutely, and another group distinguishes between persistent and non-persistent attributes. The main argument for the preferred view is that if it were literal for the past, then the statement 'Zaid is standing' and 'Zaid is not standing' would not be contradictory, since one could refer to past standing and present non-standing simultaneously, which contradicts customary understanding. This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) based on daily linguistic practice. The author also addresses objections, including the counter-argument that if negation is a sign of metaphor, then calling someone currently striking 'not a writer' would also be metaphorical, but this is refuted by noting that negation of present action is not linguistically valid in the same way. The discussion also critiques Ibn Sina's logical approach, distinguishing between linguistic and philosophical considerations. Ultimately, the Hanafi position leans towards metaphorical usage for the past, as this preserves the natural contradiction that exists in common speech.

Key Terms
مشتق (mushtaqq): A derived noun derived from a root meaning, e.g., ضارب from ضرب, indicating an attribute of the subject
حقيقة (haqiqah): Literal usage, where the word is used for what it was originally assigned to
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical usage, where the word is used for something other than its original assignment due to a relation
تكاذب عرفي (takadhib 'urfi): Customary contradiction: two statements that are considered mutually contradictory in everyday language
مطلق (mutlaq): Unrestricted; a term without a time qualifier

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the preferred view (al-mukhtar) regarding the use of a derived noun for someone who performed the action in the past?
A) Literal, absolutely
B) Metaphorical, but only if the action cannot persist
C) Metaphorical, absolutely
D) Literal if the action can persist, metaphorical otherwise

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that using a derived noun for a past action is metaphorical, not literal, to preserve the logical consistency of everyday language.

15 min
Are Derived Nouns Literal for the Past? The Hanafi Dispute
Debating whether derived nouns are literal when applied to past instances

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the arguments for and against the position that derived nouns (mushtaqq) are literal (haqiqah) when applied to past instances, and identify the Hanafi preferred view.

Core Idea
The consensus on 'ضارب أمس' supports literal usage for the past, but the dispute hinges on the nature of union (اتحاد).

This lesson examines a foundational usul al-fiqh dispute: when we call someone a 'writer' based on an action that occurred in the past, is that usage literal or metaphorical? The Hanafi school argues for literal usage, while some scholars consider it metaphorical. We will explore the key proofs, including linguistic consensus, examples from the Qur'an, and rational arguments, to understand the Hanafi position and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Main Claim and Opposing Views
First, understand the core issue: Are derived nouns (like 'striker', 'writer', 'believer') literal when used for someone who performed the action in the past but does not currently do it? The Hanafi position is that they are literal; the opposing view holds they are metaphorical.
2
Examine the First Dalil: Consensus on 'ضارب أمس'
The Hanafis cite the consensus of linguists that saying 'ضارب أمس' (striker yesterday) is correct, and the principle of linguistic usage is literal (haqiqah). The objection that 'ضارب غد' (striker tomorrow) is also correct is dismissed as a refutation (naqd) not a valid counter, because there is consensus that the future usage is metaphorical.
Note that the principle of asalah al-haqiqah applies unless overridden.
3
Analyse the Argument from 'مؤمن' for a Sleeping Person
The Hanafis argue that a sleeping person is called 'believer' (مؤمن) even though faith is not actively present, proving literal use based on past faith. The counter that we cannot call a believer 'disbeliever' based on past disbelief is answered by distinguishing a religious prohibition from linguistic reality.
The philosophical explanation of faith in the 'treasury' is included to defend the literal usage.
4
Consider the Objection from Transient Accidents
Opponents argue that terms like 'speaker' (متكلم) cannot be literal because speaking is a transient action that does not persist. The Hanafis respond by invoking customary immediacy (الحال العرفي): we consider the action as ongoing in a customary sense, not in a real moment.
This shows the importance of 'urf in linguistic interpretation.
5
Summarise the Hanafi Position and the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The Hanafis hold that المشتق حقيقة في الماضي مطلقاً. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is the permissibility of applying derived nouns to those who no longer possess the attribute, which affects interpretation of Quranic descriptions and legal names.
This position is relied upon in Hanafi usul.

On the union. (And it prevents) the correctness of negation (linguistically), even if it is correct rationally by virtue of the qualified implying the absolute, but linguistically there is no implication of the absolute by the qualified. Do you not see that it is said 'someone without peer' but it is not said 'someone non-existent'? And that which is a sign of metaphor is linguistic correctness. Then the evidence is refuted by the fact that it is true of the immediate doer in the present that he is not a writer in the past, so he is not a writer absolutely, and the correctness of negation is a sign of metaphor, so applying (the term) to the immediate doer is metaphorical. If the arguer had omitted the discussion of absolute and qualified and formulated the argument as: we have surveyed linguistic usages and found that negating derived nouns from the immediate doer in the past and the customary correctness of negation are signs of metaphor, then the argument would be complete and the objection repelled. Denying the correctness of this survey is perhaps obstinacy, so ponder. (And) he also argued that if it were literal for the past, it would be literal for the future due to the realization of existence in general, considered on this estimation. (And it is answered) that existence in general is not sufficient; rather, the common factor between past and present is required: the emergence of the original meaning in the realm of action. This is lacking in the future, so we do not accept the implication. (And) he argued that if a white body becomes black, it is truly called black due to its unity (i.e., black's unity with it in actual existence) truly, and the concept of white has ceased to exist in it truly, so applying (the term) white to it (i.e., what has become black) is applying to something other than what it was coined for, because it is contrary to it truly. I say: the cessation of white (in the present when it becomes black) does not negate the union, i.e., the union of the body that became black with white in the past. So we do not accept that that cessation and non-existence in the present necessitates application to other than what it was coined for when it is applied to what was white and is now black. That would only be necessary if the union considered in coining were union in the present, and that is not the case according to the opponent. Rather, true application requires union absolutely, whether in the past or present, so ponder. Those who hold that it is truly for the past absolutely said: Firstly, the consensus of language scholars is on the correctness of 'striker yesterday', and the principle in usage is reality. It is countered by their consensus on the correctness of 'striker tomorrow', and the principle is reality, so it would follow that it is true for the future. It is clear that this is a refutation, not a counter-argument. How, when the opponent's claim has not been disproven? In any case, it is answered that the principle is only authoritative if not overridden by something stronger, and here there is consensus that it is metaphorical for the immediate doer in the future, unlike its use for the past, which is disputed, so understand. The author said: This refutation does not apply to Ibn Sina; you know that he did not claim that it is true, and we have no argument with him. And the solution is that the discussion is about the derived noun free from restriction, and 'striker yesterday' is restricted. The truth of the restricted linguistically does not entail the truth of the unrestricted likewise, so that the truth of 'striker yesterday' would entail the truth of 'striker' absolutely. Do you not see that your statement 'Zayd is peerless in his counterpart' entails the truth of the absolute intellectually, which is 'non-existent' as a general notion, whether it be himself, his counterpart, or his opposite? (But) in customary usage, one does not say 'Zayd is non-existent', because customarily it is understood as non-existent in himself, so understand. (And) they said, secondly: The application of 'believer' is established linguistically and customarily to a sleeping person, for he is a believer by consensus, even though faith is not present in him at the moment, so it is correct with application based on the past. (And it is countered) by the prohibition of applying 'disbeliever' to a believing man for a past disbelief; otherwise, it would follow that the great Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, who are the greatest believers after the prophets, peace be upon them, would be true disbelievers—God forbid. (And it may be said) in response to the counter that this objectionable application is linguistically permissible, and what prevents it is religious, so it is not permissible religiously to maintain the required respect. It is clear that this backfires on the original evidence, for the preventer of negating faith from a sleeping person is religious. (And the solution is) that faith is more general than being present in the faculty of perception or the treasury. The sleeper's faith is present in his treasury. The verification is that we have two states: a state of witnessing the known and a state of heedlessness of it. But due to its strength, whenever he pays attention, he witnesses and is certain of it without needing to consider evidence, and awakening occurs by alerting. Faith is more general than actual witnessing. Due to the strength of this kind of power, the sleeper is a believer because the second part exists. The author expressed the first as being in the perceptive faculty, and the second as being in the treasury, based on what philosophers say: when witnessing, the known is present in the perceptive faculty, and in the other state called by them forgetfulness, it leaves the perceptive faculty and resides in the treasury. If you understand this, then it does not matter that the discussion is about linguistic usages, whose basis is what the general public understands on the surface, while the treasury and perceptive faculty are philosophical subtleties. This (and it may be answered) in Sharh al-Mukhtasar by restricting the claim to active participles that imply temporality, so their application to the past is metaphorical, as opposed to those that imply permanence, for they are true for the past as well, and 'believer' is of this type. This has an apparent problem, for there is no difference between them; we only know that language operates under a single rule, for the requirement of being described by the basis exists in both temporals and permanent. It is not objected that this is a linguistic analogy, for similar to 'knowing', 'able', and 'owner' are also permanent in meaning, and therefore applied to Allah Almighty. Then it would follow that they are true for the past by agreement. This (and) they said, thirdly: It would necessitate that 'speaker' and similar transient accidents are metaphorical, for they do not exist in the present moment, so it cannot be applied to anything truly. (And it is answered) that what is considered is customary immediacy in the customary present, not immediacy in the real present which is a moment separating past and future, as it is said: So-and-so is writing the Quran and walking from Mecca to Medina, meaning by the present parts from the past and the future connected without a break that customarily counts as abandonment and turning away. This is besides the fact that your evidence does not necessitate the absence of the condition absolutely for active participles, but only for what cannot persist, so the evidence is insufficient for the claim, so understand.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a discussion (usul_dispute) on whether derived nouns (المشتقات) are literal (حقيقة) when applied to past instances. The text presents several arguments for the position that they are literal for the past, along with counterarguments and responses. The first argument refutes the objection based on the linguistic correctness of negation; it shows that صحة النفي does not necessarily imply metaphor because the negation is valid only when the term is absolute, not restricted. The second argument deals with a body turning from white to black, arguing that applying 'white' after it has become black would be metaphorical, but the response maintains that the union in the past is sufficient for literal application. The third argument appeals to the consensus of linguists on 'ضارب أمس', and the counter that 'ضارب غد' is also accepted is dismissed as a refutation, not a genuine counter, because there is consensus that it is metaphorical for the future. The fourth argument involves 'مؤمن' for a sleeping person; the counter about 'كافر' is answered by distinguishing that the preventer is religious, not linguistic, and then a philosophical explanation about faith being in the 'treasury' is given. Finally, the passage addresses the objection about transient actions like 'speaking' by appealing to customary immediacy. The text ultimately defends the view that المشتق is حقيقة in the past, using both linguistic and rational proofs.

Key Terms
مشتق (mushtaqq): كل اسم أخذ من لفظ غير لفظه بدلالة على معنى وذات مقارنة له
حقيقة (haqiqah): اللفظ المستعمل فيما وضع له في اللغة
مجاز (majaz): اللفظ المستعمل في غير ما وضع له لعلاقة مع قرينة مانعة من إرادة المعنى الحقيقي
اتحاد (ittihad): الوحدة بين الموصوف والصفة في الوجود
إطباق أهل اللغة (itbaq ahl al-lughah): إجماع اللغويين على صحة استعمال لفظ في معنى
الحال العرفي (al-hal al-'urfi): الزمن الذي يعتبره العرف مستمراً، وليس الآن الحقيقي الفاصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) إطباقهم على صحة ضارب أمس

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that derived nouns are literal for the past, based on the consensus of linguists and the principle of asalah al-haqiqah, with the practical effect that attributes can be applied to individuals who once possessed them.

Deriving Active and Passive Participles Based on Real vs Relational Attributes
Rule on derivation of active and passive participles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul rule that the active participle is derived only from a real attribute subsisting in the subject, and distinguish it from the passive participle derived from a relational attribute.

Core Idea
The active participle requires the action as a real quality of the doer; the passive participle derives from a relational connection.

This passage presents a foundational rule in Hanafi usul al-fiqh about the derivation of active and passive participles. The rule hinges on whether the action is a real attribute (صفة حقيقية) of the subject or merely a relational attribution. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing linguistic evidence in legal texts, as it affects how derived nouns (like active/passive participles) are interpreted. The rule also reflects the Hanafi commitment to ontological precision in language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Derivations
Locate the rule regarding اسم الفاعل and اسم المفعول. Note that the active participle cannot be derived when the action belongs to another, while the passive participle can.
2
Understand the Ontological Basis
Recognize that the action (e.g., ضرب) is a real quality that subsists only in the agent. Why can't it subsist in two subjects? Because one accident cannot inhere in two substances.
This is based on the Aristotelian substance-accident ontology accepted in Hanafi usul.
3
Relational vs Real Attribute
For the object, the attribute 'being-struck' is not a real quality but a relational one (نسبة بالعرض). The derivation of اسم المفعول uses this relational aspect.
The passive participle is derived from the active infinitive through relational attribution.
4
Apply to an Example
Take 'Zaid struck Amr'. The active participle 'ضارب' is derived from Zaid; the passive participle 'مضروب' from Amr. The real quality (striking) is in Zaid only.
This illustrates the rule clearly.
5
Counter a Potential Objection
The passive infinitive (المصدر المجهول) is not a real quality; it is attributive. Yet derivation from it is still valid. This shows the distinction between real and relational attributes.
The text says 'لا يخفى' to emphasize clarity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة ( لا يشتق اسم الفاعل لشيء والفعل قائم بغيره وأما اسم المفعول فيجوز) اشتقاقه والفعل المبدأ قائم بغيره بناء على أن الضرب صفة حقيقية واحدة قائمة بالفاعل) فقط دون المفعول، وإلا يلزم قيام عرض واحد بموضوعين (و) إنما (له نسبة بالعرض إلى المفعول وهي المضروبية، وليست المضروبية (صفة حقيقية مغايرة له فمضروبية عمرو ليست إلا ضرب زيد له لا غير فهذه الصفة الحقيقية قائمة بزيد واشتق المضروب منها لعمرو (فتدبر) ولا يخفى على من له أدنى تدبر أن غاية ما لزم أن المصدر المجهول ليس صفة حقيقية، بل اعتبارية ناشئة عن المصدر المعلوم وهذا لا ينافي الاشتقاق باعتباره فإنه كما يجوز الاشتقاق من
A rule: The active participle is not derived for something when the action is performed by another, but the passive participle may be derived, and the action originates from another; this is based on the fact that striking is a single real quality residing in the agent only, not in the object, otherwise a single accident would subsist in two subjects. It has only a relational connection to the object, which is being-struck, and being-struck is not a real quality separate from it, so Amr's being-struck is nothing other than Zaid's striking him. So this real quality resides in Zaid, and 'the struck one' is derived from it for Amr. So ponder. It is not hidden from anyone with minimal reflection that the utmost that follows is that the passive infinitive is not a real quality, but an attributive one arising from the active infinitive, and this does not contradict derivation from it, for just as derivation is permitted from...
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage states a rule (usul_rule) regarding the derivation of active and passive participles. It asserts that the active participle (اسم الفاعل) cannot be derived for someone who is not the actual doer of the action, because the action (like 'striking') is a real attribute that inheres in the doer alone. For example, if Zaid strikes Amr, the active participle 'ضارب' (striker) is derived from the real quality of striking that exists in Zaid. On the other hand, the passive participle (اسم المفعول) can be derived for Amr, who is the object, because 'مضروب' (struck) is derived not from a real quality in Amr, but from the relational aspect—the being-struck (المضروبية)—which is merely the action of Zaid in relation to him. The text emphasizes that the passive infinitive (المصدر المجهول) is not a real attribute but an attributive one, yet this does not prevent derivation. The passage highlights the distinction between real qualities and relational ones in the context of derivation.

Key Terms
اسم الفاعل (ism al-fāʿil): اسم يدل على من قام به الفعل
اسم المفعول (ism al-mafʿūl): اسم يدل على من وقع عليه الفعل
صفة حقيقية (ṣifa ḥaqīqiyya): صفة قائمة بذات الموصوف حقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
لماذا لا يشتق اسم الفاعل للشيء والفعل قائم بغيره؟
A) لأن الفعل صفة حقيقية لا تقوم إلا بفاعله
B) لأن الفعل صفة اعتبارية لا تقبل الاشتقاق
C) لأن اسم الفاعل لا يشتق إلا من المصدر المجهول
D) لأن المفعول به يمنع الاشتقاق

Key Takeaway
Active participle derivation requires the action as a real attribute of the doer; passive participle derivation uses relational attribution, not a real attribute.

Derived Nouns Indicate General Essence
Linguistic analysis of derived nouns within Hanafi usul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why derived nouns (mushtaqqat) indicate a general essence rather than a specific essence, and identify the key argument that prevents deriving a specific substance from such terms.

Core Idea
Derived nouns indicate an absolute essence qualified by an attribute, not a specific substance.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the precise meaning of derived terms like 'al-aswad' (the black) is fundamental for interpreting legal texts that use such descriptors. This passage argues that these terms denote a general essence (any entity) described by the attribute, not a specific type of entity like a body. Understanding this position clarifies why the Hanafi school treats predications such as 'al-aswad is a body' as non-necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claim
The passage states that derived nouns like 'al-aswad' indicate a general essence (dhāt muṭlaqa) qualified by the attribute (e.g., blackness). Note that the claim distinguishes between 'general essence' and 'specific essence' (e.g., a body).
The term 'mushtaqq' refers to any derived noun (e.g., active participle, passive participle).
2
State the Opposing View
The opposing view holds that derived nouns indicate a specific essence – for example, 'al-aswad' would necessarily refer to a body that is black. The passage calls this the 'specific essence' interpretation.
This view would make the derived term carry more semantic content than the attribute alone.
3
Trace the Consequence
If the opposed view were true, then saying 'al-aswad' would entail 'it is a body' because body would be part of the essence. The passage argues that this would make 'body' an essential property (dhātī) of 'al-aswad', which should be self-evident.
The logical consequence is that the predication 'al-aswad is a body' would be analytically true.
4
Analyze the Essentiality Argument
The author responds that an essential property is only self-evident when the whole is considered in detail (tafsīl). However, 'al-aswad' is understood as a whole (mujmal) without such analysis, so the essentiality of body is not apparent. Therefore, the opposing view fails.
This is a subtle point about the level of analysis required for self-evidence.
5
Consider the Opponent's Rebuttal
The commentator (from Al-Hashiya) notes an ambiguity: the opponent could deny the falsehood of the necessary consequence. The argument thus relies on the premise that the whole is not considered in detail, which the opponent might contest.
This shows the debate is not settled purely by logic; it depends on acceptance of the Hanafi analysis of meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة الأسود ونحوه من المشتقات يدل على ذات ما متصفة بالسواد مثلاً) أن المشتقات يعني تدل على مطلق الذات (لا) على خصوصية الذات من كونه جسماً أو غيره وإلا) أي وإن لم يكن كذلك بل دل على خصوصية الذات لما أفاد قولنا : الأسود) جسم لأن الذاتي بين الثبوت لما هو) أي الذاتي ذاتي (له والجسم على هذا التقدير صار ذاتياً للأسود لدخوله فيه (وفيه أنه إنما يكون أي الذاتي (بينا) ثبوته (لو لوحظ الكل مفصلاً) وهو ممنوع ههنا، فالأسود، المعقول ) مجملاً ليس ثبوت ذاتياً بينا له قال في «الحاشية»: ولا يبعد أن يقرر هكذا لو كان خصوص الجسم داخلاً في الأسود لكان حمله عليه في مرتبة التفصيل غير مفيد، هذا لكن لا يخلو عن نوع شبهة، فإن للخصم أن يمنع بطلان اللازم فهو لا يخلو عن منع الملازمة إن لوحظ الكل
Issue: 'Al-aswad' (the black) and similar derived nouns indicate a certain essence qualified by blackness, for example) that derived nouns mean they indicate the absolute essence (not) the specific essence of being a body or other (otherwise) i.e. if it were not so but rather indicated the specific essence, then our saying 'al-aswad' would yield) body, because the essential (dhāti) is self-evident in proof for what it is) i.e. the essential is essential (to it, and body on this estimation becomes essential to al-aswad due to its inclusion in it. (And in it is that the essential is only (self-evident) in its proof (if the whole is considered in detail) which is prevented here. For al-aswad, when understood) as a whole, does not have a self-evident essential proof. He said in Al-Hashiya: It is not unlikely to be stated thus: If the specific body were included in al-aswad, then predicating it of it at the level of detail would be uninformative. But this is not free from some ambiguity, because the opponent can deny the falsehood of the necessary consequence, so it is not free from denying the implication if the whole is considered.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage defines the meaning of derived nouns (mushtaqqāt) like 'al-aswad' (the black). The issue is whether such nouns indicate a specific essence (e.g., a body) or a general essence (any entity) qualified by the attribute. The author argues that derived nouns indicate the absolute, general essence (dhāt mutlaqa) and not a specific substance like a body. If they indicated a specific essence, then saying 'al-aswad' would imply 'body', making body an essential (dhāti) part of blackness. However, an essential property is only self-evident if the whole is analyzed in detail, but here 'al-aswad' is understood as a whole without that analysis. The commentator (from Al-Hashiya) notes that if body were included, predicating body of 'al-aswad' at the detailed level would be redundant (since it would already be inherent). However, this reasoning is not airtight because an opponent could deny the conclusion. The passage is a nuanced linguistic analysis within Hanafi usul al-fiqh, clarifying that derived terms denote a subject characterized by an attribute, not the attribute's bearer's specific nature.

Key Terms
مشتق (mushtaqq): A derived noun that indicates a general essence (dhāt muṭlaqa) qualified by an attribute, not the specific nature of the essence.
ذات (dhāt): Essence; here it is general (muṭlaqa) as opposed to specific (khāṣṣa).
ذاتي (dhātī): Essential; a property that is intrinsic and self-evident in a definition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They indicate a specific body that is black
B) They indicate a general essence qualified by blackness
C) They indicate the attribute of blackness alone, without any essence
D) They indicate both a specific essence and the attribute

Key Takeaway
Derived nouns in Hanafi usul denote a general essence described by an attribute, a principle that preserves linguistic generality and avoids unnecessary ontological commitments.

The Meaning of Derived Nouns: Simple or Composite?
Debate on derived noun meaning and predication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two competing views on whether derived nouns indicate a simple or composite meaning, and identify the Hanafi preferred view on predication of derived nouns.

Core Idea
Derived nouns indicate a simple attribute, not the essence; predication is possible through relational existence.

This lesson explores a key debate in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: do derived nouns like 'aswad' (black) include the essence (dhat) in their meaning? We will examine the minority view of al-Mawla Jalal al-Din and the majority view, culminating in the preferred position that derived nouns are predicates with a relational existence, making explicit mention of the essence unnecessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Extract from the text the view of Jalal al-Din (simple meaning) and the opposing majority view (composite meaning or summary of composite).
Jalal al-Din argues the concept of 'aswad' is just blackness; the author argues it is a summary of 'essence having blackness'.
2
Understand the minority view
Jalal al-Din claims derived nouns indicate a simple meaning (attribute only) that necessarily entails a subject. The concept is not composite; the entailment is external.
Example: 'aswad' means blackness; the fact that it entails a subject is a logical consequence, not part of the meaning.
3
Analyze the majority critique
The author responds that the simple meaning is actually the summary (ijmal) of the composite 'essence having attribute'. The meaning is not truly simple but a condensed composite.
The critique hinges on whether the entailment is part of the concept or external; the author argues it is internal at the summary level.
4
Examine the author's preferred view
The author states that derived nouns are predicates with relational-unitive existence (wujud rabiti). They connect to subjects by 'being it' (huwa huwa) without needing to include the essence in their concept.
This view is based on the idea that predicates as such have relational existence; they are taken 'la bi-shart shay' (unconditioned).
5
Distinguish derived nouns from roots
Derived nouns are 'la bi-shart shay' (unconditioned) allowing connection to subjects; roots (mabadi) are 'bi-shart la shay' (conditioned by nothing) and self-subsistent, thus not predicable.
This distinction is crucial for understanding why derived nouns function differently in predication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مجملاً، وعن منع بطلان اللازم إن لوحظ مفصلاً وبعض المحققين) وهو المولى جلال الدين رحمه الله تعالى على أنه لا يدل المشتق على الذات أصلاً لا عاماً ولا خاصاً) فإنا نعلم ضرورة أنه ليس مفهومه إلا ما يعبر عنه بسياه وسفيد (فمعنى الجسم أسود الجسم له سواد) بل جسم سياه (لا أنه جسم له السواد حتى يلاحظ الجسم مرتين مرة موضوعاً ومرة في المحمول (ولا) أيضاً معناه (ذات) له (السواد فإنه لا يفهم أصلاً، لكن هذا المعنى البسيط يلزمه ذات له السواد لا أنه عينه، وهذا المعنى البسيط له فردان الجسم وهو يصدق عليه صدقاً عرضياً لكونه متعلقاً به علاقة مخصوصة بها ينسب وجوده إليه، ويقال إنه هو، وفرد آخر يصدق هو عليه صدقاً ذاتياً، فإنه تمام حقيقته فإن السواد أسود بنفسه ، والفرق بينهما بالإطلاق والتقييد وهو الذي قال هذا المحقق إذا أخذ هذا المفهوم لا بشرط شيء كان مفهوم أسود ،وعرضياً، وإذا أخذ بشرط لا شيء كان عرضاً وعين السواد، وإذا أخذ بشرط المحل كان الثوب الأسود، ومقصوده أنه على هذا التقرير يحصل هذا المقيد لا أنه يكون عين المحل ويتحد معه، كيف وقد عرفت أن اتحاد الاثنين باطل مطلقاً، وكيف يتفوه به أمثال هذا المحقق هذا تقرير كلامه على وجه يوافق مرامه، ثم إنه دعوى نظرية في مقابلة السواد الأعظم، فلا تسمع من غير دليل وأما أنه معنى بسيط فمسلم لكن لم لا يجوز أن يكون إجمال هذا المركب أي ذات له السواد لا بد لنفيه من دليل وما قال إنه لا يفهم منه ذات له السوادان أراد هذا المفصل فمسلم ولا يلزم منه المطلوب. وإن أراد إجماله فممنوع أنه غير منفهم بل هذا المعنى البسيط الذي ادعاه هو إجماله لا غير وقال المصنف : (وهو الأشبه فإن) المشتقات محمولات والمحمولات من حيث هي هي لها وجودات رابطية اتحادية مع الموضوعات فلا حاجة إلى أخذ الذات في مفهوماتها فإن مفهوم له السواد كما أنه يتحد مع الذات على تقدير أخذها كذلك يتحد بالجسم الموضوع بلا توسيط الذات، نعم : لو قصد إلى تعبيره ولم يكن خصوص الموضوع في البين احتيج في التعبير إلى أخذ الذات المبهمة تحصيلاً للتعبير لا غير بخلاف المبادىء لها فإن وجوداتها ليست وجودات رابطية (بناء على أن الفرق بينهما أن الأولى) أي المشتقات (لا) بشرط شيء) فهي صالحة لأن ترتبط بغيرها بهوهو لأن فيها نوعاً من الإبهام (والثانية) أي المبادىء (بشرط لا شيء) فهي متحصلة بالذات، وموجودة بوجود مغاير للموضوع فلا تصلح لأن ترتبط بغيرها بهوهو هذا تقرير كلامه (فافهم) وفيه: أن كون وجود المحمولات رابطية لا يستلزم أن تكون معانيها بسائط، بل يجوز أن تكون مركبة ويكون وجوداتها ،رابطية ثم إن ما قال لا حاجة إلى الذات حينئذ منظور فيه، فإن الخصم لا يقنع عليه، وأيضاً لم يؤخذ الذات لصحة الحمل، ولا لأنه لا يمكن صحته بدونه بل لأن معاني المشتقات وجدت في الواقع كذلك مركبات من الذات والصفة وهل هذا إلا كما يقال : يكفي للحمل على زيد مفهوم الناطق، فلا يحتاج إلى أخذ الحيوان في الإنسان ثم هذه التفرقة الاعتبارية التي ادعاها من الفرق لا بشرط شيء وبشرط لا شيء غير لازم من كون وجودات المحمولات رابطية، فإنه يجوز أن يكون معاني المشتقات بسيطة ووجوداتها رابطية لكن
In summary, and regarding the prevention of the invalidity of the necessary consequence if considered in detail - and some investigators, namely al-Mawla Jalal al-Din (may Allah have mercy on him) said that the derived noun does not indicate the essence at all, neither generally nor specifically. For we know necessarily that its concept is nothing but what is expressed as 'blackness and whiteness' (i.e., meaning of 'black' is 'the body has blackness', but rather 'body is black' - not that it is 'a body having blackness' so that the body is observed twice, once as subject and once in the predicate, nor does its meaning include 'essence having blackness' for this is not understood at all. However, this simple meaning entails an essence having blackness, not that it is identical to it. This simple meaning has two individuals: the body - it is attributed to it accidentally due to its being attached to it by a special relationship by which its existence is attributed to it, and it is said it is it; and another individual which is attributed to it essentially, for it is its complete reality, for blackness is black by itself. The difference between them is by absoluteness and restriction, and it is what this investigator said: if this concept is taken unconditionally it becomes the concept of 'black' and accidental; if taken with the condition of nothing (i.e., absolute) it becomes the accident and the essence of blackness; if taken with the condition of a locus it becomes the black garment. His intent is that according to this formulation this restricted meaning is obtained, not that it becomes identical to the locus and unites with it. How can it be when you know that the union of two things is absolutely invalid? How could someone like this investigator utter such? This is an exposition of his speech in a manner consistent with his intention. Then it is a theoretical claim opposing the majority, so it should not be accepted without evidence. As for it being a simple meaning, that is accepted, but why can it not be the summary of this composite (i.e., 'an essence having blackness')? One must prove its negation. And what he said that 'an essence having blackness' is not understood from it: if he intended this detailed meaning, it is accepted but that does not yield the desired conclusion. If he intended its summary, it is denied that it is not understood; rather, this simple meaning he claims is its summary. The author said: 'And it is more likely, for derived nouns are predicates, and predicates as such have relational-unitive existences with subjects, so there is no need to include the essence in their concepts. For the concept of 'having blackness' unites with the subject body without mediation of the essence, just as it unites with the essence if included. Yes, if one intends to express it and there is no particular subject, one needs to include an indefinite essence for the purpose of expression only, unlike the roots (mabadi) for their existences are not relational. Based on the difference between them: the first (derived nouns) are unconditioned (la bi-shart shay') so they can connect to other things by 'being it' because they have a kind of ambiguity; the second (roots) are conditioned by nothing (bi-shart la shay'), so they are self-subsistent and exist with an existence different from the subject, thus they cannot be connected to others by 'being it'. This is the exposition of his speech: Understand. And in it: That the relational existence of predicates does not necessitate that their meanings are simples; rather, they may be composites and their existences relational. Also, what he said about no need for the essence is questionable, for the opponent is not convinced. Moreover, the essence is not taken for the validity of predication, nor because it cannot be valid without it, but because the meanings of derived nouns are actually found as composites of essence and attribute. Is this not like saying: for predication of 'rational' to Zayd, it suffices to use the concept of 'rational' without including 'animal' in 'human'? Then this mental distinction he claimed - between unconditioned and conditioned by nothing - is not necessitated by the relational existence of predicates, for it is possible for the meanings of derived nouns to be simple while their existences are relational, but...
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a detailed debate on the meaning of derived nouns (mushtaqqat) like 'aswad' (black). The author presents the view of a scholar (Jalal al-Din) who argues that derived nouns do not include the essence (dhat) in their meaning; rather, they indicate a simple attribute. For example, 'aswad' simply means blackness, not 'a thing having blackness'. However, this simple meaning necessarily entails a subject but is not identical to it. The author then critiques this view, siding with the majority that derived nouns are predicates with a relational-unitive existence with their subjects, so the essence need not be included explicitly. He explains that derived nouns are 'la bi-shart shay' (unconditioned), allowing them to connect to subjects, while roots (mabadi) are 'bi-shart la shay' (conditioned by nothing), making them self-subsistent. The discussion touches on predication and the nature of existence.

Key Terms
مشتق (mushtaqq): A derived noun (e.g., fa'il, maf'ul) that does not include the essence in its concept but implies it.
ذات (dhat): Essence or subject that is not explicitly included in the meaning of a derived noun.
وجود رابطي (wujud rabiti): A relational, unitive existence that predicates have with their subjects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's preferred view, why is there no need to include the essence (dhat) in the concept of a derived noun?
A) Because the essence is already understood from the subject.
B) Because derived nouns have a relational-unitive existence with subjects.
C) Because derived nouns are simple attributes.
D) Because the essence is the same as the attribute.

Key Takeaway
A derived noun indicates a simple attribute, but its relational existence allows predication without explicitly including the essence.

Names of Time, Place, Instrument: Meaning and Specificity
Defines the semantics of derived nouns for time/place/instrument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi principle that names of time, place, and instrument indicate an absolute thing, with specificity as a necessary property, and apply this to analogous terms in fiqh.

Core Idea
Names of time/place/instrument indicate the absolute act, not a specific instance; specificity is a necessary consequence.

This passage from Ibn Abd al-Shakur's commentary refines our understanding of how nouns of time, place, and instrument function in Arabic. While they often refer to specific instances, the Hanafi school teaches that this specificity is not part of their essential meaning. Instead, the core meaning is an absolute thing (like 'time' or 'place'), and the particularity is a necessary feature in usage. This distinction prevents over-literal interpretation in legal texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three noun types
The passage discusses 'asmā' al-zamān' (time nouns), 'asmā' al-makān' (place nouns), and 'asmā' al-ālah' (instrument nouns). Recognize that these are derived forms (mīmī) that indicate the locus of an action.
Examples: maw'id (time of promise), maktab (place of writing), miftāḥ (key, instrument of opening).
2
Understand the initial claim
The author first states the common view: these nouns indicate specific things (dhawāt makhṣūṣah) from time, place, or instrument. However, they are ambiguous with respect to individuals, meaning they do not specify a particular occurrence.
For instance, 'maw'id' means 'a time', not necessarily a specific date.
3
Analyze the refinement
The passage refines: specificity is not included in the meaning itself; it is a necessary property (lāzim). The core meaning is an absolute thing (al-shay' al-muṭlaq), and the specific instances are its external realizations.
The author references an objection from al-Aṭwal that perhaps we should consider specific things, but the preferred view is to stick with the absolute meaning.
4
Apply to a concrete example
Consider 'muḍrab' (place of striking). Its meaning is 'a thing where striking occurs', which is essentially time or place. The specificity (this particular place) is not part of the meaning but a necessary property in actual use.
This analysis helps in legal contexts: when a contract mentions 'maktab', it does not refer to a specific office but to any writing place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنهم قالوا إن أسماء الزمان والمكان والآلة تدل على ذوات مخصوصة من الزمان والمكان والآلة، وإن كانت مبهمات بالنظر إلى أفرادها من الأزمنة المخصوصة، والأمكنة كذلك، والآلات المخصوصة، فالخصوصية من الزمان والمكان والآلة غير داخلة (وربما يمنع) في الأطول اعتبار ذوات مخصوصة لجواز أن يكون الخصوص من اللوازم) فالمعتبر فيها الشيء المطلق ومصداقه الخصوصيات فإن شيئاً يقع فيه الضرب) الذي هو مفهوم المضرب (مثلاً ليس إلا الزمان أو المكان فتدبر).
Then they said that names of time, place, and instrument indicate specific things from time, place, and instrument, even though they are ambiguous with respect to their individuals: specific times, places, and instruments. So specificity from time, place, and instrument is not included. And perhaps some prevent in 'al-Atwal' considering specific things, because it is possible that specificity is among the necessary properties. So what is considered is the absolute thing, and its realization is the specificities. For example, a thing in which striking occurs - which is the concept of 'mudrab' - is nothing but time or place, so reflect.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This short passage discusses the linguistic principle regarding nouns designating time (ism al-zaman), place (ism al-makan), and instrument (ism al-alah). The author clarifies that such nouns indicate a specific time, place, or instrument, but this specificity is not part of the core meaning; rather, it is a necessary consequence (lazim). For example, 'mudrab' (place of striking) means 'a thing where striking occurs', which is essentially time or place, but the specific locality is not included in the meaning itself. The author references a possible objection from 'al-Atwal' suggesting that maybe we should consider specific things, but the preferred view is that the meaning is absolute, and specificity is merely concomitant.

Key Terms
اسم الزمان (ism al-zamān): A noun indicating a time when an action occurs (e.g., 'maw'id' = time of promise).
اسم المكان (ism al-makān): A noun indicating a place where an action occurs (e.g., 'maktab' = place of writing).
اسم الآلة (ism al-ālah): A noun indicating an instrument for an action (e.g., 'miftāḥ' = key).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A specific time, place, or instrument
B) An absolute thing that entails specificity as a necessary property
C) Both the absolute thing and the specific attribute equally
D) Only the action without any reference to time or place

Key Takeaway
Names of time, place, and instrument indicate an absolute thing; specificity is a necessary property, not part of the meaning.
