Shared Terms (Mushtarak): Definitions and the Dispute over Their Existence
Defines mushtarak and presents the dispute over its existence in language

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define mushtarak, distinguish it from murtajal and haqiqah/majaz, and evaluate the Hanafi position that mushtarak occurs in Arabic using evidence from the word 'qur' and the logic of infinite meanings.

Core Idea
A single word can be placed for multiple opposite meanings — mushtarak exists and context resolves ambiguity.

This lesson introduces the concept of shared terms (mushtarak) in Arabic, a key topic in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. We first define three categories of singular words based on how they acquire multiple meanings. Then we examine a classical dispute: does mushtarak actually occur in language? The Hanafi school affirms its occurrence, supported by examples like 'qur' (menstruation and purity) and rational arguments about finite words needing to cover infinite meanings. Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to interpret ambiguous textual evidence in legal derivation.

Guided Path
1
Define the three categories of singular words with multiple meanings
Identify the criteria that distinguish mushtarak (shared from the outset), murtajal (coined after abandoning original), and transfer with haqiqah/majaz (both original and transferred used). Focus on the condition 'placed from the beginning without considering prior meaning' for mushtarak.
Murtajal is distinct from manqul; here the original is abandoned.
2
Understand the dispute over mushtarak's existence
Reduce the positions to two real views: occurrence (waqi') vs. non-occurrence. Note that 'necessity' and 'impossibility' are relative, not absolute. The Hanafi position is that it occurs.
The text states 'al-waqi' huwa al-asahh' — occurrence is the most correct.
3
Examine the primary Hanafi evidence: the word 'qur'
Analyze how 'qur' is used for both menstruation (hayd) and purity (tuhr), which are opposites. This refutes the claim that sharing between opposites is impossible.
Imam al-Razi denied sharing between contradictory opposites (naqidayn), but the Hanafis accept it between ordinary opposites.
4
Analyze the rational argument: infinite meanings vs. finite words
If every meaning required a unique word, infinite meanings would exhaust finite words. Therefore, sharing is necessary. Note the counter-argument: meanings are not infinite in the required sense; finite words can cover general types and specifics via addition (idafah).
The Hanafi response upholds the possibility of mushtarak, not absolute necessity.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preferred view)
The text sides with occurrence because of actual linguistic evidence. Understand that the dispute affects ijtihad: if mushtarak exists, a word like 'qur' must be interpreted contextually; if not, one meaning would be default.
This thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) is crucial for a faqih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الفصل الثاني) (وهو) أي المفرد (إن تعدد معناه فإن وضع لكل من المعاني المتعددة (ابتداء) من غير اعتبار أنه كان موضوعاً لمعنى قبل فمشترك وإلا يوضع لكل ابتداء فإن ترك استعماله في الأول ونقل إلى الثاني بحيث يفهم من غير قرينة (المناسبة فنقول أولا لمناسبة فمرتجل وإلا) أي وإن لم يترك الأول بل تارة يستعمل فيه وتارة في الثاني ( فحقيقة) في المنقول منه ومجاز) في المنقول إليه . مسألة المشترك قد اختلف فيه فقيل بوجوبه ، وقيل باستحالته المراد بالوجوب الضرورة بالنظر إلى معاش العباد لا الوجوب الذاتي، وكيف يتفوه به عاقل والمراد بالاستحالة ضده (وقيل بإمكانه فقيل بعدم وقوعه، وقيل بوقوعه وهو الأصح قد عرفت معنى الوجوب والاستحالة، فحينئذ لم يكن القول بالوجوب إلا بالوجوب بالغير، وهو الإمكان مع الوقوع وكذا الاستحالة المقابلة له، ليست إلا الاستحالة بالغير، وليست إلا الإمكان مع عدم الوقوع، فالأقوال إذن قولان : الوقوع ،وعدمه، ولذا أورد استدلال الفريقين فقط (لنا القرء) موضوع (للحيض والطهر معاً لتبادرهما حين الإطلاق، وإذا ثبت كونه مشتركاً بينهما وهما ضدان (فسقط منع جماعة الاشتراك بين الضدين و روي عن الإمام فخر الدين الرازي منعه بين النقيضين) لكمال التباعد (واستدل) على الوقوع بل الوجوب (أولاً) بأنه لو لم يكن) أي لم يوجد في كلام العرب الخلت أكثر (المسميات عن الألفاظ بإزائها واللازم باطل، لأنه حينئذ يفوت التعبير عنها، والملازمة (لأنها أي المسميات (غير متناهية والألفاظ متناهية فلا يفي كل واحد منها بإزاء كل واحد من المسميات بأن يكون واحد بإزاء واحد، بل يبقى الأكثر من المسميات والألفاظ تنفد، وإنما كانت متناهية لتركبها من حروف متناهية والمركب من المتناهي متناه، (وأجيب) عن هذا الاستدلال بأن الاشتراك إنما يكون بين معان متضادة أو متخالفة) فإن أردتم أن هذه المعاني غير متناهية فلا نقنع به ولا نسلم أنها غير متناهية وإن أردتم أن مطلق المعاني غير متناهية فمسلم، لكن لم لا يجوز أن تخلو الجزئيات عن الأوضاع لها بخصوصها وتكون الألفاظ المتناهية موضوعة بإزاء المتخالفات ويعبر عن هذه الجزئيات بالإضافة، هذا وأيضاً
(The second chapter) (It, i.e., the single word) if it has multiple meanings, then if it is placed for each of the multiple meanings from the beginning without considering that it was placed for a meaning before, it is a mushtarak (shared term). Otherwise, if it is placed from the beginning, then if its use in the first meaning is abandoned and it is transferred to the second such that it is understood without a contextual clue, then (if there is suitability we say it is murtajal (coined), otherwise, i.e., if the first is not abandoned but is used sometimes in the first and sometimes in the second, then it is haqiqah (literal) in the original and majaz (metaphorical) in the transferred. Issue: The shared term (mushtarak) has been disputed: some say it is necessary, some say it is impossible. By 'necessary' is meant necessary for the livelihood of people, not essential necessity (how could a rational person assert that?), and by 'impossible' is meant its opposite. Some say it is possible, then some say it does not occur, and some say it does occur, and this is the most correct. You have understood the meaning of necessity and impossibility: then the claim of necessity is only necessity by another, i.e., possibility with occurrence; similarly, impossibility in opposition is only impossibility by another, i.e., possibility with non-occurrence. So the opinions are actually two: occurrence and non-occurrence. Hence the arguments of only the two groups are presented. (Evidence: the word 'qur' is placed for both menstruation and purity (tuhr) simultaneously because they both come to mind when used absolutely. So if it is established that it is shared between them, and they are opposites, then the rejection by some of sharing between opposites falls away. And it is reported from Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi that he prohibited it between contradictory opposites due to complete distance. (They argued) for occurrence, indeed necessity, firstly by saying: if it (mushtarak) were not present (in the speech of the Arabs), there would be more named things than words for them, and the consequent is false because then expression would be lost. The correlation: because the named things are infinite and words are finite, so each one cannot correspond to each named thing one-to-one; rather, most of the named things remain and words are exhausted. They are finite because they are composed of finite letters, and the composite of finite is finite. (It was answered) that mushtarak is only between opposite or different meanings. If you mean that these meanings are infinite, we are not convinced and we do not accept they are infinite. If you mean absolute meanings are infinite, it is accepted; but why can it not be that the particulars lack specific wordings for themselves, and the finite words are placed for the different types, and these particulars are expressed by addition? This, and also...
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is from the second chapter on singular words. It begins by defining three types: mushtarak (shared term placed for multiple meanings from the outset), murtajal (coined for a new meaning after abandoning the old), and haqiqah/majaz (when used both literally and metaphorically). The main issue (mas'alah) is whether mushtarak exists in language. The author reduces the debate to two real positions: occurrence or non-occurrence. Supporters argue that words like 'qur' (which means both menstruation and purity) prove the existence of sharing between opposites, refuting those who deny it. Another proof is that if there were no sharing, infinite meanings would require infinite words, but words are finite; thus sharing is necessary. The response counters that meanings are not necessarily infinite in a way that requires new words; finite words can cover different types and express particulars through compounds (bi-al-idafah).

Key Terms
مشترك (mushtarak): A word placed for multiple meanings from the beginning, without priority.
مرتجل (murtajal): A word coined for a new meaning after the original meaning is abandoned, with no contextual clue needed.
حقيقة (haqiqah): Literal meaning when a word is used in its original sense.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical meaning when a word is used in a secondary sense.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the word 'qur' demonstrate according to the text?
A) That mushtarak cannot occur between opposites
B) That a shared term can exist between contradictory opposites
C) That mushtarak is necessary for all languages
D) That textual evidence is not needed for linguistic analysis

Key Takeaway
Mushtarak is a real feature of Arabic; it must be resolved by context, not denied on the grounds of ambiguity.

The Univocity of 'Existent' and the Necessity of Commonality
Arguing that 'existent' is univocal, requiring commonality to avoid making God and creatures identical

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to APPLY the argument for commonality (اشتراك) and DISTINGUISH between univocity and commonality in the case of 'existent' between the Necessary and contingent, and EVALUATE the Hanafi response.

Core Idea
Univocity of 'existent' does not force identical essences; difference in modality preserves commonality.

In usul al-fiqh, the nature of language and its categories—such as univocity (تواطؤ) and commonality (اشتراك)—are foundational for interpreting revealed texts. This passage tackles a theological-linguistic argument: whether the term 'existent' is univocal, which would imply God and creatures share a single essence, or common, allowing for different meanings. The author defends the possibility of commonality against a rational objection, employing metaphysical distinctions that affect how we understand divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the argument (الدليل)
The arguer claims that if commonality (اشتراك) did not occur, then 'existent' would be univocal (متواطئ) between the Necessary and the contingent, leading to the contradiction that one reality is both Necessary and contingent.
This is a rational proof for the necessity of commonality.
2
Recognize the objection
The objector insists that univocity would force the essences of God and creatures to be identical, which is impossible.
This is the core of the dispute.
3
Locate the Hanafi response
The author answers that difference by necessity and possibility does not harm univocity, as shown by terms like 'knower' and 'speaker' applied to both God and creatures.
The counterexample (نقض) is used to show the flaw in the objection.
4
Deep analysis: the solution (الحل)
The concept of 'existent' is necessary by another (واجب بالغير) in God's essence and possible in itself (ممكن بالذات) for contingents. This dual consideration avoids the contradiction.
The solution relies on the distinction between essence and attribute.
5
Note the scope of the argument
The validity of the Hanafi response depends on the underlying theological view: if existence is additional to essence (majority view), the response stands; if existence is identical (Ash'ari view), the objection returns.
This shows how usul disputes are intertwined with theological commitments.

Source Text

آخر) فالموجود بالنظر إلى ذات الباري عز وجل واجب دون غيره من الذوات ومن ههنا أي من أجل أن المقصود من الجواب الأول النقض علم سقوط ما قيل عليه أن للمستدل أن يقول : كلامي في نفس هذه الحقيقة أي حقيقة الموجود لا) في الحقائق المندرجة تحتها فإنها من حيث هي إما واجبة أو ممكنة ولا يصح الاجتماع، فحينئذ الاختلاف بالوجوب والإمكان فيه يضر وحدة الحقيقة فيضر التواطؤ واعلم أن مناط كلام هذا القائل إنه فهم حاصل الجواب أن غاية ما يلزم من تواطؤ الموجود بين الواجب والممكن اختلاف أفراده بالوجوب والإمكان وذا جائز ، فأورد عليه أنه لا بل يلزم أن يكون نفس هذا المفهوم واجباً وممكناً، إذ الوجود في الواجب واجب وفي الممكن ممكن ويمكن إرجاع كلامه إلى جواب المصنف، وحينئذ يندفع إيراد هذا القائل هذا وتحقيق هذا المقام أن المستدل إما أن يريد نفس مفهوم الوجود والموجود الانتزاعي، وإما به يصير الشيء واقعاً صالحاً لانتزاع هذا المفهوم ومطابقاً لحمله فإن أراد الثاني وبنى كلامه على قول الشيخ الأشعري من العينية لسائر الذوات كما هو الحق فلا توجه للجواب أصلاً إذ الحاصل حينئذ أنه لو كان الموجود غير مشترك بل متواطئاً يلزم أن تكون الذوات كلها متوافقة بالحقيقة، لأن الذوات نفسه فيلزم أن تكون حقيقة الواجب والممكن واحدة، هذا خلف فحينئذ تم الدليل ولا يرد النقض ولا يتوجه الحل كما لا يخفى على ذي كياسة، وكذا لو بنى على رأي المشائين من الفلاسفة من العينية في الواجب والزيادة في الممكن، وكان هذا البناء بعيد، نعم يتوجه عليه أنه على هذا إنما يلزم مطلوبكم لو ثبت وضع لفظ الوجود والموجود لمصداقه في اللغة، وإلا لا يفيد وإن بني كلامه على مذهب باقي المتكلمين من الزيادة في الكل وإن كان هذا المبنى فاسداً في نفسه فحينئذ يتم جواب المصنف فإنه حينئذ ممكن بالذات واجب باقتضاء الذات وإيجابه في الباري وكذا بالنظر إلى الواجب الموصوف وممكن بالنظر إلى الذوات الممكنة وإن أراد الأول تعين جواب المصنف أيضاً كما قررنا
(And) he argued (secondly) for the occurrence of commonality, rather its necessity, saying: if commonality were not occurring, then 'existent' would be univocal between the Necessary and the contingent, necessitating that the one reality (i.e., existent) be both Necessary and contingent – this is a contradiction. (The answer: difference by necessity and possibility does not prevent univocity, like 'knower' and 'speaker', for they are univocal between the Necessary and contingent. The author said: its gist is the counterexample by them. And it is not hidden that one then adds his words 'difference by necessity and possibility etc.' rather it suffices to mention the counterexample only. (The solution: necessity by another does not conflict with possibility by essence. So the concept of existent is necessary by another, which is the essence in the Necessary, because it is its requisite, and possible in itself. Just as necessity with respect to one attribute does not conflict with possibility with respect to another attribute.) So existent, with respect to the essence of Allah Most High, is Necessary, not other essences. And from here, i.e., because the purpose of the first answer is the counterexample, it is known that what is said against it falls: that the arguer can say: my speech is about this very reality, i.e., the reality of existent, not about the realities subsumed under it, for they insofar as they are are either Necessary or contingent, and combination is not valid. In that case, the difference by necessity and possibility harms the unity of the reality and thus harms univocity. Know that the basis of this speaker's speech is that he understood the gist of the answer: that the utmost that follows from the univocity of existent between the Necessary and contingent is the difference of its individuals by necessity and possibility, and that is permissible. So he objected: no, rather it necessitates that this very concept be both Necessary and possible, for existence in the Necessary is necessary and in the contingent is possible. And his speech can be referred back to the answer of the author, and then this speaker's objection is repelled. And the investigation of this position: the arguer either intends the very concept of existence and the abstracted 'existent', or what makes a thing actual and suitable for abstraction of this concept and corresponding to its predication. If he intends the second and builds his speech on the view of Shaykh al-Ash'ari of identity with all essences, as is the truth, then no answer applies at all, for the result then is that if 'existent' were not common but univocal, it would necessitate that all essences be identical in reality, because the essences themselves are the same, so the reality of the Necessary and contingent would be one – a contradiction. Then the proof is complete, and the counterexample does not apply nor the solution, as is not hidden to the astute. Similarly if he builds on the view of the peripatetic philosophers of identity in the Necessary and addition in the contingent, though this building is far. Yes, it is objected that on this, your desired conclusion only follows if it is established that the word 'existence' and 'existent' is placed for its referent in language; otherwise it does not benefit. And if he builds his speech on the view of the rest of the theologians of addition in all, even if this foundation is corrupt in itself, then the author's answer is valid: for then it is possible by essence, necessary by the requirement of the essence and its necessitation in the Creator, and similarly with respect to the described Necessary, and possible with respect to contingent essences. And if he intends the first, then the author's answer is also necessitated as we have stated.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument for the necessity of commonality (اشتراك) in language: if words were not common, the term 'existent' would be univocal (متواطئ) between God (the Necessary Being) and contingent beings, leading to the absurdity that one reality is both Necessary and contingent. The author then discusses the rebuttal: difference by necessity and possibility does not prevent univocity, as seen in terms like 'knower' applied to both. The text then dives into deep theological and philosophical nuances, considering different views on whether existence is identical to essence (Ash'ari view) or additional (Mu'tazilite view). The key point is that whether 'existent' is a concept derived from reality or the reality itself, the univocity argument fails because the concept can be considered in different respects (necessary by another, possible by essence). Ultimately, the Hanafi position, aligning with the majority of theologians (that existence is additional in both cases), supports the author's answer, preserving the possibility of commonality without forcing identity of essences.

Key Terms
موجود (mawjūd): What has existence; used for both God and creatures.
متواطئ (mutawāṭi’): Applied to multiple instances with the same meaning (univocal).
واجب بالغير (wājib bi’l-ghayr): Necessary due to another (the essence requiring existence).
اشتراك (ishtirāk): Commonality: a word applied to multiple distinct meanings (homonymy).
الوجوب بالغير لا ينافي الإمكان بالذات (al-wujūb bi’l-ghayr lā yunāfī al-imkān bi’l-dhāt): Necessity by another does not conflict with possibility by essence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the author's solution to the objection that if 'existent' were univocal, it would make God and creatures identical?
A) 'Existent' is not univocal because the two differ essentially.
B) The concept of 'existent' is necessary by another and possible in itself, so it is one concept with different modalities.
C) The term 'existent' is common (مشترك) between God and creatures with different meanings.
D) The argument is invalid because language does not reflect reality.

Key Takeaway
The argument for commonality hinges on the logical consequence of univocity, which is defused by distinguishing between the concept and its modes of necessity and possibility.

Types of Haqiqah: Linguistic, General Customary, Special Customary
Defining haqiqah and its divisions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of haqiqah (linguistic, general customary, special customary) and correctly classify given words according to their discourse convention.

Core Idea
Haqiqah is a word used in its placed meaning within a specific discourse; words can shift from linguistic to customary truth.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, understanding the type of truth (haqiqah) a word carries is essential for correctly interpreting legal texts. This lesson defines haqiqah and divides it into three categories based on the origin of the placement: language, general custom, or special custom. Mastering these categories allows you to determine whether a word is used in its real meaning or metaphorically.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of haqiqah
Read the definition: haqiqah is a word used in what it was placed for in the terminology of discourse. Note the key condition: the word must be used according to the convention (istilah) of the discourse.
This definition distinguishes haqiqah from majaz.
2
Recognize the three types based on the origin of placement
The origin (wadi') determines the type: if by language, it is lughawiyyah; if by general custom ('urf 'amm), it is 'urfiyyah 'ammah; if by special custom ('urf khass, e.g., philosophical or technical), it is 'urfiyyah khassah.
Special customary is also called istilahiyyah.
3
Understand how each type arises
General customary truth arises by specification of the linguistic meaning (e.g., dabbah specified from any crawler to quadrupeds) or by fame of metaphorical usage (e.g., adding prohibition to wine became famous). Special customary truth may arise by generalization (e.g., addresses to the Prophet apply to the ummah by custom).
The text mentions both specification and fame processes.
4
Apply the classification to examples
Test the example of 'dabbah': linguistically it means anything that crawls, but in common custom it means quadruped. Thus, in general discourse, it is haqiqah 'urfiyyah 'ammah.
Other examples: 'salat' in Shari'ah is special customary; 'kitaab' in language is linguistic.
5
Contrast haqiqah with majaz
If a word is used outside its placed meaning in that discourse, it is majaz (metaphor). The classification affects legal interpretation: haqiqah is taken literally unless contextual evidence indicates otherwise.
This distinction is crucial for istinbat (deduction of rulings).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث الحقيقة الكلمة المستعملة فيما وضع له في اصطلاح (التخاطب أي في اصطلاح به يكون التخاطب، فلفظ الفاعل المستعمل في التخاطب اللغوي بمعنى الجاعل مجاز، وإن كان فيما وضع له في اصطلاح الفلسفة (وهي) حقيقة (لغوية) إن كان الواضع اللغة (وعرفية عامة) إن كان الواضع واصطلاح التخاطب عرفاً عاماً كدابة) موضوعة في اللغة لما يدب على الأرض وفي العرف ذات القوائم (وهو إنما يكون بتخصيص) في المعنى اللغوي (قيل : أو باشتهار المجاز، كإضافة التحريم إلى الخمر صارت مشتهرة في إفادة خروج العين عن المحلية للفعل، كذا قيل وحقيقة الحال ستنكشف لك إن شاء الله تعالى من أنها حقيقة لغوية لا غير (أقول : وقد يكون بالتعميم) في المعنى اللغوي (لما ستعلم أن الخطاب الخاص بالنبي عليه وعلى آله الصلاة والسلام يعم الأمة عرفاً) وسيجيء تحقيقه إن شاء الله تعالى (و) عرفية (خاصة) إن كان الواضع واصطلاح التخاطب عرفاً خاصاً غير الشرع (وتسمى (اصطلاحية) أيضاً (كالمنع والنقض و)
The third chapter: Truth (haqiqah) is a word used in what it was placed for in the terminology of discourse (khatab), i.e., in the terminology by which discourse occurs. Thus, the word 'fa'il' used in linguistic discourse to mean 'maker' is a metaphor (majaz), even though it is used in its placed meaning in philosophical terminology. And it (haqiqah) is: linguistic (lughawiyyah) if the originator is language; general customary ('urfiyyah 'ammah) if the originator and the terminology of discourse is a general custom (e.g., 'dabbah' is placed in language for what crawls on the earth, but in general custom it refers to quadrupeds). This comes only by specification in the linguistic meaning; it is said: or by the fame of the metaphor, such as adding prohibition to wine became famous in indicating the removal of the substance from being subject to action. This is what is said, and the reality of the matter will become clear to you, Allah willing, that it is only a linguistic truth. I say: It may also be by generalization in the linguistic meaning, as you will learn that specific address to the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him and his family, generally applies to the ummah by custom, and its verification will come, Allah willing. And special customary ('urfiyyah khassah) if the originator and the terminology of discourse is a special custom other than the Shari'ah, and it is also called terminological (istilahiyyah), such as 'al-man' and 'al-naqd' and...
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the third chapter on the types of truth (حقيقة). The author defines حقيقة as a word used in its placed meaning within the specific context of discourse. He then categorizes it into three types: 1) لغوية (linguistic truth) – when the word is used in its original linguistic meaning; 2) عرفية عامة (general customary truth) – when the word is used in a general custom that has overwritten the linguistic meaning, like 'dābbah' which linguistically means any creeping thing but customarily means quadruped; 3) عرفية خاصة (special customary truth) – when the word is used according to a specialized custom (e.g., philosophical or technical terminology). The author also notes that general customary truth can arise either by specification or by the fame of a metaphorical usage, and special customary truth can also arise by generalization (e.g., addressing the Prophet includes the ummah by custom). This classification is crucial for proper interpretation of texts.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqiqah): A word used in its placed meaning within a specific discourse convention (linguistic, general custom, or special custom).
حقيقة لغوية (haqiqah lughawiyyah): A word used in its original linguistic meaning as placed by the language.
حقيقة عرفية عامة (haqiqah 'urfiyyah 'ammah): A word used according to a general custom, even if different from its linguistic meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Linguistic truth (haqiqah lughawiyyah)
B) General customary truth (haqiqah 'urfiyyah 'ammah)
C) Special customary truth (haqiqah 'urfiyyah khassah)
D) Metaphor (majaz)

Key Takeaway
Haqiqah in Hanafi usul includes linguistic, general customary, and special customary truths; a word's meaning is determined by the discourse convention in which it is used.

The Common Term (Mushtarak) and Its Legal Ambiguity
Defining mushtarak and its ambiguity with/without qarina

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a mushtarak, explain its ambiguity based on the presence or absence of a qarina, and apply the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions to a given textual scenario.

Core Idea
A mushtarak without a determining qarina is mujmal for Hanafis; Shafi'is take it as all meanings.

Words with multiple possible meanings—mushtarak—pose a challenge for legal interpretation. How do we know which meaning is intended? This passage explains that the presence or absence of a contextual indicator (qarina) determines whether the word is ambiguous (mujmal) or clear. The Hanafi and Shafi'i schools differ on the default status when no qarina exists. Understanding this distinction is essential for interpreting legal texts correctly.

Guided Path
1
Define mushtarak and mujmal
Identify the term 'mushtarak' (common term) as a word with multiple meanings. Understand 'mujmal' as an expression whose intended meaning is concealed and requires specification.
The passage contrasts the Hanafi and Shafi'i definitions of ijmal.
2
Identify the condition for ambiguity
Recognize that a mushtarak becomes mujmal only when it is 'stripped of the determining qarina' – meaning no contextual clue is present.
If a qarina is present, the word is not necessarily mujmal.
3
Contrast the Hanafi and Shafi'i default positions
Hanafis: without a qarina, mushtarak is mujmal and cannot be used as evidence. Shafi'is: without a qarina, mushtarak indicates all possible meanings (al-kull) and is not mujmal.
This is the core ikhtilaf in the passage.
4
Analyze scenarios based on qarina type
Walk through the five types of qarina mentioned: specifying, non-specific single, majority, cancellation for some, cancellation for all. For each, determine whether the word remains mujmal or becomes clear according to the rules.
Use the passage's examples: 'qarina mu'ayyinah', 'qarinat al-akthar', 'qarinat al-ilgha''.
5
Apply the rules to a concrete example
Consider a mushtarak like 'qur'' (قرء) in Quran 2:228. Without a qarina, Hanafis treat it as mujmal; Shafi'is take it as both meanings. With a qarina (e.g., context of menstruation), it becomes clear.
This illustrates the practical consequence of the theoretical difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه : المشترك أن تجرد عن (القرينة المعينة للمراد بحيث لا يمكن بالرأي تعيين المراد أصلاً (فمجمل) ولا يبعد أن يراد بالإجمال مطلق اختفاء المراد على ما هو مصطلح الشافعية، وحينئذ لا حاجة إلى التقييد فافهم إلا عند الشافعي ومن تبعه فيحمل عندهم على الكل) فليس بمجمل وإن اقترنت به قرينة الأعمال إما لواحد معين فيحمل عليه اتفاقاً (أو) لواحد (غير معين فمجمل بالاتفاق أما عندنا فظاهر، وأما عندهم فلوجود قرينة صارفة عن الكل (أو) إن اقترنت به القرينة (الأكثر فيحمل عليه عند المجوز للعموم وعند المانع مجمل، أو اقترنت به (قرينة الإلغاء، إما للبعض فيحمل على الباقي إن كان واحدا معيناً) بالاتفاق لوجود القرينة (وإلا) أي وإن لم يكن الباقي واحداً (فمجمل إلا عند المجوز للجمع فيحمل على الباقي الأكثر من واحد وإما للكل فيحمل على المجاز (الأرجح اتفاقاً وهو ظاهر (فإن تساوت المجازات بقي الإجمال) وهو ظاهر أيضاً.
Alert: The common term (mushtarak) is stripped of the determining qarina so that it is impossible to determine the intended meaning by opinion at all, so it is ambiguous (mujmal). It is not far-fetched that what is meant by ambiguity (ijmal) is the absolute concealment of the intended meaning according to the terminology of the Shafi'is, and then there is no need for the qualification. Understand, except according to al-Shafi'i and those who follow him, for they consider it to mean the whole (al-kull), so it is not ambiguous. If a qarina of specification (iqrar al-a'mal) is attached, either for a specific single meaning, then it is taken for that meaning by agreement; or for a non-specific single meaning, then it is ambiguous by agreement. As for us (Hanafis), it is clear; as for them (Shafi'is), because there is a qarina diverting from the whole. Or if the qarina of the majority (al-akthar) is attached, then it is taken for that meaning for those who allow general application (al-umum), but for those who disallow it, it is ambiguous. Or if a qarina of cancellation (qarinat al-ilgha') is attached: either for some (al-ba'd), then it is taken for the remainder if it is a specific single one by agreement because of the existence of a qarina; otherwise (i.e., if the remainder is not single), it is ambiguous except for those who allow combination, so it is taken for the remainder of more than one. Or if for the whole (al-kull), then it is taken as a metaphor (majaz) as the more likely by agreement, and that is apparent. If the metaphorical meanings are equal, the ambiguity remains, and that is also apparent.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage defines the common term (mushtarak) – a word with multiple possible meanings. It explains that when such a term is stripped of any qarina (contextual indicator) that would specify which meaning is intended, it becomes ambiguous (mujmal). The Shafi'i school, however, considers it to mean all possible meanings (al-kull) unless a qarina specifies otherwise, so it is not ambiguous for them. The text then categorizes scenarios based on the type of qarina: (1) if a qarina specifies a single meaning, it is taken for that meaning; (2) if it specifies a non-specific single meaning, it is ambiguous by agreement; (3) if a qarina indicates the majority meaning, those who accept general application (umum) take it for that majority, while others consider it ambiguous; (4) if a qarina cancels part of the meaning and the remainder is a single specific meaning, it is taken for that remainder; if the remainder is not single, it is ambiguous except for those who allow combining multiple meanings; (5) if the qarina cancels the whole literal meaning, it is taken as a metaphor. If multiple metaphorical meanings are equally valid, ambiguity remains. The passage underscores the Hanafi position that ambiguity arises in specific circumstances, contrasting with the Shafi'i default assumption of comprehensiveness.

Key Terms
المشترك (al-mushtarak): A word with multiple possible meanings; when stripped of a determining qarina, it is ambiguous unless the Shafi'i view is adopted
القرينة (al-qarina): A contextual indicator that determines the intended meaning of a mushtarak
المجمل (al-mujmal): An expression whose intended meaning is concealed and cannot be determined without further specification

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this passage, when is a common term (mushtarak) considered ambiguous (mujmal)?
A) When it is stripped of any determining qarina
B) When it is used in the Quran
C) When it has only one meaning
D) When it is used by a Shafi'i jurist

Key Takeaway
A mushtarak without a determining qarina is mujmal for Hanafis, requiring external specification before it can be used as evidence.

Haqiqah: Types of Literal Meaning in Hanafi Usul
Definition and classification of haqiqah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define haqiqah and distinguish between its four types: linguistic, customary general, customary specific, and legal.

Core Idea
Haqiqah is a word used in its original meaning according to the discourse convention, not always the dictionary meaning.

This lesson introduces the foundational concept of haqiqah (literal meaning) in Hanafi usul. Understanding the different types of haqiqah is essential for correctly interpreting legal texts, as words may shift meaning between linguistic, customary, and legal contexts.

Guided Path
1
Define haqiqah
According to the text, haqiqah is a word used in what it was originally placed for in the terminology of discourse (al-takhāṭub). Identify the key condition: the convention of the discourse context.
The definition excludes accidental or figurative usage.
2
Identify the four types
List the four types: linguistic (lughawiyyah), customary general ('urfiyyah 'āmmah), customary specific ('urfiyyah khāṣṣah), and legal (shar'iyyah). Note the basis of each: the 'coiner' of the meaning.
Legal haqiqah is based on Sharī'ah terminology, e.g., 'ṣalāh'.
3
Explain how customary haqiqah arises
The text states that customary haqiqah can arise by specification (takhṣīṣ) of the linguistic meaning or by generalization (ta'mīm). It can also come from prevalence of a metaphor (ishtihār al-majāz).
Example: 'dābbah' originally means any moving creature, but in custom it specifies quadrupeds.
4
Apply to examples
Classify the following words according to the types: 'dābbah' (customary general), 'man' (customary specific), 'ṣalāh' (legal), 'kitāb' in a linguistic context (linguistic). Explain your reasoning.
Refer to the text's examples and definitions.

(الفصل الثالث) الحقيقة الكلمة المستعملة فيما وضع له في اصطلاح (التخاطب أي في اصطلاح به يكون التخاطب، فلفظ الفاعل المستعمل في التخاطب اللغوي بمعنى الجاعل مجاز، وإن كان فيما وضع له في اصطلاح الفلسفة (وهي) حقيقة (لغوية) إن كان الواضع اللغة (وعرفية عامة) إن كان الواضع واصطلاح التخاطب عرفاً عاماً كدابة) موضوعة في اللغة لما يدب على الأرض وفي العرف ذات القوائم (وهو إنما يكون بتخصيص) في المعنى اللغوي بالتعميم) في المعنى اللغوي (لما ستعلم أن الخطاب الخاص بالنبي عليه وعلى آله الصلاة والسلام يعم الأمة عرفاً) وسيجيء تحقيقه إن شاء الله تعالى (و) عرفية (خاصة) إن كان الواضع واصطلاح التخاطب عرفاً خاصاً غير الشرع (وتسمى (اصطلاحية) أيضاً (كالمنع والنقض و) حقيقة (شرعية) إن كان الواضع واصطلاح التخاطب الشرع كالصلاة ، والمجاز هي) أي الكلمة (المستعملة في غير الموضوع له بعلاقة) وإن كان من غير علاقة كان خطأ (وهي خمسة وعشرون نوعاً كما في حاشية السيد المحقق قدس سره (للمختصر) السببية، المسببية الكلية والجزئية، ويشترط فيهما أن يكون للكل اسم على حدة وينتفي بانتفاء الجزء عرفاً الملزومية اللازمية ،الإطلاق التقييد العموم ،الخصوص الحالية المحلية المجاورة، الكون فيه، الأول إليه البدلية ،الآلية التشبيه التضاد عموم النكرة في حيز الإثبات، استعمال المعرف باللام في المعهود الذهني حذف المضاف حذف المضاف إليه الحذف مطلقاً الزيادة، ثم إن في النكرة العامة في الإثبات لا تتحقق علاقة مغايرة للتشبيه وكذا في المعرف في العهد الذهني تشبيه الفرد المبهم بالواحد المعين وأقسام الحذف والزيادة ليست من العلاقات للمجاز اللغوي في شيء (وقيل اثنا عشر كما في المنهاج السببية وهي أربعة أنواع الفاعلية، والمادية، والصورية، والغائية المسببية المشابهة معنوية كانت أو صورية المضادة الكلية، الجزئية، الاستعداد الكون فيه المجاورة، الزيادة النقصان التعلق الحاصل بين المصدر واسم المفعول، وبهذه العلاقة يصح إطلاق اسم الفاعل على اسم المفعول وعكسه، وإطلاق المصدر على اسم الفاعل وعكسه، وإطلاقه على اسم المفعول وعكسه كذا في بعض شروح المنهاج وفيه أيضاً أن علاقة الكون فيه ساقطة عن أكثر نسخه (وقيل) في «المختصر» (خمسة) المشاكلة والمشابهة والكون فيه والأول إليه والمجاورة (وقيل) في «البديع» (أربعة) وهي الأخيرة وهذا كله رد إلى الإجمال ولا تناقض كما حصرها مشايخنا في الاثنين المشابهة والمجاورة وهذا كما قال علماء البيان المجاز استعارة ومجاز مرسل.
(Chapter Three) Haqiqah: a word used in what it was originally placed for in the terminology of discourse, i.e., in the convention in which discourse takes place. So the word 'fa'il' (agent) used in linguistic discourse in the sense of 'maker' is majaz (metaphorical), even if it is used in what it was placed for in the terminology of philosophy. (It is) haqiqah (linguistic) if the coiner is the language; (customary general) if the coiner and the terminology of discourse is a general custom, like 'dabbah' (beast) is placed in language for what moves on the ground, and in custom for a quadruped. This only occurs by specification of the linguistic meaning. It was said: or by the prevalence of the metaphor, like the addition of 'prohibited' to wine has become prevalent in indicating the removal of the essence from being suitable for action; thus it was said, and the reality of the matter will become clear to you, God willing, that it is a linguistic haqiqah and nothing else. I say: it may be by generalization in the linguistic meaning, as you will learn that the address specific to the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) generally includes the ummah by custom. Its verification will come, God willing. (And) customary specific if the coiner and the terminology of discourse is a specific custom other than the Law; it is also called terminological, like 'prevention' and 'abrogation'. (And) legal haqiqah if the coiner and the terminology of discourse is the Law, like prayer. Al-Majaz: the word used in other than its original meaning with a relation; if without a relation, it is an error. It comprises twenty-five types as in the hashiyah of al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq on al-Mukhtasar: causality, effectuality whole and part – and in these two it is required that the whole have a distinct name and it is negated by the negation of the part customarily – necessary implication; absolute, restricted; general, specific; state; place; adjacency; being in it; the first to it; substitution; instrument; similarity; opposition; generality of the indefinite in positive contexts; use of the definite article with the cognitively present object; omission of the construct; omission of the construct phrase; omission absolutely; addition. Then, regarding the general indefinite in positive contexts, no relation distinct from similarity is realized; similarly for the definite in cognitive covenant – resemblance of an ambiguous individual to a specific one. The categories of omission and addition are not among the relations of linguistic metaphor at all. It was said: twelve as in al-Minhaj: causality, which is of four types: agentive, material, formal, final; effectuality; similarity, whether conceptual or formal; opposition; whole; part; preparation; being in it; adjacency; addition; subtraction; the relation between the source and the passive participle – by this relation, it is correct to apply the active participle to the passive and vice versa, and the source to the active and vice versa, and it to the passive and vice versa; so in some commentaries of al-Minhaj. It also says that the relation of 'being in it' is dropped from most copies. It was said in al-Mukhtasar: five: similarity, resemblance, being in it, the first to it, and adjacency. It was said in al-Badi': four: the last ones. All this reduces to overall summary and no contradiction; as our shaykhs have limited it to two: similarity and adjacency. This is as the scholars of bayan say: metaphor is metaphor (istiarah) and metonymy (majaz mursal).
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This chapter defines two foundational categories of word usage in Hanafi usul: haqiqah (literal) and majaz (figurative). A word is literal if used in the meaning originally assigned by the linguistic convention of the discourse context. Slight variations in terms like 'fa'il' across different fields (linguistics vs. philosophy) require careful identification of the appropriate convention. Haqiqah is further subdivided into linguistic (based on original language), customary general (based on common custom, e.g., 'dabbah' in Arabic is any creature that walks, but in custom it refers to quadrupeds), customary specific (based on specific technical terms outside law, like 'man' and 'naqd' in disputation), and legal (based on Shari'ah terminology, e.g., 'salah'). The passage notes that some customary haqiqah arises from specification or generalization of linguistic meaning, and that addresses to the Prophet often apply to the ummah as a matter of custom. Majaz, in contrast, is the use of a word in a meaning other than its original one, connected by a 'relation' (ilaqah). The text then enumerates various classifications of these relations: a detailed list of twenty-five from al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq's hashiyah, twelve from al-Minhaj, five from al-Mukhtasar, four from al-Badi', ultimately converging to two core relations: similarity (mushabahah) and adjacency (mujawarah), corresponding to the two broader types of metaphor in balaghah: istiarah (simile-based) and majaz mursal (context-based). This section is for reference and memorization; the key point is that all these categories revolve around the concept of a valid relation linking the figurative usage to the original meaning.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-ḥaqīqah): A word used in its original meaning according to the convention of discourse.
الحقيقة اللغوية (al-ḥaqīqah al-lughawiyyah): Haqiqah based on original language coining.
الحقيقة العرفية العامة (al-ḥaqīqah al-'urfiyyah al-'āmmah): Haqiqah based on general custom among people.
الحقيقة العرفية الخاصة (al-ḥaqīqah al-'urfiyyah al-khāṣṣah): Haqiqah based on a specific technical convention, not the Law.
الحقيقة الشرعية (al-ḥaqīqah al-shar'iyyah): Haqiqah based on Sharī'ah terminology.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification, under which type of haqiqah does the word 'ṣalāh' (prayer) fall?
A) Haqiqah lughawiyyah
B) Haqiqah 'urfiyyah 'āmmah
C) Haqiqah 'urfiyyah khāṣṣah
D) Haqiqah shar'iyyah

Key Takeaway
The type of haqiqah a word carries depends on the discourse convention that assigns its meaning.

Majaz: Figurative Meaning and Its Ilaqat in Hanafi Usul
Definition and classification of majaz and its ilaqat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define majaz, state the condition of a valid relation (ilaqah), and identify the major classifications of ilaqat (similarity and adjacency).

Core Idea
Majaz is a word used in a non-original meaning connected by a valid relation; without relation, it's an error.

This lesson explores majaz (figurative speech) in Hanafi usul. The key requirement for a valid figurative usage is the existence of a relation (ilaqah) between the original and new meanings. The text presents various enumerations of these relations, ultimately converging on two fundamental types: similarity (istiarah) and adjacency (majaz mursal).

Guided Path
1
Define majaz
According to the text, majaz is a word used in other than its original meaning with a relation. Without a relation, it is an error (khaṭa').
Relation is the defining feature of majaz.
2
Understand the necessity of ilaqah
The passage stresses that without a valid relation, the usage is incorrect. This distinguishes rhetorical majaz from mere mistake.
The ilaqah connects the literal and figurative meanings.
3
List the major classifications of ilaqat
The text lists several enumerations: 25 types from al-Sayyid al-Muḥaqqiq, 12 from al-Minhāj, 5 from al-Mukhtaṣar, 4 from al-Badī', and finally 2 from the Hanafi mashāyikh: similarity (mushābahah) and adjacency (mujāwarah).
All these reduce to the two main categories of balāghah: isti'ārah (similarity) and majāz mursal (adjacency).
4
Identify examples of ilaqat
From the enumerated list, pick examples: causality (e.g., rain for growth), part-whole, opposition, etc. Recognize that similarity includes metaphors, and adjacency includes metonymy.
Focus on understanding the concept of ilaqah rather than memorizing all types.

Commentary
This chapter defines two foundational categories of word usage in Hanafi usul: haqiqah (literal) and majaz (figurative). Key Terms
المجاز (al-majāz): A word used in a meaning other than its original, with a valid relation (علاقة).
العلاقة (al-‘ilāqah): The connection between literal and figurative meanings that validates the figurative usage.
المشابهة (al-mushābahah): Relation of similarity; basis for isti'ārah (metaphor).
المجاورة (al-mujāwarah): Relation of adjacency or contiguity; basis for majāz mursal (metonymy).

The Requirement of Hearing for Figurative Usage in Hanafi Usul
Debate on the necessity of hearing particulars for metaphor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on whether each metaphorical usage must be heard from the Arabs and evaluate the opposing proof.

Core Idea
Hanafis hold that figurative usage does not require transmission of each specific instance; knowledge of the relation suffices.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the validity of metaphorical interpretation (majaz) in legal texts depends on whether each figure of speech must be attested from classical Arabic or can be derived through recognized semantic relations. This lesson examines the dispute between the majority of Hanafis and a minority who insisted on transmission of each particular metaphorical usage. You will learn why the chosen view (mukhtar) emphasizes the role of the 'relation' (ilaqah) and why empirical induction shows the necessity of that knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
Determine what condition is being debated regarding the use of metaphorical expressions (majaz).
The issue is whether 'hearing of particulars' (sama' al-juz'iyyat) is required.
2
State the Hanafi chosen view
State the Hanafi position: hearing of particulars is not required; what matters is knowledge of the relation (ilaqah) and qarina.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The text argues that if metaphor were purely transmitted, knowledge of the relation would be unnecessary, but we clearly need it. Additionally, Arabs coin new metaphors and didn't compile dictionaries of metaphors.
This is a logical argument (dalil aqli).
4
Examine the opposing position and response
A minority requires transmission. The text distinguishes between the coiner (wadi') needing the relation and the user (mutajawwiz). The Hanafi response asserts that the user also needs the relation.
The passage ends mid-argument; we rely on the partial text.
5
Apply the difference in practice
Understand how this affects a Hanafi jurist interpreting a legal text: he may accept a figurative meaning if a valid relation exists, even without a direct classical precedent.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.

مسألة المختار أنه لا يشترط سماع الجزئيات) لأنواع المجاز بحسب العلاقات خلافاً لشرذمة قليلة (وإلا) أي وإن شرط التوقف أهل العربية في التجوز على النقل) في كل جزئي جزئي وهم لا يتوقفون عليه (بل) يستعملون مجازات متجددة لم تسمع ويعدّون اختراع المجاز فضلاً (ولذلك لم يدوّنوا المجازات تدوينهم الحقائق) ولو كانت جزئيات المجاز نقلية لدوّنوها أيضاً (واستدل على المختار بأنه لو كان المجاز (نقلياً لما افتقر في التجوز (إلى العلم بالعلاقة) بين المعاني الحقيقية والمجازية إذ السماع كافٍ في الاستعمال والتالي باطل، لأنا نفتقر إلى معرفة العلاقة قطعاً وفيه أن المتفق عليه افتقار الواضع إلى العلم بالعلاقة (لا افتقار المتجوز) فإن أريد أنه لو كان نقلياً لما افتقر الواضع إلى العلم بالعلاقة فالملازمة ممنوعة، فإنه إنما يحتاج إلى تعيين الاسم من بين الأسماء وإن أراد لما افتقر المتجوز فالملازمة مسلمة وبطلان اللازم ممنوع فإنه غير مفتقر عند القائل بسماع الجزئيات هذا والمختار الشق الثاني من الشقين والمتجوز محتاج إلى العلم بالقرينة والعلاقة بالضرورة الاستقرائية والمنع مكابرة الشارطون لسماع الجزئيات (قالوا :أولاً: لو لم يجب النقل في استعمال المجاز (بل استقل
Issue: The chosen view is that hearing the particulars (of types of metaphor according to relations) is not required, contrary to a small group. (Otherwise, i.e., if it were a condition), then the Arabic scholars would rely on transmission for each particular usage, but they do not rely on it; rather, they use new metaphors that have not been heard and they consider the invention of metaphor a virtue. That is why they did not compile metaphors as they compiled literal usages. If the particulars of metaphor were transmitted, they would have compiled them as well. They argued for the chosen view that if metaphor were transmitted, then the one using metaphor would not need to know the relation between the literal and figurative meanings, because hearing would suffice for usage; but the consequent is false because we definitely need to know the relation. There is an objection: it is agreed that the coiner needs knowledge of the relation, not the one using the metaphor. If it is meant that if it were transmitted, the coiner would not need to know the relation, then the implication is denied, for he needs to assign the word from among words; if it is meant that the user would not need it, then the implication is granted but the falsity of the consequent is denied, for he is not in need according to the one who holds hearing of particulars. The chosen view is the second branch of the two: the user of metaphor needs knowledge of the qarina and relation by inductive necessity; denial is obstinacy. Those who require hearing of particulars say: First, if transmission were not necessary in using metaphor, but rather independent...
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents a debate in Hanafi usul about a condition for valid metaphorical usage: must each specific metaphorical expression be heard (transmitted) from the original Arab speakers? The chosen view (mukhtar) is that such hearing of particulars is not required, contrary to a minority. The evidence is that Arab scholars themselves create new metaphors and consider it a merit, and they did not compile lists of metaphors as they did for literal meanings. The main proof for the chosen view is that if metaphor were purely transmitted, knowledge of the 'relation' (ilaqah) between literal and figurative meanings would be unnecessary, but we clearly need that knowledge. An opponent's objection distinguishes between the coiner (wadi') needing the relation to coin, not the user; the response clarifies that the user also needs the relation to understand and use the metaphor properly, by inductive necessity. The text also cites the opposition's first argument but cuts off. The overall thrust is that the Hanafi position emphasizes the role of rational relation over mere transmission, allowing flexibility in legal interpretation and ijtihad.

Key Terms
سماع الجزئيات (sama' al-juz'iyyat): The condition that each specific instance of metaphorical usage must have been heard (transmitted) from Arabic speakers to be valid
العلاقة (al-ilaqah): The recognized relation between the literal and figurative meanings that makes the metaphor valid
المتجوز (al-mutajawwiz): The one who uses a word in a figurative sense

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the chosen view (al-mukhtar) in this passage, what does a person using a metaphor (mutajawwiz) need to know?
A) That the metaphor has been transmitted from the Arabs
B) The relation (ilaqah) between literal and figurative meanings and the qarina
C) The exact original context of the word
D) Nothing; any usage is permissible

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a valid metaphorical usage does not require transmission of the specific instance; knowledge of the relation (ilaqah) and the presence of a qarina are sufficient.

Definition and Types of Majaz in Hanafi Usul
Defines majaz and lists relationship types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define majaz, identify the essential condition of a valid ʿalaqa, and explain why the multiple counts of ʿalaqāt reduce to similarity and contiguity.

Core Idea
Majaz requires a recognized relationship between literal and metaphorical meanings; all such relationships ultimately reduce to similarity or contiguity.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, words can be used in their original meaning (haqiqa) or in a transferred meaning (majaz). This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut defines both and surveys the different types of relationships that justify such transfer. Understanding these relationships is crucial for correctly interpreting legal texts and deriving rulings.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish haqiqa from majaz
Identify the definitions given: haqiqa sharʿiyya is a word used in the meaning assigned by the Lawgiver (e.g., ṣalāh for prayer); majaz is a word used in other than its assigned meaning with a relationship (ʿalaqa).
The passage opens with 'nʿam, ḥaqīqa (sharʿiyya)... wa al-majāz hiya al-kalima al-mustaʿmala fī ghayr al-mawḍūʿ lahu biʿalāqa'.
2
Identify the essential condition for majaz
The text explicitly states that without a relationship, the usage is an error. Thus, the ʿalaqa is mandatory for valid majaz.
The phrase 'wa in kāna min ghayr ʿalāqa kāna khaṭa'' is key.
3
List the different counts of relationships
The passage gives several counts: 25 (in the marginalia of al-Sayyid al-Muḥaqqiq), 12 (in al-Minhāj), 5 (in al-Mukhtaṣar), 4 (in al-Badīʿ), and 2 (from 'our shaykhs').
The counts reflect different scholarly enumerations but are not contradictory.
4
Trace the reduction to two
The author resolves the multiplicity by noting that all these counts reduce to two universal categories: similarity (mushābaha) and contiguity (mujāwara). This aligns with the science of balāgha (rhetoric), where majaz is either istiʿāra (simile-based) or mursal (metonymy).
The concluding sentence: 'wa hādhā kulluhu radd ilā al-ijmāl wa lā tanāquḍ... fa-laysa li al-majāz al-lughawī fī shay''
5
Apply the concept to a legal text
Consider a word like 'yad' (hand) in a Qur'anic verse. If used literally, it refers to the limb; if used metaphorically for 'power' or 'control', a valid ʿalaqa (e.g., contiguity) must be present. The Hanafi mufti identifies the ʿalaqa to determine the intended meaning.
This illustrates the practical importance of the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم، حقيقة (شرعية) إن كان الواضع واصطلاح التخاطب الشرع كالصلاة، والمجاز هي) أي الكلمة (المستعملة في غير الموضوع له بعلاقة) وإن كان من غير علاقة كان خطأ (وهي خمسة وعشرون نوعاً كما في حاشية السيد المحقق قدس سره (للمختصر) السببية، المسببية الكلية والجزئية، ويشترط فيهما أن يكون للكل اسم على حدة وينتفي بانتفاء الجزء عرفاً الملزومية اللازمية ،الإطلاق التقييد العموم ،الخصوص الحالية المحلية المجاورة، الكون فيه، الأول إليه البدلية ،الآلية التشبيه التضاد عموم النكرة في حيز الإثبات، استعمال المعرف باللام في المعهود الذهني حذف المضاف حذف المضاف إليه الحذف مطلقاً الزيادة، ثم إن في النكرة العامة في الإثبات لا تتحقق علاقة مغايرة للتشبيه وكذا في المعرف في العهد الذهني تشبيه الفرد المبهم بالواحد المعين وأقسام الحذف والزيادة ليست من العلاقات للمجاز اللغوي في شيء (وقيل اثنا عشر كما في المنهاج السببية وهي أربعة أنواع الفاعلية، والمادية، والصورية، والغائية المسببية المشابهة معنوية كانت أو صورية المضادة الكلية، الجزئية، الاستعداد الكون فيه المجاورة، الزيادة النقصان التعلق الحاصل بين المصدر واسم المفعول، وبهذه العلاقة يصح إطلاق اسم الفاعل على اسم المفعول وعكسه، وإطلاق المصدر على اسم الفاعل وعكسه، وإطلاقه على اسم المفعول وعكسه كذا في بعض شروح المنهاج وفيه أيضاً أن علاقة الكون فيه ساقطة عن أكثر نسخه (وقيل) في «المختصر» (خمسة) المشاكلة والمشابهة والكون فيه والأول إليه والمجاورة (وقيل) في «البديع» (أربعة) وهي الأخيرة وهذا كله رد إلى الإجمال ولا تناقض كما حصرها مشايخنا في الاثنين المشابهة والمجاورة وهذا كما قال علماء البيان المجاز استعارة ومجاز مرسل.
Yes, a reality (legal) if the assigner and the convention of discourse is the Shari'ah like prayer, and the metaphor is the word used in other than its assigned meaning with a relationship, and if without relationship then it is an error. And it has twenty-five types as in the marginalia of al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq (may his secret be sanctified) on al-Mukhtasar: causality, effectuality, universality and particularity, and it is required in both that the whole has a separate name and it ceases with the cessation of the part customarily, necessary concomitance, absoluteness, restriction, generality, particularity, state, place, adjacency, being in it, first to it, substitution, instrumentality, similarity, opposition, generality of an indefinite in positive context, use of the definite article for mental reference, omission of the construct, omission of the constructee, omission absolutely, addition. Then indeed in the indefinite general in positive context no relation other than similarity is actualized, and similarly in the definite article for mental reference it is a similarity of an ambiguous individual to a specific one. And the categories of omission and addition are not among the relations for linguistic metaphor at all. (And it was said twelve as in al-Minhaj: causality which has four types: effective, material, formal, final; effectuality; similarity whether conceptual or formal; opposition; universality; particularity; disposition; being in it; adjacency; addition; subtraction; the connection between the verbal noun and the passive participle; and with this relation it is valid to apply the active participle to the passive participle and vice versa, and to apply the verbal noun to the active participle and vice versa, and to apply it to the passive participle and vice versa; as in some commentaries on al-Minhaj; and in it also that the relation of being in it is dropped from most of its copies.) (And it was said in al-Mukhtasar: five: similarity, likeness, being in it, first to it, adjacency.) (And it was said in al-Badi': four: the last ones.) And all this is a reduction to the summary and no contradiction; just as our shaykhs limited it to two: similarity and adjacency. And this is as the scholars of eloquence said: metaphor is either a simile or a metonymy.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage defines two key terms in usul al-fiqh: haqiqa shar'iyya (legal reality) where the Lawgiver assigns a word to a new meaning (e.g., salat for the ritual prayer), and majaz (metaphor) where a word is used for a meaning other than its original assignment due to a relation (علاقة). The author then lists the various types of relations that justify metaphorical usage, citing different counts from different scholars (25, 12, 5, 4, 2). He resolves the apparent discrepancy by explaining that all these counts ultimately reduce to two universal relations: similarity (مشابهة) and contiguity (مجاورة), which correspond to the rhetorical categories of isti'ara (simile-based metaphor) and mursal (metonymy). Hanafi usul accepts all these relations as valid bases for majaz.

Key Terms
حقيقة شرعية (haqiqa shar'iyya): A word used in the meaning assigned by the Lawgiver (e.g., salat for the ritual prayer)
مجاز (majaz): A word used in other than its assigned meaning with a relationship (علاقة)
علاقة ('alaqa): The relation between the literal and metaphorical meaning, e.g., causality, similarity, etc.
مشابهة (mushābaha): Relation of similarity between metaphorical and literal meanings
مجاورة (mujāwara): Relation of contiguity between metaphorical and literal meanings

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the minimum condition for a word to be considered majaz (metaphor)?
A) It must be used in a meaning other than its original by prior transmission (naql)
B) It must be used with a recognized relation (علاقة) between meanings
C) It must be a haqiqa shar'iyya
D) It must be a type of isti'ara

Key Takeaway
Majaz is defined by the presence of a recognized 'alaqa; all valid 'alaqat ultimately fall under similarity or contiguity.

Transmission of Majaz: The Dispute on Samā' al-Juz'iyyāt
A dispute in usul al-fiqh on the necessity of transmission for majaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two positions on whether specific metaphorical usages require prior transmission, and apply the Hanafi reasoning involving the preventer (māni') and the role of generic assignment (wad' naw').

Core Idea
A new metaphor is valid if the relation is clear and no natural preventer blocks it; transmission of every instance is not required.

In this passage, the author discusses a key usul dispute: must each metaphorical usage be heard from the Arabs (سماع الجزئيات), or can the relation (علاقة) alone justify its coinage? The preferred Hanafi view takes the latter, but a minority insists on transmission. The argument unfolds with objections about absurd applications and accusations of qiyas or invention, and the Hanafi response introduces the concepts of a preventer (مانع) and general knowledge via induction. A marginal note (حاشية) adds a crucial refinement: even Hanafis need a generic assignment (وضع نوعي) for correct composition, meaning some pattern must be transmitted, though not each instance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing views
State the Hanafi position (no need for transmission of instances) and the minority position (requires samā' al-juz'iyyāt). Note that the Hanafi view is called al-mukhtār.
The minority is described as شرذمة قليلة (a very small group).
2
Analyze the first objection and response
The opponents argue that if relation alone sufficed, absurd metaphors like calling a tall non-human 'tall date-palm' would be valid. The Hanafi response introduces the preventer (māni'), such as natural aversion, that blocks such usage.
The text says: 'قلنا الملازمة ممنوعة، بل يصح إذا لم يمنع مانع'.
3
Analyze the second objection and response
Opponents claim that without transmission, using a word metaphorically would be either qiyas in language (invalid) or pure invention. The Hanafi response: it is not invention because the general assignment (الوضع الكلي) is known by induction, and intellectual indication (دلالة عقلية) with a qarina suffices.
Al-Jawnpuri is cited; the Hashiya later qualifies this.
4
Examine the Hashiya refinement
The Hashiya argues that intellectual indication alone does not guarantee correct composition; a generic assignment (وضع نوعي) is needed, which requires hearing or permission of the pattern from the Arabs. This implies some transmission is necessary, but not of every instance.
The Hashiya says: 'لكن لا بد لصحة التركيب من أمر زائد، فلا بد من السماع والإجازة منهم للاستعمال حتى يكون جارياً على قوانينهم وهذا هو الوضع النوعي'.
5
Synthesize the preferred view
The overall Hanafi position: transmission of particulars is not required, but a generic assignment (wad' naw') of the pattern is needed, which itself may rely on transmission of examples of that pattern. The dispute thus shifts to what kind of transmission is necessary.
This nuance is praised as 'كلام متين عند المنصف الحاذق'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة المختار أنه لا يشترط سماع الجزئيات) لأنواع المجاز بحسب العلاقات خلافاً لشرذمة قليلة (وإلا) أي وإن شرط التوقف أهل العربية في التجوز على النقل) في كل جزئي جزئي وهم لا يتوقفون عليه (بل) يستعملون لو لم تمنع القرينة عن إرادة (الملزوم) الموضوع له إلى اللازم المتعلق به لكن ههنا الدلالة بالعقل، والانتقال بالقرينة، وحينئذ لا ،اختراع، وعلى هذا لا يحتاج إلى النقل أصلاً لا في الجزئيات ولا في الكليات، وقد التزمه الجونفوري، ولعل هذا خرق للإجماع، قال في «الحاشية»: ولك أن تقول إن الدلالة العقلية تكفي للمفهومية، والقرينة للمرادية، لكن لا بد لصحة التركيب من أمر زائد، فلا بد من السماع والإجازة منهم للاستعمال حتى يكون جارياً على قوانينهم وهذا هو الوضع النوعي، وحينئذ لا مخلص عن القول بالوضع، وهذا كلام متين عند المنصف الحاذق .
Issue: The preferred view is that hearing of particulars is not required for types of metaphor according to the relations, contrary to a very small group. (Otherwise) meaning if the condition of the Arabic speakers is to stop at transmission in every individual instance, while they do not stop at it. Rather, they use novel metaphors that have not been heard and consider inventing metaphor a virtue. (Therefore they did not record metaphors as they recorded realities.) If instances of metaphor were transmitted, they would have recorded them as well. (And the proof for the preferred view is that if metaphor were transmitted, it would not require knowledge of the relation between real and metaphorical meanings, since hearing suffices for use. The consequent is false, because we certainly need knowledge of the relation. And in it, it is agreed that the assigner needs knowledge of the relation, not the one using the metaphor. Because if it were transmitted, the assigner would not need knowledge of the relation; the implication is denied. He only needs to select the name from among the names. If he meant the user of the metaphor, the implication is granted but the falsity of the consequent is denied, because he does not need it according to the one who holds the hearing of particulars. And the preferred view is the second of the two branches: the user of metaphor needs knowledge of the qarina and the relation by inductive necessity, and denial is obstinacy.) (They said: First: If transmission were not required in using metaphor, and the relation alone suffices, then metaphor would be valid wherever the relation exists, and it would be valid to say 'tall date-palm' for a non-human also due to participation in tallness, and 'father' for a son and vice versa due to causality and effectuality. (We said: The implication is denied. Rather, it is valid if no preventer prevents. And failure due to a preventer does not undermine the completeness of the cause. So failure due to a preventer does not undermine the independence of the relation without need for hearing. (And perhaps that preventer is their text prohibiting it because it is very far from natural disposition) such that the mind does not transfer to it, so ponder. And it may be answered that the independence of the relation does not follow from the non-obligation of transmission; rather, the cause may be composed of it and something else, and perhaps that something else is absent here. And the author objected that there is no disagreement about the absence of something else; the disagreement is only about the sufficiency of the relation or considering transmission along with it, so ponder.) (And) they said (secondly): If transmission were not required in using metaphor, then using a word in other than its assignation would be a qiyas in language if it is for a commonality entailing a ruling, otherwise it would be invention and imagination. Both qiyas in language and invention are invalid, so hearing is necessary. (We said: We do not accept invention if it is not for a commonality entailing a ruling. Invention would only follow if the assignment for the meaning connected to what it was assigned for was not known, with one of the mentioned connections, by universal knowledge through induction. And here universal assignment is known, so there is no invention. (I say) conforming to what al-Jawnpuri answered: (And also invention would only follow if the word did not intellectually indicate the metaphorical meaning) even if the qarina did not prevent intending the entailed meaning assigned for to the necessary related to it, but here there is intellectual indication and transfer by qarina, and then there is no invention. And on this, transmission is not needed at all, neither in particulars nor in universals. And al-Jawnpuri adopted this, and perhaps this is a breach of consensus. He said in the Hashiya: You may say that intellectual indication suffices for understandability, and the qarina for intended meaning, but for correct composition an additional factor is needed, so hearing and permission from them for usage is necessary so that it conforms to their rules. This is specific assignment (wad' naw'i). Then there is no escape from the statement of assignment. And this is sound speech for the discerning sound-minded person.)
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute (مسألة) on whether specific instances of majaz require prior transmission (سماع) from Arabs or can be coined anew based on the relation (علاقة) alone. The preferred Hanafi view is that transmission is not required; Arabs invent new metaphors following recognized relations. The opponents argue that without transmission, any relation would allow absurd usages like calling a tall palm tree 'tall' for a non-human. The Hanafi response is that a preventer (مانع) such as natural aversion blocks such usage, so the relation alone is a valid basis unless prevented. Another objection claims that such coinage would be either invalid qiyas in language or pure invention. The Hanafis reply that it is not invention because the general assignment is known by induction (استقراء) and the intellectual indication (دلالة عقلية) with a qarina suffices. However, a marginal note (حاشية) counters that intellectual indication alone does not guarantee correct composition; a type of generic assignment (وضع نوعي) is still needed, requiring transmission of the pattern but not each instance. This nuanced position is praised as sound. The passage shows the depth of Hanafi usul debate on the nature of linguistic convention.

Key Terms
سماع الجزئيات (samā' al-juz'iyyāt): The requirement that each specific metaphorical usage be heard (transmitted) from native speakers
علاقة ('alāqa): The relation between the literal and metaphorical meaning that justifies the metaphor
وضع نوعي (wad' naw'i): A generic assignment of a pattern, not each individual word, required for correct linguistic composition
مانع (māni'): A preventer that blocks an otherwise valid metaphorical usage, such as natural aversion of the mind

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view as refined by the Hashiya, what is required for a new metaphorical usage to be valid?
A) The exact phrase must be transmitted from the Arabs
B) The relation (علاقة) alone suffices, no transmission needed
C) The relation must exist, no preventer, and the generic pattern (وضع نوعي) must be attested
D) The metaphor must be recorded in early dictionaries

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position on majaz requires knowledge of the relation and absence of a preventer for individual coinages, but the Hashiya further demands a generic assignment (وضع نوعي) of the pattern, implying that some transmission of linguistic patterns is still necessary.

Truth of Negation as a Sign of Majāz
Explaining the sign of truthful negation to identify majāz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the sign of sidq al-nafy to determine whether a usage is majāz or haqīqah, and explain the resolution of the objection concerning parts and necessary concomitants using primary predication.

Core Idea
If you can truthfully negate the literal meaning from the usage, it is majāz; if not, it is haqīqah.

This passage introduces one of the classical signs (amārāt) used by Ḥanafī uṣūlīs to distinguish majāz from ḥaqīqah: ṣidq al-nafy, the truth of negation. The idea is straightforward: if the literal meaning can be truthfully denied of the thing intended, then the word is being used metaphorically. For example, calling a dull person a 'donkey' is majāz because you can truthfully say 'he is not a donkey' in the literal sense. However, the text also addresses a subtle objection concerning words used for a part or necessary consequent of the original meaning, and refines the criterion with the concept of primary predication (al-ḥaml al-awwalī). Understanding this sign is crucial for analyzing Quranic and legal language.

Guided Path
1
State the sign
Identify the sign of sidq al-nafy: if you can truthfully negate the haqīqī meaning from the word's usage, the usage is majāz.
Example: 'He is not a donkey' (said of a dullard) shows 'donkey' is majāz.
2
Apply the converse
If negation is false (i.e., you cannot truthfully negate the literal meaning), the usage is haqīqah.
Example: You cannot truthfully say a dullard 'is not a human', so 'human' is haqīqah.
3
Understand the objection
When the usage is for a part or necessary consequent of the literal meaning, negating the literal meaning from the usage seems invalid, yet the usage is still majāz.
E.g., 'hand' used for 'power' – the literal meaning 'hand' cannot be negated from 'power'? Actually, the passage clarifies the issue.
4
Apply primary predication
The resolution: negation should be considered under primary predication (al-ḥaml al-awwalī), i.e., 'the whole is not the part'. Thus, truthfully negating the literal meaning from the usage is valid even for parts/necessaries.
The whole is not identical to its part, so 'power is not hand' is true in primary predication.
5
Avoid overextension
Be careful not to apply the sign to universals (kullī) applied to particulars; e.g., 'animal' for Zayd – you can truthfully say 'Zayd is not animal' in primary predication, but it is haqīqah, not majāz. The negation here is between two different things (kullī and juz'ī), not between the literal meaning and the usage.
The sign applies only when negating the literal meaning from the intended referent, not when negating a universal from an individual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة (للمجاز أمارات بها يستدل على المجازية (منها صدق النفي) أي صدق نفي المعنى الحقيقي عن المستعمل فيه كقولك للبليد : ليس بحمار علم أن الحمار مجاز فيه (وعكسه) أي عدم صدق نفي المعنى الحقيقي عنه (دليل الحقيقة) فيه فليس يصح للبليد ليس بإنسان) فالإنسان حقيقة فيه ويشكل بالمستعمل في الجزء أو اللازم فإنه لا يصح النفي) أي نفي الجزء أو اللازم (ولا حقيقة اعلم أن عدم صحة نفي المعنى الحقيقي أمارة الحقيقة، وفي المستعمل في الجزء أو اللازم المعنى المجازي هو الجزء أو اللازم، فعدم صحة نفيه لا يكون إشكالاً، فالأولى أن يقال : المستعمل في الكل أو الملزوم فإنه لا يصح نفي الحقيقي هو الجزء أو اللازم ثم إنه هل يرد
