باعتبار الحمل الحقيقي) الأولي فإنه ليس الكل نفس الجزء ، ولا الملزوم نفس اللازم، والمراد بصحة النفي وعدمها صحته وعدمها باعتبار الحمل الأولي، فإنه إذا صح النفي باعتبار الحمل الأولي علم أنه مغاير للموضوع له، فعلمت المجازية وإلا يصير حقيقة (أقول : بل فيه إشكال، فإن هذا عكس المجاز، ولا يمكن أخذ النفي هناك باعتبار حمل الشيء على نفسه) حتى يكون الحاصل أن صحة نفي الحمل الأولي بين الحقيقي والمستعمل فيه يوجب المجازية (وإلا) أي وإن كان النفي المعتبر بهذا الحمل الحقيقي يلزم أن يكون قولك لزيد حيوان مجازاً) أي إطلاق الحيوان على زيد بأن يراد منه كما إذا رأيت زيداً فأخبرت بقولك رأيت حيواناً يكون إطلاقاً مجازياً، لأنه يصح النفي هناك باعتبار الحمل الحقيقي، فإن زيداً ليس نفس الحيوان، فيلزم كونه مجازاً، وهو باطل، فإن إطلاق الكلي على فرده حقيقة هذا ولم يرد أنه لو كان المعتبر الحمل الحقيقي لكان زيد حيوان مجازاً، لأنه يصدق النفي ههنا باعتبار الحمل الحقيقي حتى يرد عليه أن المعتبر في المسألة صدق نفي المعنى الحقيقي عن المستعمل فيه، وههنا لا يصح نفي الحيوانية عن الحيوان ولا نفي زيد عن زيد حتى يكون منهما، بل إنما يصح نفي الحيوان عن زيد، وبهذا لا تلزم المجازية فهذا النفي خارج عن المسألة فتدبر (فتأمل) فإنه دقيق (ثم
Question: Majāz has signs by which one can infer its metaphorical nature. Among them is the truth of negation, i.e., the truth of negating the real meaning from the thing used in; e.g., saying to a dullard 'He is not a donkey' indicates that 'donkey' is metaphorical in that usage. Its opposite, i.e., the non-truth of negating the real meaning from it, indicates it is real; e.g., it is not valid to say to a dullard 'He is not a human', so 'human' is real in him. An objection is raised concerning the usage in a part or necessary consequent; for negation of the part or necessary is not valid, nor is it real. Know that the non-validity of negating the real meaning is a sign of reality, and in the usage in a part or necessary, the metaphorical meaning is the part or necessary, so the non-validity of its negation is not a problem. Rather, it should be said: When used for the whole or the original, negation of the real meaning (which is the part or necessary) is not valid. Then, does this objection invalidate the sign of majāz? It is said no, because it is not impossible for a sign of something to be absent while the thing is present, since it may be a specific sign not comprehensive. The author objected that this specific sign is comprehensive, because they said its absence is a sign of reality, and this would not be valid unless it is comprehensive for majāz. So this objection also applies. Understand. It is said there is no problem, because negation of the original meaning (which is the part or necessary) from the thing used in (which is the whole or original) - even if not valid according to customary predication, it is valid according to real primary predication; for the whole is not the same as the part, nor the original the same as the necessary. What is meant by the validity and non-validity of negation is according to primary predication. If negation is valid according to primary predication, it is known that the usage is other than the original, so majāz is known; otherwise it is real. I say: There is a problem, for this reverses majāz. One cannot take negation there according to predicating a thing of itself, so that the result would be that validity of negation of primary predication between the real and the thing used in necessitates majāz. Otherwise, if negation is considered according to this real predication, saying 'Zayd is an animal' would be majāz, because negation is valid there according to primary predication, since Zayd is not the same as animal. This would necessitate it being majāz, which is false, because applying a universal to its particular is real. This is not intended. Rather, the objector means: if the considered matter were the truth of negating the real meaning from the thing used in, here it is not valid to negate animality from animal or Zayd from Zayd such that it would be from them; rather, it is only valid to negate animal from Zayd, and this does not entail majāz, so this negation is outside the issue. So reflect, for it is subtle.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage discusses one of the signs (amārāt) of majāz: sidq al-nafy (truth of negation). If you can truthfully negate the literal meaning from the word's usage, that usage is metaphorical. For example, calling a dull person a 'donkey'—you can truthfully say 'he is not a donkey' in the literal sense, so it's metaphorical. Conversely, if negation is false (e.g., you cannot say 'he is not a human'), then it's real (haqīqah). An objection arises when the word is used for a part or necessary concomitant of the original meaning. For instance, if 'hand' is used for 'power', the metaphorical meaning is the necessary, and negating the literal meaning (hand) from the usage (power) is valid? Actually, the text explains that the objection is about usage for the whole (when the metaphorical meaning is a part). The answer involves considering primary predication: the whole is not identical to its part, so negation is valid under primary predication. However, a subtle issue emerges: applying a universal (like 'animal') to a particular (Zayd) is real, not metaphorical, and here negation is valid in primary predication. But the sign is about negating the literal meaning from the usage, not about negating the subject from itself. So the sign works when the negation is between the literal meaning and the thing used for, not between a universal and its instance. The author calls for reflection.

Key Terms
صدق النفي (sidq al-nafy): Truth of negation; the sign that if one can truthfully negate the literal meaning from the usage, the usage is metaphorical.
الحمل الأولي (al-ḥaml al-awwalī): Primary predication; predicating something of another in an essential sense, e.g., 'the whole is not the part'.
الكلي (al-kullī): Universal term; applying to all individuals of a category, e.g., 'animal' for Zayd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the sign of sidq al-nafy not make 'Zayd is an animal' into majāz?
A) Because 'animal' is a universal and Zayd is a particular, and negation is invalid.
B) Because the negation here is not between the literal meaning and the usage, but between a universal and its instance.
C) Because the literal meaning of 'animal' cannot be negated from Zayd.
D) Because primary predication does not apply to this case.

Key Takeaway
The sign of sidq al-nafy is a powerful tool for identifying majāz, but it must be applied with the proper understanding of primary predication and only when the negation is between the literal meaning and the intended referent, not between other logical relations.

Critique of Indicators for Metaphorical Usage
Critiques of proposed indicators for identifying majaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the validity of proposed indicators (amarat) for identifying majaz and identify which ones fail due to homonymy or circularity.

Core Idea
No single linguistic indicator can definitively prove metaphorical usage; each is undermined by homonymy, circularity, or arbitrariness.

Distinguishing literal (haqiqah) from metaphorical (majaz) usage is a foundational task in usul al-fiqh, as it affects how legal texts are interpreted. Hanafi usulis have proposed several indicators (amarat) to perform this distinction. This passage from 'Fawatih al-Rahmut' subjects these indicators to rigorous critique, revealing their limitations and demonstrating the need for a holistic approach. We will walk through the critique of three main indicators: negation of all literal meanings, failure of immediate mental attendance, and lack of consistency in usage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the indicators under critique
Locate the three proposed amarat: salb al-kull (negating all literal meanings), 'adam al-tabadur (non-immediacy of meaning), and 'adam al-ittarad (lack of consistency).
These are discussed sequentially in the Arabic text.
2
Analyze the circularity objection against salb al-kull
Understand why proving majaz by negating all literal meanings is circular: it presupposes the very metaphorical status it aims to prove.
The text calls this 'musadarah' (circular reasoning).
3
Examine the rebuttal and its counter-critique
Follow the dialectic: an opponent claims salb al-kull entails majaz without circularity, but the author shows that hesitation (taraddud) about the meaning prevents knowledge, making the indicator useless.
This is a typical Hanafi talil argument using epistemic conditions.
4
Evaluate the tabadur indicator in light of mushtarak
Note that the homonym (mushtarak) presents a problem: without context, either both meanings come to mind or neither, so the indicator fails to distinguish haqiqah from majaz.
The text discusses substitutive coming-to-mind as a possible salvage, but it is debated.
5
Critique the ittarad indicator with examples
Examine the Quranic example 'ask the village' (Yusuf 82) and the 'sakhi' example. The author argues that lack of consistency may be due to causes other than majaz, such as legal prohibition or absence of the underlying attribute.
Note the distinction between linguistic possibility and actual usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

المجازية فإنه إذا سلب واحد من المعاني الحقيقية عما استعمل فيه علم أنه مباين له وأن له معنيين فتلزم المجازية في أحدهما دفعاً للاشتراك) ولما كان المسلوب متعيناً لكونه حقيقة تعين المستعمل فيه لكونه مجازاً، ثم إن هذا الجواب لا ينطبق إذا أورد السؤال في الحقيقة، فإن عدم صحة سلب المعنى الحقيقي موقوف على كونه حقيقياً وسلب المطلق لا يجدي كذا قالوا وتعقب عليه المصنف وقال : يكفي عدم صحة سلب بعض المعاني القرينة وإن لم يتبادر المراد لكن يخطران في الذهن ولا يرد مجازية اللفظ الموضوع للمركب المستعمل فيه، لأنه يتبادر غيره وهو الجزء لأنه لا يتبادر من اللفظ نفسه، بل إنما يتبادر في ضمن تبادر الكل، وأيضاً : ليس أمارة المجاز تبادر الغير فقط، بل مع عدم تبادر نفسه هذا (ومنها عدم اطراده) يعني إذا استعمل لفظ في معنى لأجل وجود معنى فيه، ولا يطرد استعماله في غيره مع وجود ذلك المعنى الموجب فيه فيدل على أن الاستعمال مجازي فيه (نحو: ﴿وَسْثَلِ الْقَرْيَةَ [يوسف: ۸۲] دون واسأل البساط) مع اشتراكهما في وجه الاستعمال. (أقول : المنع ممنوع نعم لم يسمع) اسأل البساط وعدم السماع لا يدل على عدم الجواز، كيف وقد تقدم أنه لا يشترط سماع الجزئيات، وهذا إن تم فمناقشة في المثال فافهم (ولو سلم) المنع فلا يختص هذا المنع بالمجاز (إذ) المنع في البعض دون البعض مع الاشتراك في وجه الاستعمال من العلاقة تحكم والتحكم غير مختص) بالمجاز فيجوز أن يمنعوا استعمال لفظ في بعض أفراد الموضوع له دون البعض إلا تحكماً) وهو غير حجة وجوابه أنه يجوز في المجاز الاختلاف في الانتقال مع الاشتراك في العلاقة فما كان أبعد يمنعون فيه ولا تحكم بخلاف الحقيقة فإن الانتقال فيه لأجل الوضع لا يختلف في الإفراد، هذا بل (عرف) نحو : ﴿وَسْتَلِ الْقَرْيَةَ ﴾ [يوسف: (۸۲] بأنها لا تسأل بناء على أنه مجاز في الإسناد) فليس مما نحن فيه، وهذا أيضاً مناقشة في المثال ولا تنعكس هذه الأمارة حتى يكون الاطراد أمارة الحقيقة ( فإن المجاز قد يطرد فلا يكون أمارة الحقيقة (وأورد) عليه (السخي) فإنه حقيقة فيمن قام به السخاوة ولا يطرد إذ لا يطلق على الله تعالى مع أنه الجواد المطلق الجواب أنه ملكة (بالاستقراء والملكة أمر كسبي لا يتحقق فيه سبحانه، ولا يطلق لعدم وجود جهة الإطلاق فيه، وقد يجاب بأنه يجوز الإطلاق لغة وإنما لا يجوز شرعاً، لأن الأسماء توقيفية، ولا توقيف فيه أو لأنه موهم للمنقصة، وحينئذ لا یرد العلامة المرادفة للعلام أيضاً، هذا لا يقال عدم الاطراد، إنما يعلم بسببه لأنه ممكن غير محسوس والعلم به إنما يعلم من جهة العلم بالسبب لا يظهر لهذا وجه ظاهر والظاهر أنه من قلم الناسخ، والصحيح إنما يعلم من جهة سببه كما بين في المنطق، فحينئذ عدم الاطراد، إنما يعلم من جهة سببه ، ثم عدم الاطراد عدم ولا يكون سببه إلا عدم عدم المانع المستلزم لوجود المانع أو
The third discourse on linguistic principles: The two indicators were objected to that negating some literal meanings does not indicate the metaphorical nature of the usage, because the homonym used in one of its meanings is literal in it and it is valid to negate the other meaning, so it cannot be intended that negating some literal meanings is valid in the issue. The intention to negate all remains, and that is also invalid as he said: And negating all, i.e., negating all literal meanings, depends on the metaphorical nature of the metaphorical meaning; proving it thereby, i.e., proving the metaphorical by negating all, is a circularity. So the two indicators are not sound. And what was said in answer: suspension, i.e., the suspension of negating all on the metaphorical nature of the metaphorical is denied; rather, negating all entails metaphorical nature, so no circularity. I say: there is hesitation about the metaphorical nature, i.e., the metaphorical nature of the metaphorical necessitates hesitation in negating all because it is possible that this usage is also literal, and it cannot be valid. And hesitation and knowledge are opposites, and the absence of opposite is a condition for the existence of the other. So knowledge of negating all depends on the removal of hesitation, which depends on the metaphorical nature of the metaphorical. And one can deny the condition of the absence of opposite for the existence of the other; rather, the truth is to suffice in the objection that negating all is necessary for the metaphorical nature and equal to it in obscurity and hiddenness, rather more hidden, so it is not a valid indicator. This, and Allah knows best. Understand: an indication that the discussion is about proof, not reality, and that when one intends to obtain it through reflection, the assumption is when one does not know by another way, neither intuitively nor by a sign. And it is known that theoretical matters only occur where both possibilities exist. That only occurs in a word used correctly. Thus the objection of some excellencies to suspension is refuted, based on it being possible to know that this is not any of the literal meanings without knowing the metaphorical, based on the absence of a valid relationship in metaphor. And what al-Taftazani objected: that it is valid to negate the literal meanings of 'lion' about a human, and one does not know its usage in it, let alone metaphorical. And it was answered that negating some is sufficient to prove metaphorical nature, because if one literal meaning is negated from what it is used for, it is known that it is different and that it has two meanings, so metaphorical nature is necessitated in one of them to avoid homonymy. And since the negated meaning is determined to be literal, the usage is determined to be metaphorical. Then this answer does not apply if the question is about actual reality, because the validity of negating a literal meaning depends on it being literal, and absolute negation is useless - as they said. And the author criticized it and said: it is sufficient that negating some meanings is not valid, for it is literal in it to avoid uselessness. And this is insufficient, because if it is meant the absolute invalidity of negating some meanings, it does not necessitate it being literal nor does uselessness follow, because it could have another literal meaning that can be negated from it. And if it is meant the invalidity of negating literal meanings, it depends on knowing the literal meaning, and this is applicable whether the negation is considered as primary or conventional predication, as is not hidden from one of intelligence. So reflect. For if you say: this answer necessitates the metaphorical nature of the homonym, because of the validity of negating some literal meanings from the usage, he said: it does not necessitate the metaphorical nature of the homonym, because the discussion is about what is doubted whether it is literal in this meaning or not, and the homonym is known to be literal in both. And among the indicators of metaphor is that it itself does not come to mind first, but another does without the context, which is the opposite of the indicator of literalness: its indicator is that it comes to mind first without context, and another does not come to mind, but it itself comes to mind. And the homonym was objected due to the absence of an indicator of literalness in it, since the intended meaning does not come to mind without context. And it can be directed to an indicator of metaphor, for the homonym used metaphorically, another meaning does not come to mind from it without context. It was said: the second is correct and the first is false, for the departure of a specific property from what it is specific to is not impossible, because it may be non-comprehensive, and this is false because coming to mind in their view is a necessary concomitant of literalness. And the second would apply if it were conditioned that another meaning comes to mind for metaphor; if they sufficed with its non-coming to mind without context, this would hardly apply. And this only applies to the view of one who denies generality in the homonym, for according to him both come to mind without context; and this is according to one who considers it apparent in generality. The answer: it suffices that it comes to mind even by substitution, and substitutive coming to mind exists in the homonym. And perhaps it is answered that what is meant is coming to mind as a thought, and in the homonym devoid of context, though the intended meaning does not come to mind, both occur in the mind. And the metaphorical nature of a word assigned to a compound used in it is not objected, because another meaning comes to mind, which is the part, because it does not come to mind from the word itself, but rather comes to mind within the whole. Also, the indicator of metaphor is not merely that another comes to mind, but also that it itself does not come to mind. And among them is lack of consistency, meaning: if a word is used for a meaning due to the existence of a meaning in it, and its usage in others is not consistent despite the existence of that meaning causing it, then this indicates that the usage is metaphorical in it, like 'And ask the village' (Yusuf 82) but not 'and ask the carpet', despite their sharing in the aspect of usage. I say: the prohibition is prohibited; yes, it has not been heard: 'ask the carpet', and lack of hearing does not indicate lack of permissibility. How, since it has preceded that hearing of particulars is not required. And if this is accepted, it is a discussion of the example - understand. And if it is granted that the prohibition is specific, then this specificity is not exclusive to metaphor, because the prohibition in some cases without others despite the sharing in the aspect of usage from the relationship is arbitrary, and arbitrariness is not exclusive to metaphor; so it is permissible that they prohibit the use of a word in some individuals of the assigned meaning without others, except arbitrarily, which is not a proof. The answer: it is permissible in metaphor that there is difference in transfer despite the sharing in the relationship; what is more distant they prohibit, without arbitrariness, unlike literalness, because transfer in it is due to assignment and does not differ among individuals. Rather, it is known that 'And ask the village' is considered metaphorical in attribution, so it is not within our scope. This is also a discussion of the example. And this indicator does not have a converse such that consistency is an indicator of literalness, for metaphor may be consistent, so it is not an indicator of literalness. And it was objected to by 'al-Sakhi' (the generous), for it is literal in one in whom generosity resides, but it is not consistent as it is not applied to Allah, though He is absolutely generous. The answer: it is a disposition by induction, and disposition is an acquired matter not realized in Him, and it is not applied due to the absence of a basis for application. And it may be answered that it is linguistically permissible but not legally, because names are revelatory, and there is no revelation regarding it, or because it implies deficiency. Then there is no objection. This remark parallels another remark. It is said: lack of consistency is only known by its cause, because it is a possible non-sensible matter, and knowledge of it is only from knowledge of the cause - no apparent face for this, and it appears that it is from the scribe's pen. The correct is that it is known from its cause, as explained in logic. Then lack of consistency is only known from its cause, and lack of consistency is a negation, and its cause is nothing but the absence of the preventer which necessitates the existence of the preventer, or...
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage from the third discourse on linguistic principles in 'Fawatih al-Rahmut' critiques several proposed indicators (amarat) for identifying metaphorical usage (majaz). The author, a Hanafi usuli, systematically examines each indicator, showing its inadequacy or limitations. The first indicator examined is the negation of some literal meanings to prove majaz. The objection is that a mushtarak (homonym) used in one of its literal senses can validly have the other literal meaning negated, so this does not indicate majaz. The attempt to negate all literal meanings is then shown to be circular (musadarah), because negating all literal meanings depends on knowing that the usage is metaphorical, which is what we are trying to prove. The discussion then moves to the indicator of 'failure to come to mind first' (takaful), where the literal meaning comes to mind readily without context, while the metaphorical meaning does not. However, the mushtarak is problematic here because both meanings come to mind (or neither distinctly) without context. The later indicator of 'lack of consistency' (ittarad) is illustrated with the Quranic verse 'Ask the village' (Yusuf 82), which is a clear case of majaz (asking the inhabitants), and the absence of similar expressions like 'ask the carpet' suggests metaphorical usage. The author critiques this by noting that such prohibition may be arbitrary or due to other factors, not necessarily indicating majaz. He also brings the example of 'sakhi' (generous) which is literal for a person but not applied to Allah, yet this does not prove it is metaphorical in Allah's case; rather, the non-application is due to Allah not possessing acquired dispositions. Overall, the passage demonstrates the Hanafi usuli method of rigorous analytical critique, showing that no single indicator is foolproof. The commentary is careful to follow the text's order, explaining each objection and response.

Key Terms
المجاز (al-majaz): Metaphorical usage; use of a word in a meaning not originally assigned to it, based on a relationship (علاقة) and a contextual indicator (قرينة مانعة من إرادة الحقيقة).
الأمارة (al-amarah): A sign or indicator that helps determine whether a word is used literally or metaphorically; it is not definitive but probabilistic.
سلب الكل (salbu al-kull): Negation of all possible literal meanings of a word to prove that the usage is metaphorical.
التبادر (al-tabadur): The immediate coming to mind of a meaning when a word is uttered without any contextual indicator.
الاطراد (al-ittarad): Consistency in usage; a word being consistently used in a particular meaning across similar contexts.
المشترك (al-mushtarak): A homonym; a word with multiple distinct literal meanings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the passage argue that 'salb ba'd al-ma'ani al-haqiqiyyah' (negating some literal meanings) is not a valid indicator of majaz?
A) Because it would require knowing all literal meanings, which is impossible.
B) Because it can also apply to a mushtarak (homonym) where the word is literal in both meanings, so negating one does not prove the other is metaphorical.
C) Because the Quranic example of 'ask the village' shows that such negation is arbitrary.
D) Because the indicator only works for verbs, not nouns.

Key Takeaway
A valid indicator of majaz must avoid circularity, account for homonymy, and not rely on arbitrary or unattested usage; the Hanafi critique shows that no single amarah is foolproof.

The Indicator of Immediacy (Tabaddur) for Haqiqah and Majaz
Defines and analyzes al-tabaddur as an indicator for literal vs metaphorical meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that tabaddur (immediacy) is a necessary concomitant of haqiqah and identify when substitutive immediacy applies to common words.

Core Idea
Tabaddur is the sign of haqiqah; its absence indicates majaz, but a mushtarak retains haqiqah through substitutive immediacy.

This lesson explores the concept of tabaddur (immediate understanding) as a key indicator in Hanafi usul for distinguishing literal (haqiqah) from metaphorical (majaz) usage. The passage delves into how this applies to words with multiple meanings (mushtarak) and introduces the solution of substitutive immediacy (tabaddur badali). Understanding this rule is crucial for correctly interpreting legal texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Why does a mushtarak seem to lack an indicator of haqiqah? The intended meaning does not immediately come to mind without context.
This creates a challenge for the definition of haqiqah.
2
Recall the definition
Tabaddur is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of haqiqah in Hanafi usul. Absence of tabaddur suggests the usage is not haqiqah.
3
Explore the objection
Some argue that in a mushtarak, no single meaning immediately comes to mind, so the word would be majaz. This is the opposing view.
This objection targets the Hanafi position.
4
Apply the Hanafi solution
Substitutive immediacy (tabaddur badali) satisfies the condition: both meanings occur to the mind together, each representing the other. This preserves haqiqah.
5
Confirm the ruling
A mushtarak without qarinah is haqiqah for all its meanings due to substitutive immediacy.
This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشترك لعدم وجود أمارة الحقيقة فيه حيث لا يتبادر المراد عند عدم القرينة، ويمكن توجيهه إلى أمارة المجاز فإن المشترك المستعمل في المجازي لا يتبادر منه غيره لولا القرينة، قيل : الثاني صواب والأول فاسد، فإن خروج الخاصة عما هي خاصة له غير مستحيل، لجواز أن تكون غير شاملة وهذا فاسد لأن التبادر عندهم من لوازم الحقيقة والثاني يتوجه لو شرط تبادر غيره للمجازية : أما لو اكتفى على عدم تبادره لولا القرينة لم يكد يتوجه هذا (وهو إنما يرد على مذهب من نفى العموم في المشترك، فإنه عنده يتبادران عند عدم القرينة وهذا عند من يجعله ظاهراً في العموم، والجواب أنه يكفي التبادر ولو بدلاً) والتبادر البدلي موجود في المشترك وربما يجاب بأن المراد التبادر خطوراً، وفي المشترك المجرد عن القرينة وإن لم يتبادر المراد لكن يخطران في الذهن ولا يرد مجازية اللفظ الموضوع للمركب المستعمل فيه، لأنه يتبادر غيره وهو الجزء لأنه لا يتبادر من اللفظ نفسه، بل إنما يتبادر في ضمن تبادر الكل، وأيضاً : ليس أمارة المجاز تبادر الغير فقط، بل مع عدم تبادر نفسه هذا
The common term (mushtarak) because there is no indicator of literal meaning in it since the intended meaning does not immediately come to mind in the absence of a context, and it can be directed to an indicator of metaphorical meaning, for the common term used in a metaphorical sense does not immediately bring to mind anything other than it without a context. It was said: the second is correct and the first is invalid, because the departure of a specific meaning from what it is specific to is not impossible, as it is possible that it is not inclusive, and this is invalid because immediacy (tabaddur) in their view is a necessary concomitant of literal meaning, and the second would apply if they stipulated that something other than it immediately comes to mind for metaphoricalness. As for if they are content with the absence of its immediate coming to mind without a context, it hardly applies. This (objection) is only directed against the view of those who deny generality in the common term, for in their view both meanings come to mind in the absence of a context, while this is according to those who consider it apparently general. The answer is that immediacy suffices even if it is substitutive (badali), and substitutive immediacy exists in the common term. Perhaps it is answered that what is meant is immediacy as a passing thought (khuturan). In the common term devoid of context, even if the intended meaning does not immediately come to mind, both meanings occur to the mind. And the metaphoricalness of the word placed for a composite meaning used in it is not countered, because something else (the part) immediately comes to mind, since it does not immediately come to mind from the word itself, but rather it comes to mind within the immediate understanding of the whole. Also, the indicator of metaphorical meaning is not only that something else immediately comes to mind, but also that itself does not immediately come to mind. This.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage explains the amara of tabaddur (immediacy) as a sign to distinguish haqiqah (literal) from majaz (metaphorical). The author begins by noting that the mushtarak (common word) lacks an amara for haqiqah because without a qarinah (context) the intended meaning does not immediately come to mind, and it could be majaz. However, some argue that the absence of tabaddur is itself an amara for majaz. The author discusses an objection: if we define haqiqah as immediate understanding, then a mushtarak without qarinah would be majaz because no one meaning is immediately understood. But the response is that substitutive immediacy (tabaddur badali) suffices: in a mushtarak, both meanings occur to the mind, so the word is haqiqah for both. The passage also addresses the case of composite terms: when a word is used for a composite meaning, the part is understood not from the word alone but from the whole, so the immediate understanding of the part does not indicate that the word is majaz for the composite. Ultimately, the amara for majaz requires both: something else comes to mind and the intended meaning does not. This discussion is typical of Hanafi usul, where the conditions for haqiqah and majaz are carefully defined.

Key Terms
التبادر (al-tabaddur): Immediate coming to mind of a meaning from a word without the aid of a context (qarinah).
المشترك (al-mushtarak): A word that has multiple meanings due to original placement (wad') for each.
التبادر البدلي (al-tabaddur al-badali): Substitutive immediacy: when multiple meanings come to mind together, each in representation of the others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the complete indicator (amara) of metaphorical meaning (majaz)?
A) Something other than the intended meaning immediately comes to mind.
B) The intended meaning does not immediately come to mind.
C) Something else immediately comes to mind and the intended meaning does not.
D) Neither meaning immediately comes to mind.

Key Takeaway
Tabaddur is the necessary concomitant of haqiqah; for mushtarak, substitutive immediacy preserves haqiqah status for all meanings.

Non-Regularity as an Indicator of Metaphorical Usage
Explains the indicator of non-regularity for majaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the meaning of 'adam al-ittarad, apply it to identify metaphorical usage from Quranic examples, and evaluate objections including the non-reversibility of the indicator.

Core Idea
If a word used for a meaning is not extended to all similar cases, it is likely metaphorical.

In Hanafi usul, identifying whether a word is used literally (haqiqah) or metaphorically (majaz) is crucial for correct interpretation of revelation. One of the key indicators (amarat) is the lack of regularity ('adam al-ittarad). This lesson unpacks this indicator, its logical basis, and the powerful example of "wa s'ali al-qarya" from the Quran, while also addressing common objections.

Guided Path
1
Define ittarad and its opposite
Understand what 'ittarad' (regularity) means: a word is used for a meaning due to an aspect, and it is extended to all other instances where that aspect exists. Its absence is 'adam al-ittarad.
Ittarad is not about the word's original meaning but about consistent extension based on a shared aspect.
2
Understand the indicator
If the word is not extended to some similar cases, the usage is likely metaphorical (majaz), because literal usage follows the placement (wad') uniformly.
This indicator is probabilistic (zanni), not certain.
3
Analyze the example
Quran 12:82 says 'wa s'ali al-qarya' (ask the village) – 'village' is used metaphorically for its people. The same construction is not used for other objects like 'rug' (for rug seller), showing non-regularity.
The relationship (place for inhabitants) is the motivating aspect.
4
Address objections on arbitrariness
Objection: prevention from extension can happen in literal terms too. Hanafi response: In metaphor, transition from original meaning is based on a relation with varying ease; prevention of distant extensions is not arbitrary. In literal meaning, transition is by uniform placement, so restriction would be arbitrary.
Thus, non-regularity is a stronger indicator for majaz.
5
Note the limitation: regularity does not prove haqiqah
The indicator is not reversible. Example: 'sakhi' (generous) is regularly applied to generous humans but not to Allah, yet the usage for humans is literal. This is due to acquiredness (malaka) or legal restriction, not metaphorical usage.
Regularity is not a sign of haqiqah; majaz can also be regular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها عدم اطراده يعني إذا استعمل لفظ في معنى لأجل وجود معنى فيه، ولا يطرد استعماله في غيره مع وجود ذلك المعنى الموجب فيه فيدل على أن الاستعمال مجازي فيه نحو: ﴿وَاسْأَلِ الْقَرْيَةَ﴾ [يوسف: 82] دون واسأل البساط مع اشتراكهما في وجه الاستعمال. أقول : المنع ممنوع نعم لم يسمع اسأل البساط وعدم السماع لا يدل على عدم الجواز، كيف وقد تقدم أنه لا يشترط سماع الجزئيات، وهذا إن تم فمناقشة في المثال فافهم. ولو سلم المنع فلا يختص هذا المنع بالمجاز إذ المنع في البعض دون البعض مع الاشتراك في وجه الاستعمال من العلاقة تحكم والتحكم غير مختص بالمجاز فيجوز أن يمنعوا استعمال لفظ في بعض أفراد الموضوع له دون البعض نحو: ﴿وَاسْأَلِ الْقَرْيَةَ﴾ [يوسف: 82] بأنها لا تسأل بناء على أنه مجاز في الإسناد فليس مما نحن فيه، وهذا أيضاً مناقشة في المثال ولا تنعكس هذه الأمارة حتى يكون الاطراد أمارة الحقيقة فإن المجاز قد يطرد فلا يكون أمارة الحقيقة. وأورد عليه السخي فإنه حقيقة فيمن قام به السخاوة ولا يطرد إذ لا يطلق على الله تعالى مع أنه الجواد المطلق الجواب أنه ملكة بالاستقراء والملكة أمر كسبي لا يتحقق فيه سبحانه، ولا يطلق لعدم وجود جهة الإطلاق فيه، وقد يجاب بأنه يجوز الإطلاق لغة وإنما لا يجوز شرعاً، لأن الأسماء توقيفية، ولا توقيف فيه أو لأنه موهم للمنقصة، وحينئذ لا يرد العلامة المرادفة للعلام أيضاً، هذا لا يقال عدم الاطراد إنما يعلم بسببه لأنه ممكن غير محسوس والعلم به إنما يعلم من جهة العلم بالسبب لا يظهر لهذا وجه ظاهر والظاهر أنه من قلم الناسخ، والصحيح إنما يعلم من جهة سببه كما بين في المنطق، فحينئذ عدم الاطراد إنما يعلم من جهة سببه، ثم عدم الاطراد عدم ولا يكون سببه إلا عدم عدم المانع المستلزم لوجود المانع أو عدم المقتضى وليس السبب وجود المانع إذ لا منع ههنا فإن الكلام فيما لا نص فيه فتعين عدم المقتضى للسببية، والمقتضى للاطراد الوضع فعدم الاطراد لعدم الوضع فيعلم به وقد جعلتم عدم الوضع معلوماً بعدم الاطراد فدار وعدم القول لأن توقف العلم بذي السبب على العلم بسببه إنما هو في اليقين الكلي الدائم لا في اليقين الجزئي الغير الدائم ولا الظنون ومباحث اللغة مظنونة ثم لنا في توقف العلم بذي السبب على العلم بسببه وإن كان يقيناً كلياً كلام استوفيناه في شرح السلم
And among them (the indicators) is its non-regularity, meaning if a word is used for a meaning due to the existence of a meaning in it, but its use does not extend to others with that same meaning present, it indicates that the usage is metaphorical in it. Example: 'And ask the village' (Quran 12:82) but not 'ask the rug', despite both sharing the aspect of usage. I say: the prevention is prevented; indeed it was not heard 'ask the rug', and lack of hearing does not indicate impermissibility. Moreover, it has preceded that hearing of particulars is not required. If this is accepted, it is a discussion about the example, understand. And even if prevention is granted, this prevention is not specific to metaphor, because prevention in some but not others despite sharing the aspect of usage from the relationship is arbitrary, and the arbitrary is not specific to metaphor. So it is permissible that they prevent the use of a word for some individuals of the subject matter but not others except arbitrarily, which is not a proof. The answer is that in metaphor, difference in transition is permissible despite sharing the relationship, so what is more distant they prevent without arbitrariness, unlike literal meaning where transition is due to placement and does not differ among individuals. Rather, it is known that the example 'and ask the village' is interpreted as metaphorical in attribution, so it is not from our subject. This too is a discussion about the example, and this indicator is not reversed such that regularity becomes an indicator of literal meaning, for metaphor may be regular, so it cannot be an indicator of literal meaning. And it was objected regarding 'generous' (al-sakhi), which is literal for the one in whom generosity exists, but it does not extend since it is not applied to God Almighty despite Him being the absolute giver. Answer: It is a acquired trait (malaka) by induction, and a acquired trait is something acquired not realized in Him, so it is not applied due to the absence of the aspect of application. Or it may be answered that application is linguistically permissible but not permissible legally because names are tawqifiyya, and there is no such specification for it, or because it implies deficiency. Then this does not affect the synonym... Also, it cannot be said that non-regularity is known only by its cause, because it is a possible non-sensible thing, and knowledge of it is only from the knowledge of the cause. This has no apparent face, and it appears to be from the scribe's pen. The correct is that it is known by its cause as explained in logic. Therefore, non-regularity is known by its cause. Then non-regularity is a non-existence, and its cause can only be either the absence of the non-preventer entailing the existence of a preventer, or the absence of the requisite. And the cause is not the existence of a preventer, for there is no prevention here, as the discussion is about what has no text, so the absence of the requisite for causality is determined. The requisite for regularity is the placement (wad'), so non-regularity is due to the absence of placement. Thus it is known by it. And you have made the absence of placement known by non-regularity, so it is circular. The claim of circularity is refuted because the dependency of knowledge of the effect on knowledge of its cause is only in absolute certain knowledge, not in particular non-permanent certainty nor in probabilities, and investigations of language are probable. Moreover, we have a discourse on the dependency of knowledge of the effect on knowledge of its cause even if it is absolute certain knowledge, which we covered in our commentary on al-Sullam.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage discusses the amara of 'adam al-ittarad (non-regularity). If a word is used for a meaning because of some aspect, but it is not used for other words that have the same aspect, then the usage is likely metaphorical. For example, the Quranic 'ask the village' is used to mean 'ask the people of the village', but we do not say 'ask the rug' for 'ask the rug seller', even though both have a similar relationship (place for inhabitants). The author responds to objections: the counterexample of 'generous' (sakhi) which is not applied to God even though He is generous, but this is because generosity is a acquired trait not attributable to God, or because of legal restrictions on naming. The passage also addresses the epistemological issue: how do we know non-regularity? It is known by its cause, which is the absence of placement (wad'). Circularity objection is refuted because linguistic knowledge is probabilistic, not certain. This amara is a sign of majaz but not the reverse: regularity does not prove haqiqah because majaz can also be regular.

Key Terms
الاطراد (al-ittarad): Regularity or consistency in the application of a word to all cases where the motivating aspect exists.
المجاز (al-majaz): Metaphorical usage of a word, where it is used for a meaning not originally placed for it due to a relation.
الملكة (al-malaka): An acquired trait or habit that is not inherent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the example 'واسأل القرية' considered metaphorical according to the amara of non-regularity?
A) Because 'village' is literally a place, not a people, and the same construction is not used for 'ask the rug'.
B) Because 'ask' cannot be used for inanimate objects.
C) Because the word 'قرية' has multiple meanings.
D) Because the Qur'an uses it only once.

Key Takeaway
Lack of ittarad (non-regularity) is a probable indicator that a word is used metaphorically, but ittarad itself does not prove literal meaning.

The Indicator of Plural on a Different Pattern for Majaz
Explains the indicator of plural on a different pattern for majaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a word's plural pattern indicates metaphorical meaning and apply this indicator in Hanafi usul to avoid positing a common word.

Core Idea
Difference in plural forms signals metaphorical meaning; uniformity does not signal literalness.

In Hanafi usul, distinguishing haqiqa from majaz is crucial for proper textual interpretation. One subtle but powerful indicator is when a single word has multiple plural forms on different patterns. This lesson unpacks how this sign works, why it is preferred over positing a common word (ishtirāk), and why the opposite—uniformity of plural—does not prove literalness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word and its plurals
When encountering a word used in a text, note if it has more than one plural form. For instance, 'amr' has plurals 'awāmir' and 'umūr'.
The plural forms must be morphologically distinct.
2
Determine if the plurals are on different patterns
If the plurals follow different morphological patterns (e.g., one is a sound plural, the other a broken plural), this is a strong sign that the word is not univocal.
This indicates the word applies to different realities.
3
Conclude the word is not univocal (mutawāti')
Since the same word cannot be truly univocal if its plurals are tied to distinct meanings, the word must have multiple meanings.
A univocal term would have a single plural or plurals on the same pattern.
4
Prefer metaphorical interpretation over common word
Hanafi usul avoids positing a common word (mushtarak) if possible. Therefore, one meaning is considered literal (haqiqa) and the other metaphorical (majaz), based on context and other indicators.
This is a probabilistic indicator, not a definitive proof.
5
Note the non-reversibility of the indicator
If a word has only one plural form (uniformity), that does not prove it is literal. The indicator works only when plurals diverge.
Reversing the sign would lead to false conclusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أي من الأمارات جمعه على خلاف جمع الحقيقة كأمور فعلم أنه ليس متواطئاً فتعدد المعنى باعتبار أحدهما وباعتبار الآخر جمع جمع آخر فيحمل على المجاز دفعاً للاشتراك فإذا قرر هكذا فما أورد في التحرير على التقرير المشهور بأن اختلاف الجمع موجب للمجازية دفعاً للاشتراك أنه لا أثر لاختلاف الجمع فإنه يتم الكلام بدونه ساقط لأن باختلاف الجمع يعلم تعدد المعاني وسيأتي الكلام فيه في بحث الأمر ويتضح منه أن اختلاف الجمع علامة الحقيقة ويستوفي الكلام هناك إن شاء الله تعالى ولا ينعكس فإن اتحاد الجمع ليس أمارة الحقيقة
And among them (the indicators) is its plural being contrary to the plural of literal meaning, like 'umūr' (matters). So it is known that it is not univocal (mutawāti'), so meaning is multiple, and with one meaning it takes one plural and with another meaning it takes another plural. So it is interpreted as metaphorical to avoid common word (ishtirāk). Thus when it is established like this, what was objected in al-Taḥrīr against the well-known formulation that difference in plural necessitates metaphoricalness to avoid common word, that difference in plural has no effect (since the intended meaning is understood without it) is invalid, because by difference in plural multiplicity of meanings is known. And the discussion will come in the topic of 'amr, and it will be clear there that difference in plural is a sign of literal meaning, and the discussion will be covered there, God willing. And it is not reversed, because unity of plural is not a sign of literal meaning.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage presents the amara that if a word takes different plural forms for different meanings, then when one plural is used, it indicates that the meaning corresponding to that plural is intended, and the other meaning with a different plural is likely metaphorical. For example, the word 'amr' (command/affair) has plural 'awāmir' for commands and 'umūr' for affairs. The difference in plural shows the word is not univocal (it has multiple meanings). To avoid having a common word (mushtarak), one of the usages is considered metaphorical. The author refutes an objection from al-Taḥrīr that the difference in plural is irrelevant, arguing that it does indicate multiplicity. He notes that this will be clarified later in the discussion of 'amr. Importantly, the amara is not reversible: uniformity of plural does not prove literalness.

Key Terms
الجمع على خلاف جمع الحقيقة (al-jam' 'alā khilāf jam' al-ḥaqīqah): The phenomenon where a word has multiple plural forms that correspond to different meanings, suggesting that one meaning is metaphorical.
المتواطئ (al-mutawāti'): A univocal term that applies equally to all its individuals without priority.
الاشتراك (al-ishtirāk): A word with multiple independent meanings (common word).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does difference in plural forms indicate?
A) That the word is univocal.
B) That the word has multiple meanings and one usage may be metaphorical.
C) That both meanings are literal.
D) That the word is common (mushtarak).

Key Takeaway
Difference in plural forms is a directional indicator: it suggests metaphorical usage for one meaning, but uniformity of plural does not prove literalness.

The Indicator of Requirement of Qualification (Iltizām al-Taqyīd) for Majaz
Explains the amara of iltizām al-taqyīd and defends it against the idāfa objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the amara of iltizām al-taqyīd to identify metaphorical usage in Arabic legal texts and evaluate the objection based on idāfa.

Core Idea
If a word needs qualification to convey a meaning, it indicates metaphorical usage.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, distinguishing literal (haqīqah) from metaphorical (majāz) usage is critical for correct derivation of rulings. One key indicator is the necessity of adding a qualifying expression (taqyīd) to convey a specific meaning. This lesson unpacks that indicator, examines a classic objection involving construct phrases (idāfa), and clarifies the conditions under which the indicator is valid.

Guided Path
1
State the amara
Begin by articulating the principle: if a word is known to be used without qualification in one sense, but requires a qualifying expression to convey another sense, then that second usage is likely metaphorical.
The qualification acts as a qarīna (contextual clue).
2
Analyze the example
Consider the phrase 'ظلمة الكفر' (darkness of disbelief). 'ظلمة' literally means physical darkness. Here it is qualified by 'الكفر' to mean spiritual darkness, indicating metaphorical usage.
The same applies to 'نور الإيمان' (light of faith).
3
Understand the idāfa objection
The objection: in any construct phrase (idāfa), the second term qualifies the first. So every idāfa would be an instance of this amara, making it useless.
The objector argues that the amara is refuted by the necessary implication of idāfa.
4
Distinguish the Hanafi response
The response: the amara applies only when the word has a known unrestricted (without qualification) literal usage, and then the qualification is added specifically to shift meaning. In idāfa, the qualification is inherent and not added to shift from a known unrestricted use.
Thus, not every idāfa qualifies; only those where the word has a prior unrestricted usage.
5
Apply to a new example
Take the word 'يد' (hand). Without qualification it means physical hand. In 'يد الله' (hand of Allah), it is qualified; since physical hand cannot be attributed to Allah, the qualification indicates metaphorical usage.
If there is no prior unrestricted usage known, the amara may not apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أي من الأمارات التزام التقييد عند استعماله في هذا المعنى كظلمة الكفر فإن استعماله في العقائد الباطلة لا يصح بدون التقييد ونور الإيمان إذ مع التقييد يستعمل في العقائد الحقة أقول هذا منقوض بلازم الإضافة فإن استعماله في معناه لا يجوز إلا بالإضافة وهي تقييد فافهم وفيه أن المراد التزام التقييد لإفادة هذا المعنى الذي لولاه لفهم معنى آخر فكأنه قرينة الدلالة عليه، وهو لا يوجد في لازم الإضافة قطعاً، وقال في الحاشية إن التقييد في مورد معين بعد معرفة استعماله في مورد آخر بدون التقييد أمارة المجاز ولازم الإضافة ليس فيه هذا النحو من التقييد فتأمل فيه
And among them is the requirement of qualification (taqyīd) when using it in this meaning, like 'the darkness of disbelief' (ẓulmat al-kufr). For its use in false beliefs is not valid without qualification, and 'the light of faith' (nūr al-īmān), since with qualification it is used in true beliefs. I say: this is refuted by the necessary implication of idāfa (construct phrase), because its use in its meaning is not permissible except with idāfa, which is a qualification. Understand. And in it, the meaning is that the requirement of qualification is to convey this meaning, without which another meaning would be understood, as if it is a context (qarīna) indicating it. And this is absolutely not present in the necessary implication of idāfa. And the commentator said in the Ḥāshiya: Qualification in a specific instance, after knowing its use in another instance without qualification, is an indicator of majāz. And the necessary implication of idāfa does not have this type of qualification. So ponder it.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage discusses the amara that if a word requires an additional qualification (taqyīd) to convey a specific meaning, then that meaning is likely metaphorical. For example, 'darkness' normally means physical darkness, but in 'darkness of disbelief', it is qualified to mean spiritual darkness, indicating metaphorical usage. The author addresses an objection: in construct phrases (idāfa), qualification is inherent, but the response is that the amara applies to cases where the word is known to be used without qualification in one sense, but then requires qualification for another sense. This is different from the inherent qualification in idāfa. The Ḥāshiya clarifies that the qualification serves as a context (qarīna) that points to the intended meaning.

Key Terms
التزام التقييد (iltizām al-taqyīd): The necessity of adding a qualifying description to a word to convey a particular meaning, considered an indicator of metaphor.
الإضافة (al-iḍāfa): Construct phrase (genitive construction) where the second term qualifies the first inherently.
القرينة (al-qarīna): A contextual clue that indicates the intended meaning, especially in cases of ambiguity or metaphor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the phrase 'ظلمة الكفر' considered an example of metaphorical usage according to the amara of taqyīd?
A) Because 'ظلمة' literally means physical darkness and requires 'الكفر' to indicate metaphorical meaning.
B) Because 'ظلمة' is a common word.
C) Because it is a construct phrase.
D) Because it is used in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Requirement of qualification is a valid indicator of metaphor only when the word has a known unrestricted literal usage that is shifted by the qualifier.

The Indicator of Dependence: Understanding Mushākala in Hanafi Usul
Analyzes the amara of dependence for majaz mushākala

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the indicator of dependence on another usage (mushākala) and evaluate the proposed relations for metaphorical usage in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
A metaphor is valid only if its claimed analogy is known beforehand; mushākala requires the literal sense to precede.

In this lesson, we examine a specific indicator of metaphorical usage: dependence on another word. The Hanafi usul tradition distinguishes mushākala as a type of majaz where the metaphorical sense cannot stand alone. We will unpack the key objections and the preferred Hanafi resolution through the concept of claimed analogy (tashbīh iddi'ā'ī).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Indicator
Recognize that the indicator is the dependence of a word's usage in a certain sense on the prior usage of another word. For example, in 'wa makarū wa makara Allāh', the term 'makar' for Allah cannot be used independently.
This indicator suggests that the usage is metaphorical (majāz) rather than literal.
2
Examine the Quranic Example
Analyze the verse: ﴿وَمَكَرُوا وَمَكَرَ اللهُ﴾. The term 'makar' (plot) for Allah is not permissible initially because it would attribute imperfection. Its validity depends on the prior mention of human plotting.
The metaphorical meaning is only understood through the correlation (mushākala) with the literal usage.
3
Critique Proposed Relations
Evaluate the three proposed relations: (1) association in mention (muṣāḥaba fī al-dhikr) – rejected because it is too broad; (2) proximity in imagination – rejected as insufficient; (3) claimed analogy (tashbīh iddi'ā'ī) – accepted as the proper relation.
The author shows that only claimed analogy fulfills the requirement that the relation must be known prior to usage.
4
Apply to the Poetic Example
Consider the line: 'قالوا اقترح شيئاً نجد لك طبخه قلت اطبخوا لي جبة وقميصا'. Here 'cook' is used metaphorically for 'tailor'. The claimed analogy is based on the intense need for a cloak, likening it to food.
This analogy, though not common, becomes valid once the literal meaning is mentioned first.
5
Derive the Hanafi Principle
The indicator of dependence shows that a word is used metaphorically. The metaphorical usage is only valid if the analogy (tashbīh) is recognized or can be claimed. Without prior recognition, the metaphor is impermissible.
This principle helps distinguish valid majāz from invalid usages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أي من الأمارات توقف إطلاقه على إطلاق آخر نحو: ﴿وَمَكَرُوا وَمَكَرَ اللهُ﴾ [آل عمران: 54] فإنه لا يصح مكر الله ابتداء فالمشاكلة مجاز وقد يقال تحقق العلاقة في المشاكلة بما هي مشاكلة مشكل إذ أين الطبخ من الخياطة فإنه لا علاقة ههنا أصلاً مع أنه أطلق عليه في قوله: قالوا اقترح شيئاً نجد لك طبخه قلت اطبخوا لي جبة وقميصا فقيل لدفعه كأنهم جعلوا المصاحبة في الذكر علاقة وهذا بعيد كل البعد فإن المصاحبة في الذكر تكمن في كل لفظين فيجوز استعمال أحدهما في معنى الآخر، واعترض أيضاً بأن هذه المصاحبة غير معدودة في تعداد أنواع العلاقات أجاب المصنف بأنها نحو من المجاورة، واعترض أيضاً بأنها بعد الاستعمال والعلاقة يجب تحققها قبله قال في الحاشية المصاحبة المتصورة علاقة وهي متقدمة ولا يخلو عن تكلف وقيل ليس العلاقة المصاحبة في الذكر بل المجاورة في الخيال وفيه أيضاً بعد فإن المجاورة الاتفاقية غير كافية أقول ليس العلاقة المصاحبة في الذكر ولا المجاورة الخيالية بل التشبيه الادعائي فإنه لما اشتدت حاجته إلى الجبة شبهها بالطعام الذي به قوام بدن الإنسان وشبه خياطته بطبخه لكن لما لم يعرف هذا التشبيه من قبل لم يجز هذا المجاز ابتداء بل بعد ذكر الحقيقة ولهذا لا يجوز مكر الله
And among them is the dependence of its usage on another usage, like: 'And they plotted, and Allah plotted' (Quran 3:54). For 'plot of Allah' is not valid initially, so the correlation (mushākala) is metaphorical. And it may be said: the connection in correlation as such is problematic, for where is cooking from tailoring? There is no connection here at all, even though it was used in the verse: 'They said: propose something we can cook for you. I said: cook for me a cloak and a shirt.' So it was said to refute it: they made association in mention a relation, but this is far-fetched, for association in mention can occur for any two words, so it would permit using one in the meaning of another. And it was also objected that this association is not counted among the types of relations. The author answered that it is a kind of proximity (mujāwara). And it was also objected that it is after usage, while the relation must exist before usage. The commentator said in the Ḥāshiya: the conceived association is a relation and it is prior, though not free from difficulty. And it was said: the relation is not association in mention, but rather proximity in imagination. There is also difficulty in this, because accidental proximity is insufficient. I say: the relation is neither association in mention nor imaginary proximity, but rather a claimed analogy (tashbīh iddi'ā'ī). For when his need for a cloak became intense, he likened it to food that sustains the body, and likened its tailoring to cooking. But because this analogy was not known beforehand, this metaphor is not initially valid, only after mentioning the literal meaning. For this reason, 'plot of Allah' is not permissible.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage analyzes the amara of dependence on another usage, exemplified by the Quranic 'makarū wa makara Allāh' (they plotted, and Allah plotted). Since one cannot say 'makar Allāh' (Allah plotted) independently, it is considered a case of mushākala (correlation/metaphorical correspondence). The author critically examines various proposed relations: association in mention, proximity in imagination, and eventually adopts claimed analogy. For example, in a poetic verse where 'cook' is used metaphorically for 'tailor', the resemblance is based on the intense need. The key point is that such metaphors are only valid after the literal meaning is established; thus 'makar Allāh' is not permissible because the analogy is not apparent.

Key Terms
المشاكلة (al-mushākala): A metaphorical usage where two expressions are correlated in wording due to similarity in context, though one is not literal.
التشبيه الادعائي (al-tashbīh al-iddi'ā'ī): Claimed analogy, where something is likened to another due to a perceived attribute, even if not commonly known.
المصاحبة في الذكر (al-muṣāḥaba fī al-dhikr): Association in mention, where two words are frequently mentioned together.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is 'مكر الله' (Allah plotted) not permissible as a standalone metaphorical expression?
A) Because Allah cannot be described with the word 'مكر' under any circumstances.
B) Because the claimed analogy between human plotting and divine action is not known beforehand.
C) Because the Quranic verse is an exception that does not establish a general rule.
D) Because 'مكر' is a purely literal word.

Key Takeaway
The indicator of dependence for majaz (mushākala) requires that the relation be a claimed analogy (tashbīh iddi'ā'ī) that is recognizable, not mere association in mention or imaginary proximity.

8 min
The Three Indicators of Metaphorical Usage in Hanafi Usul
Enumeration of signs identifying metaphorical usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three linguistic indicators of metaphorical usage (amarat al-majaz) and apply them to classify Quranic and hadith expressions as haqiqah or majaz.

Core Idea
Three linguistic signs reveal metaphor: irregular plural, mandatory qualification, and dependence on another usage.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, distinguishing literal (haqiqah) from metaphorical (majaz) usage is crucial for correct legal interpretation. This passage from the section on linguistic principles enumerates three specific indicators (amarat) that signal a word is being used metaphorically. These indicators are based on linguistic patterns and serve to avoid interpretive errors such as unnecessary homonymy or forced literalism.

Guided Path
1
Understand the purpose of amarat al-majaz
Recognize that these indicators help us identify when a word is not used in its original literal sense, guiding correct interpretation of revelation.
This is a tool to avoid assuming literal meaning where it leads to incongruity.
2
Examine the first indicator: irregular plural (ikhtilaf al-jam‘)
When a word's plural form is different from the plural of its literal meaning, it indicates metaphorical usage. Example: 'umoor' (affairs) as plural of 'amr' meaning 'command'? Actually 'amr' has plural 'awamir' for commands, but 'umoor' is used for affairs, so it is majaz.
This avoids assuming the word is univocal (mushtarak) when it is actually used metaphorically.
3
Examine the second indicator: mandatory qualification (iltizam al-taqyid)
If a word requires a restrictive phrase to convey a specific meaning, that restriction signals metaphor. E.g., 'darkness of disbelief' – 'darkness' alone would be literal; the addition 'of disbelief' shows it is metaphorical.
The key is that the restriction is necessary for this meaning; without it, another meaning would be understood.
4
Examine the third indicator: dependence on another usage (mushakalah)
When a word's usage depends on another word being mentioned first, like 'And Allah plotted' (Quran 3:54) which only makes sense after 'And they plotted'. This is metaphorical through similarity in form (mushakalah).
The relationship (al-ilaqah) is debated: association in mention, imaginary contiguity, or allegorical similitude.
5
Apply the indicators to new examples
Practice using these three signs to classify expressions in Quranic verses or hadith as literal or metaphorical.
Remember that these are probabilistic (zanni) indicators, not certain proofs.

يعرف هذا التشبيه من قبل لم يجز) هذا المجاز (ابتداء ، بل بعد ذكر الحقيقة ولهذا لا يجوز مكر الله ،
The third article on linguistic principles. [Discussion on] absence of the effective cause (muqtada) and presence of the preventer (mani‘)... Then from among the indicators: its plural being contrary to the plural of the literal meaning, like 'umoor' (affairs), so it is known that it is not univocal, thus multiplicity of meaning... So it is interpreted as metaphorical to avoid homonymy... and it is not reversible, for uniformity of plural is not an indicator of literalness. And from among them: adherence to restriction when used in this meaning, like 'the darkness of disbelief'... And from among them: dependence of its usage on another usage, like 'And they plotted and Allah plotted' [Quran 3:54]... Then it was said to refute it that they made association in mention a relation, but this is far-fetched... He said in the supercommentary: the imagined association is a relation and it is prior... I say: the relation is neither association in mention nor imaginary contiguity, but rather an allegorical similitude.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage from the section on linguistic principles enumerates various indicators (amarat) that help distinguish metaphorical usage (majaz) from literal usage (haqiqah). The author first addresses a preliminary objection concerning the absence of the effective cause and the presence of a preventer, clarifying that the discussion pertains to cases without explicit text. He then lists three indicators: first, when the plural of a word differs from its literal plural, as in 'umoor' (the plural of 'amr' meaning 'affair' rather than 'command'), this signals metaphorical usage because it avoids homonymy. Second, when a word requires a restriction (taqyid) to convey a particular meaning, such as 'the darkness of disbelief' where 'darkness' alone would mean physical darkness, but with the addition 'of disbelief' it becomes metaphorical. Third, when a word's usage depends on another word, like 'And Allah plotted' which can only be understood alongside 'And they plotted' — this is metaphorical through mushakalah (similarity). The passage then engages in a detailed discussion about the nature of the relationship (al-'ilaqah) in such metaphors, rejecting mere association in mention or imaginary contiguity in favor of allegorical similitude (tashbih ida'i).

Key Terms
أمارات المجاز (amarat al-majaz): Linguistic signs that indicate a word is being used metaphorically rather than literally.
اختلاف الجمع (ikhtilaf al-jam‘): When a word has a plural form different from the plural of its literal meaning, indicating metaphorical usage as in 'umoor' (affairs) from 'amr'.
التزام التقييد (iltizam al-taqyid): The necessity of adding a qualifying phrase to convey a specific meaning, e.g., 'darkness of disbelief', which is an indicator of metaphorical usage.
المشاكلة (al-mushakalah): A type of metaphor based on similarity in verbal expression, as in 'And they plotted and Allah plotted'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT listed as an indicator (amara) of metaphorical usage?
A) Plural form differing from the literal plural
B) Mandatory qualification (taqyid) of the word
C) The word being used in a compound expression
D) Dependence of the word's usage on another word

Key Takeaway
Three probabilistic signs—irregular plural, mandatory qualification, and dependence on another usage—help the Hanafi usuli identify metaphorical usage in revealed texts.

Does Metaphor Necessitate Prior Literal Usage? The Case of 'al-Rahman'
Dispute on whether metaphorical usage requires prior literal usage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on whether majaz requires prior haqiqah and apply the proof from 'al-Rahman' to reject istilzam.

Core Idea
Metaphorical usage does not require prior literal meaning—'al-Rahman' has no literal meaning for God.

This lesson examines a fundamental dispute in usul al-fiqh: does metaphorical usage (majaz) necessitate that the word was previously used in its literal sense (haqiqah)? The Hanafi school argues it does not. We will focus on the strongest proof: the word 'al-Rahman', which is used exclusively for God yet has no possible literal meaning (tenderness of heart) applicable to Him. Understanding this dispute is crucial for proper istinbat (derivation of rulings) from the Quran and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
State the dispute
Identify the two positions: those who require prior haqiqah and those who reject it. The Hanafi school holds the negation.
2
Examine the primary Hanafi proof: 'al-Rahman'
Consider the two arguments: (1) 'al-Rahman' cannot have its literal meaning (tender-hearted) applied to God; (2) it is only used for a specific individual (God), not for the general class. This shows no prior literal usage exists.
3
Address the counterargument about Musaylimah
Some claimed that the followers of Musaylimah used 'Rahman' literally for him. The text refutes this as contrary to proper Arabic and a distortion.
4
Evaluate the insufficiency of the two standard arguments
The author notes that the argument that mercy means tenderness of heart is not proven; mercy could mean beneficence. However, the key is that 'al-Rahman' is not applied to other than God due to the absence of that complete meaning.
5
Conclude with the Hanafi tarjih
The correct view (al-ashah) is negation: majaz does not require prior haqiqah. This is supported by linguistic evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ولو مرة، فقيل : يستلزم والأصح النفي) فلا يستلزم (لنا الرحمن فإنه مجاز لغة أو عرفاً ولا حقيقة) قد قرروه بوجهين : الأول : لا يطلق إلا على الله تعالى ولا يتحقق معناه الحقيقي فإنه ذو الرحمة، والرحمة رقة القلب، ولا قلب له سبحانه . والثاني : أنه لا يطلق إلا على فرد خاص من ذي الرحمة وهو الله سبحانه، ولم يطلق على المطلق أصلاً، فإن قلت : قد أطلق أصحاب مسيلمة الكذاب عليه لفظ الرحمن، وقد اشتهر حتى قال أبو لم يقم دليل على أن الرحمة رقة القلب بل يجوز أن تكون موضوعة بإزاء التفضل والإحسان، نعم في الإنسان لا يكون هذا التفضل إلا برقة القلب، وانعطافه وعدم إطلاقه على غيره تعالى لعدم وجود معناه فإنه اعتبر مبالغة كاملة، فإنه ذو تفضل عظيم، وهذا التعظيم باعتبار سعة المرحوم عليه وشموله لكل أحد، وباعتبار المرحوم به من النعماء من حيث الكثرة والشدة، وهذا لا يوجد في غيره تعالى قطعاً، وبعد التنزل إطلاق العام على فرد منه ليس بمجاز تأمل في هذا التنزل (و) لنا (عسى) ونعم لأنهما صيغتان وضعتا للإخبار ولم يستعملا فيه قط، بل في الإنشاء فقط، هذا أيضاً مجرد دعوى لم يقم عليه دليل (و) لنا المبهمات على رأي) وهو رأي من يجعلها موضوعة لمفهومات كلية لتستعمل في الإفراد ولا يخفى أن رأي واحد لا يصلح حجة لا سيما رأي شهدت الحجة العدلة على بطلانه ( وأما الاستدلال على المطلوب بالمركبات من :نحو: قامت الحرب على ساقٍ وشابت لمة الليل فإنها مجازات ولم تستعمل هذه التراكيب في معانيها الحقيقية قط (فخروج عن النزاع فإنه في المفردات وههنا مجاز في الهيئة التركيبية، ولا تجوز في شاب وفي اللمة وهما مستعملان في معناهما الحقيقي أيضاً وما قيل عليه إنه مشترك الالزام) علينا وعليهم لانتفاء معنى محقق موضوع بإزائه اللفظ ، ولا بد منه إنما النزاع في كونه مستعملاً فيه أولا (فوهم) فاسد ( لأن الواجب) للمجاز (معلومية المعنى وإن كان موهوماً) غير متحقق في نفس الأمر (وهي) أي المعلومية (متحققة أما تحققه أي المعنى (في الواقع فليس بواجب كالكواذب) ومن ههنا يخرج الجواب بوجه آخر عن الدليل، فإنه يجوز أن يكون له حقيقة يستعمله الكذاب والهزال والناقل فافهم (وما ) قيل (في «التحرير» إنه مشترك) الزاماً لاستلزامه وضعاً، والاتفاق على أن المركب لم يوضع شخصياً، والكلام فيه فحينئذ آل الكلام إلى أن المجاز لا بد له من
المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية موضوع له بالوضع الشخصي، وهل يجب استعماله فيه أم لا؟ ففيه كلام) فإنه لا خصوصية للوضع الشخصي ألا ترى أنهم استدلوا بالرحمن وعسى مع أنهما موضوعان بالوضع النوعي بل يخرج عن البحث المشتقات والأفعال الملزمون قالوا لو لم يستلزم) المجاز الحقيقة (انتفت فائدة الوضع وهي إفادة المعنى التركيبي حين الاستعمال وإذ لا استعمال فلا إفادة (قلنا: الملازمة ممنوعة فإن انتفاء فائدة خاصة لا يوجب انتفاءها مطلقا فإن صحة التجوز) فيه من الفوائد) ولم تنتف (قيل بطلان التالي ممنوع إذ لا استحالة في انتفاء الفائدة (أقول : إذا كان الواضع هو الله تعالى كما هو الظاهر (فالبطلان أي بطلان انتفاء الفائدة (ظاهر).
After agreement that the word after coinage and before usage is neither literal nor metaphorical: they are both categories of words used correctly. There is disagreement whether metaphor necessitates literal meaning and whether it is required that the word be used in its literal sense at least once. Some say it does necessitate, but the correct view is negation—it does not necessitate. Our proof: 'al-Rahman' for it is metaphorical linguistically or customarily and has no literal meaning. They presented it in two ways: First: it is only applied to God and its literal meaning is not realized, for it means 'possessor of mercy', and mercy is tenderness of heart, and He has no heart. Second: it is only applied to a specific individual of 'possessor of mercy', namely God, and was never applied to the absolute. If you say: The followers of Musaylimah the liar applied the word 'al-Rahman' to him, it became well-known until Abu Jahl said upon hearing 'al-Rahman' from the Prophet: 'We do not know al-Rahman except the Rahman of Yamama.' He replied: The Rahman of Yamama is rejected because it is not in accordance with the language, rather it is from their obstinacy and ignorance. Then the two ways are insufficient: no proof has been established that mercy is tenderness of heart; it could be coined for favor and beneficence. Indeed in humans this favor does not occur except with tenderness of heart. And its not being applied to other than Him is due to the absence of its meaning, for it considers complete hyperbole, as He is possessor of great favor. This glorification is in terms of the vastness of the objects of mercy encompassing everyone, and the favors in terms of abundance and intensity, which absolutely does not exist in other than Him. After concession: applying a general term to a specific individual is not metaphor; ponder this concession. And our proof: 'asa' and 'ni'ma' because they are formulas coined for informing but were never used in that, only in construction—this is also a mere claim with no proof. And our proof: the ambiguous words—according to one opinion—that they are coined for universal concepts to be used for individuals, and it is obvious that one opinion is not a proof, especially an opinion refuted by clear proof. As for proving the claim by compounds like 'the war stood on a leg' and 'the forelock of night turned white'—they are metaphors and these structures were never used in their literal meanings—this is outside the dispute because the dispute is about individual words, and here the metaphor is in the compositional structure, not in 'shaba' and 'lamma' which are used in their literal meanings. What was said that it is a shared obligation is an error: because what is required for metaphor is the knowledge of the meaning even if it is imaginary and not real in itself. That knowledge is realized, but its actual realization is not required, as in false statements. From this another answer to the proof emerges: it is possible that it has a literal meaning used by the liar and the weak narrator, so understand. And what was said in 'al-Tahrir' that it is shared—obligingly due to its necessitating coinage, and the agreement that the compound was not individually coined—the discussion about it then led to the point that metaphor must have... The third section on linguistic principles: it is coined by individual coinage, and is using it in that required or not? There is discussion. For there is no specificity of individual coinage; do you not see that they used 'al-Rahman' and 'asa' as proofs even though they are coined by generic coinage? Rather, derivatives and verbs exit from the discussion. The compelers said: if metaphor does not necessitate literal meaning, the benefit of coinage—which is conveying compositional meaning at the time of usage—would be lost, and with no usage, no benefit. We say: the conjunction is denied, for the negation of a specific benefit does not necessitate its absolute negation, because the validity of metaphorical usage is among the benefits and is not negated. It was said: the negation of the consequent is denied, for there is no impossibility in the nonexistence of benefit. I say: if the coinage is from God as is apparent, then the negation of benefit is apparent.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a dispute in usul al-fiqh regarding whether metaphorical usage (majaz) necessarily implies that the word was previously used in its literal sense (haqiqah). The correct Hanafi position, as stated, is that it does not necessitate such prior literal usage. The proof is given using the word 'al-Rahman' which is metaphorically applied to God but has no literal human meaning (tenderness of heart). The author critiques the two standard arguments, showing they are insufficient, and provides his own reasoning. He also addresses counterarguments from opponents such as the usage by Musaylimah's followers and the claim that compounds like 'the war stood on a leg' prove prior literal usage. He clarifies that the dispute concerns individual words, not compositional structures. Finally, he discusses the issue of individual coinage (al-wad' al-shakhsi) and refutes the objection that without prior literal usage, the benefit of coinage is lost. Throughout, the Hanafi school's preference for the negation of istilzam is upheld.

Key Terms
مجاز (majaz): A word used in other than its original coined meaning with a valid contextual connection
حقيقة (haqiqah): A word used in its original coined meaning
استلزام (istilzam): Necessitation; here, whether majaz necessitates a prior haqiqah

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the correct Hanafi position regarding whether majaz necessitates a prior haqiqah?
A) Majaz always requires a prior haqiqah
B) Majaz does not require a prior haqiqah
C) Majaz requires haqiqah if the word is from the Quran
D) Only compound majaz requires haqiqah

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that metaphorical usage does not require a prior literal meaning; words like 'al-Rahman' are valid metaphors without evidence of prior literal use.
